'Pursuing Literature' it' Australia.
Henry Lawson unburdens his soul—
In the first fifteen years of my life I saw the
last of the Roaring Days on Gulgong goldfield
N.S.W. I remember the rush as a boy might his
first and only pantomime. ‘‘ On our selection ”
I tailed cows amongst the deserted shafts in the
gullies of a dreary old field that was abandoned
ere Gulgong “broke out.” I grubbed, ring-
barked, and ploughed in the scratchy sort of way
common to many “native-born” selectors round
there; helped fight pleuro and drought; and
worked on building contracts with “Dad,-” who
was a carpenter. Saw selectors slaving their lives
away m dusty holes amongst the barren ridges :
saw one or two carried home, in the end, on a sheet
of bark; the old men worked till they died.. Saw
how the gaunt selectors’ wives lived and toiled.
Saw elder sons stoop-shouldered old men at 30.
Noted, in dusty patches in the scrubs, the pile of
chimney-stones, a blue-gum slab or two, and the
remains of the fence —the ultimate result of 10
years, Id years, and 20 years' jiard, hopeless graft
by strongmen Who died like broken-down bullocks'
further out. And all the years miles and miles of
rich black soil flats and chocolate slopes lay idle
because of. old-time grants, or because the country
carried sheep—for the sake of an extra bale of
wool and an unknown absentee. I watched old
fossickers and farmers reading “Progress and
Poverty earnestly and atg.uing oyer it Sunday
afternoons. And 1 wished that I could w»ite
The droughts of the early Eighties, coming with
the pleuro,. the rabbits, crop and vine Elis cases and
other troubles, burst a lot of us round there. Home
old selectors did pick-and-shovel work in the city
or drove drays, while their wives took in washing!
I. worked for sub-contractors in coach-factories
painting; tramped the cities in search of work •
saw the haggard little group in front of tlm board
outside the Herald office at 4 o’clock in the
morning, striking matches to run down the
Wanted coin nine ; saw the slums and the poor
—and wished .that I could write, or paint
I heard Tommy Walker, and Collins, and the
rest of em, a: id, of course, a host of Yankee free-
thought and socialistic lecturers. I wore the green
m fancy,, gathered at the rising of the moon
charged for the fair land of Poland, and dreamed
of dying on the barricades to the roar of the
Marseillaise for the Young Australian Repub-
lic. then came the unexpected and inexplicable
outburst of. popular feeling (or madness)—called
tu6n til© KepuWicaii riots—in J B7, when tlio Sydney
crowd carried a disloyal amendment on the
Queens Jubilee, and cheered, at the Town Hall
for an “Australian Republic.” And I had to
write then or burst. I’hk Bulletin saved me
from bursting.
Youth : The first four fines are the best. Try again.”
Answer s to Correspondents, Bullbtin, June 18, 18,87.
The first four lines were printed. I haven’t felt
so excited over a thing since. The fire blazed
too fiercely to last; but it burned for ten years.
“ H.L. : Will publish your “ Song of the Republic.”
I was up at daylight every publishing morning
and down to the earliest news-agent’s, but “ The
Song of the Republic ” was held over for a special
occasion—Eight Hours’ Day (Oct., 1887). Demo-
cracy and Unionism were alive those times, and
Eight Hours’ Day was called “ The Carnival of
Labour. ”
I was a coach-painter’s improver at ss. per day,
with regular work, and only needed to practise
“lining,” or tracing, to be master of the trade. I
helped write, machine, and publish a flyblister
called The Republican. I wrote some verses called
“The Song of the Outcasts,” or “The Army of
the Rear ” ; also “ Golden Gully, ” “ The Wreck of
the Derry Castle,” and one or two others (re-
jected). I took the parcel down to the old Bulle-
tin office, in Pitt-street, after dusk, intending to
slip it surreptitiously into the letter-box ; but the
charwoman, broom in hand, opened the door
suddenly, and gave me a start. I thrust the
screed into her hands and made off.
In Dec., ’B7, I was coach-painting at Windsor,
Melb., for Gs. a day, when J got my first Xmas
Bulletin. I tore it open, tremblingly ; glanced
through it, to make sure I was there; and
hid it in a hearse I was “rubbing-down”
for the boss was a fierce Wesleyan. I
rubbed hard with the pumice-stone till my heart
didn’t thump so much, and I felt cabner. I stole
glances, behind the hearse, at “Golden Gully”
and ‘ ‘ The Wreck of the Derry Castle, ” and the
kindly editorial note to the effect that I was a
mere lad (aged 19), earning a living, under diffi-
culties, at house : painting, and that my education
was, as yet unfinished (N.B. —I couldn’t spell), and
that my talent spoke for itself in the following
poem. I was in print, and in the Xmas number of
a journal I had worshipped, and devoured every
inch ofy for years. I felt strong and proud enough
to clean pigstyes, if need be, for a living for the
rest of my natural life-provided The Bulletin
went on publishing the poetry. Varnish on old
hearses is hard as Hint; but I made a good job of
that one, and a quick job—for I “rubbed down ”
on air if I didn’t walk on it. It was the shortest
eight hours’ graft I ever did.
. When house-painting on Mt. Victoria early in
’BB (Bs. a day—trade Was good then) I got my
first cheque, £1 75., from The Bulletin. It was
totally unexpected,' for, being in constant work
and getting what I thought such a grand outlet
reo(^r> t f ! 10U8 ' hfcS T? fe , elin o s > I hadn’t dreamed of
receiving payment for literary work—which might
ft™ 1 t rd faC n for th ® Present cashier of The Bul-
But before that 1 had written
and worked, and I have written and worked since
tor Australian unionism and Democracy— for no-
tning. 1 had a strong, deep-down feeling against
taking money for anything I wrote in the interest
about— CaUSe 1 believed ln > and 1 felt red-hot
I hate the wrongs I read about ! I hate the wrones I see!
The marching of that Army is as music unto me !
—“ Army of the Rear," ’B7.
X went a bit mad over—
We’ll make the tyrants feel the strength
Of those that they would throttle !
nee d not say the fault is ours
if blood should stain the wattle !
Freedom on the Wallaby," ’99.
-“ I believed what I wrote was true.
When out of graft awhile in Sydney I helped
turn the old Republican machine, and wrote
h aces m the Street, ” for which I received a
guinea. Along in these times I wrote bush
ballads for the T. and C. Journal, but only
got an occasional half-sovereign. I tried “Tom’’
Butler of. the Freeman'* Journal, of whom
1 have kindly recollections. He told me when T
first.saw him that they didn’t pay for poetry, but I
might bring something round to him; arid if it
was fairly good and suitable for his readers, he
' v T ol;ld see wiiafi he could do. I wrote a few bush
rhymes for him ; whenever I brought one round
he reminded me that they didn’t pa/ for verse—
except, perhaps, at Xmas, and by special arrange-
ment, or tor special stuff ; and whenever he wrote
me a cheque lie never failed to draw my attention to
trie tact that the Freeman's Journal didn’t nay for
poetry, Ti, T and C. proprietary treat, 1m ■
a tittle better later on—but only took Xmas
matter; and, when I got “finally” hard-up in
Sydney, contributed £1 towards my fare to Maori-
land.
But it was before that -in ’8!)-that I went to
Albany, W.A. I painted-; and wrote articles
at a penny a. line for a local paper.
Game back, and lmng out for the best part of
.*() m a third-rate hash-house in Sydney, where I
got some good “copy.” Up country again, and
started house-painting at Bs'. or 9s.' a day, with
every prospect of a good run of work ; but one day
as 1 was painting a ceiling, 1 got a telegram to
say that Brush. Boomerany offered £2 per week
mid a position on the staff.' [ was doubtful of my
abilities, and wired to an old friend in Sydney for
advice. He advised accept; so L accepted.
it was the first, the last, and only chance I got
in journalism. L wrote pars.,' sketches, and verse'
for six months for the £2; and barracked,
siwre tunes, for Democracy, in the Brisbane
M orker, for nothing. 1 got very fond of the work,
ami was with'difficulty, kept out of the office on
Sundays, publishing days, Saturday afternoons,
and other holidays, f might, have been an experi-
enced journalist to-day, with a good “screw ” and
no ambition, but the Bq&nereiuy “ghost” was
fading fast. We hashed up a couple of columns of
pars, from the country papers every week, with
the names of the papers attached -to curry favour
with the country press; and I conceived
the idea of rhyminy this “Country Crumbs”
column, and having it set as prose, and kept
two columns a week going for a couple of months!
You can rhyme anything if you stare at it long
enough between whiles of walking up and down
and scratching your head.
Perhaps the “Country Crumbs” in prosy
rhyme had the same effect on the readers as at
first on the comps. ; anyway the spectre grew less
and less discernible, and deputations of comps,
went up oftener to the sanctum to discuss the in-
advisability of their taking shares in the paper in.
Part-payment of wages. A piece J wrote, caJled
the Gambaroora Star,’’was the Boomer any' a own
epitaph.
I came south, steerage, with £2. It wasn’t the
first tune I went saloon and carno back steer-
age, so to apeak. I got as far as Bathurst
once, during an unemployed period, and came
back m charge'of the guard.
I hung out (with difficulty) in a restaurant in
(Sydney, getting an occasional guinea from The
Bulletin, and painting for nigger-driving bosses
at ss. a day. Hard times had come to Sydney,
and it took a good, all-round tradesman to be sure
of seven or even six “bob” and fairly constant
graft. When the trade failed me I used to write
a column of red-hot socialistic and libellous politi-
cal rhymes for Truth. I .still believed in revolu-
tions, and the spirit of righteousness upheld me.
Truth’s “ghost ” was eccentric, and the usual rates
for outside contributions were from ss. upwards ;
but John JN orton gave me 15s. to£l, for special stuff.
He cursed considerably; and there were times
when it wasn’t advisable to curse back ; but he
saw that I, and one or two other poor devils of
scribblers on their uppers, were paid—even before
the comps. I haven’t forgotten it.
lowards the end of ’92 I got £5 and a rail way-
tieket from The Bulletin and went to Bourkc.
Painted, picked-up in a shearing-shed, and swagged
it for six months ; then came back to Sydney “in
charge of five trucks of cattle.” Bourkc people
will understand that dodge. (Most of my hard-up
experiences are iri my published books, disguised
but not exaggerated.)
Most of the matter in “ While the Billy Boils ”
(and some of what my reviewers considered the
best) was written for Syd. Worker for 12s. 6d. a
column. During one of the frequent interregnums
I edited the Worker a while gratis, on the under-
standing that I should get the permanent editor-
ship for “the Cause” didn’t loom so big in my
eyes as it used to, and I was only then beginning
to find ofit that others had not been quite so
enthusiastic as I was. But that mysterious inner
circle, the trustees and their friends, brought an
editor from another province.
Towards the end of ’93 I landed in Wellington
with a pound in my pocket—just in time to see the
women vote for the first time. Got a little painting
to do now and then, and a guinea (5s. “ out of the
editor’s pocket,” I understood) from the N.Z.
Mail for a 1| col. rhyme called “For’ard.” And
I wrote some steerage sketches at the rate of 5s. a
ool. Did a three-months’ unemployed “ perish, ”
and then went with a mate to a sawmill in the
Hutt Valley, for a boss who had contracted to
supply the mill with logs. We two bullocked in
a rough, wet gully for a fortnight—felling trees,
making a track for the bullocks, and “jacking”
logs to it over stumps and boulders. But we
were soft and inexperienced, and at the end of the
fortnight the boss said we weren’t bushmen—
which, strange to say, hurt me more than any ad-
verse criticism on my literary work could have
done at the time. The boss had no cash ; and my
mate was only restrained from violence by the
fact that he was a big man and the boss a little
one. He gave us each an order for our wages on
the owner of the sawmill in Wellington, and, as
we, had no money for railway fares, we “tramped
it ” —twenty miles, without tucker or tobacco.
Those orders have not been cashed yet.
I house-painted a bit; then got am with a
ganging lineman on a 'telegraph line in South
Island. It was hard graft at first, througfh
rough country, in the depth of winter, and eamp-
ing-out all the time—humping poles some times
where tlie traqe-horses,couldn’t go. Thg boss was
a bit of a-driver, with a fondness for “hazing”
the gang when his liver went wrong ; but it’s
better to be driven to the lamefit of your muscles,
general health, and consequent happiness, than to
be brain-sweated in the city to the danger of your
reason through brooding over it: In four or five
months I was too healthy to read or write, or
bother about it, or anything, or to hate anybody
except the cook when “dun” didu’t eventuate at
reost> 11 able intervals. But there came ,i letter from
the Worker people to say that a Daily Worker ha i
been successfully floated, and tlicre was a place
for me on it. 1 said, “Get behind me, Litera-
ture ! ” hut she didn’t ; so I tiir -w up the hillot,
and caught a steamer that toitulied the coast to de-
liver poles. I arrived in Sydney three days after
the Daily Worker went bung.-
After a deal of slut filing humbug, I was put on the
Weekly Worker as “provincial editor,” but in a
month I received a notice, alleged too-omefrom I lie
trustees, to the effect that, on account of the
financial position of the Workers’ Union, they
were regretfully ol> iyed to dispense with my ser-
vices “ for the present, at least.No one was re
sponsible for the baity Worker, nor for the thousand
pounds sunk in it, nor the crowded staff, exorbitant
“screws” and gross mismanagement of the WerHy
Worker, nor for me- -except, perhaps, the “last
committee.”
House-painting was dead ; clerical work was
always out of the question— I couldn’t add a
column of figures without hanging on like grim
death till 1 got to the top, and two trips with
poor results utterly demoralised me. Deafness
stood in the way of a possible Government billet.
My two books published by Angus and Jtobert-
son, “ In the Days When the World was Wide ”
and “ \V bile the Hilly Boils,” are advertised as in
th.ir seventh thousand and eighth thousand re-
spectively. The further is sold to the public at8s.;
the latter has been sold in various editions at
from os. to 2s. fid. My total receipts from those
books have been something over .£200 ; and .1 have
sold the entire rights. The hooks represent the
cream of twelve years’literary work. 1 estimate
my whole literary earnings during that period at
£700.
I went to W.A. again, painted houses, and
wrote a little for the Western Mail people—who,
by the way, didn’t treat me so badly. Came
back, went to Maori land again, and taught school.
L had a wife by this time. When I came back to
Sydney last year there were three in the party.
Up to a couple of years ago The Bul-
letin paid me at the fixed rate of a guinea a
column; but advances written off and special
prices for special matter brought it nearer 30s. per
col. all through. The only thing I have to com-
plain about with regard to The Bulletin is that
the paper is unable to publish the sketches and
stories within reasonable time. Some of mine
published lately were written and paid for as far
back as ’91. While the publication of “ W.B.B.”
was being arranged for The Bulletin held some
stories and sketches which were to Complete the
“Steelman ’’and “ Mi tohell ’’series ; and, as The
B. could not rush them through, an idea of having
the matter arranged with an eye to sequence had
to be abandoned/ Which explains the apparently
hap-hazard appearance of the order of the stories
arid sketches in that volume, and will be respon-
sible for the same thing in my next prose volume—
which will contain-some introductory sketches to
others in W.B.B.
There are, perhaps, a score of Australian writers
known to The Bulletin, and. most of thorn little
more than lads, who could write better stuff .than
has been appearing in the shoal of popular English
magazines lately (no offenoe intended); but they
have no scope, and, as far as I can see, no hope of
future material encouragement from the “great”
and wealthy Australasian weeklies and dailies,
only-one or two of which (excepting'the Sunday
Times) that I know of have, up to date, offered
even the most niggardly assistance to purely Aus-
tralian writers, and this only after The Bul-
letin had introduced them and established their
Australian reputations. Many papers, notably
in Maoriland, clip .their racy Australian sketches
from The Bulletin ; and in at least one nf
offices that I know, and have a
for, it would be thought an act of charity to X
a hard-up Bulletin writer ss. per eol •wKiiT"
another it would be a mark of special £
offer him a chair I have stood (and walked up
and down and boiled over) for two hours in the
passage outside the office of a paper which had
been “clipping ” my work for years, and tins
because they knew I was hard-up and wanted
them to pay for a contribution by way of a change
Meanwhile, our best Australian artists and
writers are being driven to England and America
—where the leaders are making their mark and a
decent living; and the rest would follow in a lumn
if they got the show; y
, The work of some of those who have gone
brightened Australia for years, yet no one asked
how they lived; and no one, in all the wide 4us
tr as, stood up and asked whether a native-born
artist or writer went aboard the boat with a
decent suit to his back, or a five-pound note in his
pocket. And they talk about our “cheap,” “un-
healthy ”or “affected pessimism ” ! The fools !
A last word for myself. I don’t know about
the merit or value of my work ; all I know
is that I started a shy, ignorant lad from
the Bush, under every disadvantage arising from
poverty and lack of education, and with the extra
disadvantage of partial deafness thrown in I
started with implicit faith in human nature, and a
heart full of love for Australia, and hatred for
wrong and injustice. 1 taught mvself a trade—
the first years in Sydney I rose at five o’clock in
the morning to go to work with a rough crowd
ill the factory of a hard taskmaster ; and
learnt the little 1 did at a night-schoof; and
1 worked even then, before 1 could write
for a okuse I believed in. I sought out my
characters and studied thfeui ; I wrote of nothing
that 1 had not myself seen or exftarieneed : I
wrote and re-wrote painfully, and believed that
every line was true and for the right. 1 kept
Steady and worked hard for seven years, and that
work met with appreciation in Australia and a
warm welcome.in .London. When desperately
hard up and with a wife to provide for, X at last
was forced to apply to the Govt, for temporary
work. T was kept hanging about the office
for weeks; and when, as a last resource, 1
applied for a railway-pass for a month to
enable me to find work in the country arid
gather now. material for literary work, I did
not receive a reply. I was obliged to seek the
means of earning-bread and butter from the Govt,
of a province (.M.L.) in whose people’s interests X
had never written a line. .
My advice to any young Australian writer
whose talent® have been recognised, would be to
go steerage, stow away, swim, and seek London,
yankooland, or Tim buetoo—-rather than stay m,
Australia till his genius turned to gall, or beef.
Or, failing this and still in the interestsnf human
nature aitd literature—to study elementary ana-
tomy, especially ins applies to the cranium, and
then shoot hirnsulf carefully with tl)o aid of a
looking-glass.

lie “tx.13.” on Mark Twain. No
one can find fuult with the riifht of a writer to term the

enough to say what he does not think, and his remarks
on the “ Vicar of Wakefield” convinced me of his sin-
cerity, even were his taste in question. He says of it in
one of his magazine articles : “ That strange menagerie
of complacent hypocrites and idiots, of typical Cheap
•John heroes and heroines who are always showing off, of
bad people who are not interesting, and'good people who

character that invites respect.” t re-read “the Vicar”
after reading this wholesale condemnation, and can only
say; “ Them’s mv sentiments.” Voltaire has given us in
“ Candide ” a lesson on the folly of pretending to appre-
ciate authors simply because of their reputation, when
he makes l’ocnourante say of Homer—" It is a book one
must have in one's library simply as a monument of anti-
quity ; just like those rusty old coins no longer used in
commerce. . . . Fools admire everything contained in a
celebrated author. I read only for myself, and I only
care for that which suits myself,” Put Mark Twain in
the place of Voltaire, and you have his views to a T.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Note to Nell.
(Per favor of THE BULLETIN. )
I watched the full harvest-moon springing,
White-faced, o’er the rim of the .
And the soldier-birds homeward went wingin B
While all for a moment was still-
Now round my camp crickets are che p n >
And yonder, in accents more hars ,
The bull-frogs, awakened from sleeping.
Chant hoarsely their tunes in the marsh.
The pine in my camp-fire is crackling,
The summer night gathers m soon,
Though a jackass, belated, is cackling
At the placid white face of the moon ;
On the sward the soft shadows are lying
Vague and dark—as the moon-shadows do,
And gently the night-wind is Sl^'i n g ,
And its sigh sets me sighing—for you .
As the tired bird at evening flies restward
When the gay, garish daylight is done.
So the broken-up bushman hies westward
When he’s finished and paid for his fun.
Yes, it strikes one as rather a pity
So many things chanced to go wrong,
Or I might have been yet in the city.
Not hereabouts—singing this song l
Well! this jingle I send you to tell you
You still are “ the dearest and best,
And there’s one fellow will not forget you
When roving about in the West ;
But the low-burning pine-log gives warning
To end this (I write by its light),
There’s a long stage to ride m the morning
So Sweetheart, so-long—and Good-JNight!
BREAKER.
That Day at Boiling Downs.
He was driving Irish tandem, but perhaps I talk
I’d forgotten for a moment you are not all mulga-
WhaTf mean’s he had his swag up through his
having knocked his nag up ;
He had .smio in off the Cooper-anyhow, that s
what he said. , , ~ . ,
And ho looked as full of knowledge as a thirty-
As Imanswered to the question—“ How’s things
look the way you come?
“ Well, they were a trifle willing for a bit. 1 Meres
been some killing ; , .
In fact, I’m the sole survivor of the district
mine’s a rum ! ”
Then we all got interested in the chap as In-
divested , , ... , .
Himself of a fat puppy that he carried in his
But he said no more until he had put down his
swag and billy . .
And had taken off his bluchers just to empty out
Bits^of o cork were tied with laces round his hat in
manv places,
Out of which he gave the puppy some refreshment,
and began— ■ ~ .. . .
“ Sammy Suds was bound’ry-riding, quite content
and law abiding, , „
Till he bought some reading-matter one day off a
hawker man.
“ Then he started to go ratty, and began to fancy
that he , , . ,
Was an Injun on the warpath ; so he plaited a
Shaved and smear’d his face with raddle, and
knocked up a greenhide saddle,
After creeping on his belly through the grass a
mile or two. . . .
Then he decked himself in feathers, and went out
and scalped some wethers —_
Just to give himself a lesson in the sanguinary
Sammy’then dug up the hatchet, chased a snake
but couldn’t catch it,
Killed his dog, lassooed a turkey, scalped the cat
and made a start.
“ And he caused a great sensation when he landed
at the station ; , . ..
And the boss said, ‘Hello! Sammy, what the
devil’s up with you ?’ , , .
‘lam Slimy Snake the Snorter, wretched pale-
face, crave not quarter ! ’
He replied, and with a shot-gun nearly blew the
boss in two. ... .
Next, the wood-and-water joey fell a victim to
his bowie, .
And the boss’s weeping widow got a gash from
ear to ear, . ,
And you should have seen his guiver when he
scalped the bullock-driver .
And made openings for a horse-boy, servant-maid,
and overseer.
“Counting jackaroos and niggers, he had put up
double figures,
When ensued his awful combat with a party of
new-chums, , , ,
All agog to do their duty, with no thought of
home or beauty, , , ,
But he rubbed them out as rapid as a school-boy
would his sums. , ,
Out across the silent river, with some duck-shot m
Went the store-man, and a lassooed lady left in
the same boat.
Sam then solved the Chinese question—or at least
made a suggestion— ,
For he dragged one from a barrel by the tail and
out his throat.
“But, with thus the job completed, Sammy he got
overheated T fit , ..
And dropped dead of apoplex.v-I felt better
when lie did! . ... T ,,
For I’d got an awful singeing while I d watched
this mulga engine
Doing all that I’ve related—through a cracked
brick oven-lid. _
And when now I find men strangled, or I come
across the mangled ,
Corpses of a crowd of people or depopulated
Or even a blood-stained river, I can scarce repress
o shiver
For my nerves were much affected that day out on
‘ Boiling Downs.’”
JACK MATHIEU.

HISTORY Of
YANKEE DOODLE.
SEE
DIXSON’S TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
It) An?*rica.
ID Australia.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father, with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head filing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
grandsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight, The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
arose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Doodle was heard as never was heard
before or since.
YANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to jDroduce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD TN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Y ales alone now reach
Tons per Day.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TONGUE. Every smoker should
TRY FOR HIMSELF.

During the past year the Commissioners
j.f Taxation have tested the accuracy of a
number of tax-payers’ returns by personal
inspection of their accounts, and it is pro-
posed to submit several hundred cases each
year to investigation ■and verification. It is
satisfactory to note that in nearly every in-
stance where the tax has been increased the
reason haa;been explained to the satisfaction
of the taxpayer. Already in numerous
cases persons have, for the first time, an in-
telligent idea of their operations and
financial position, and not a few have ex-
pressed the opinion that this alone goes far
!to recompense them for the trouble each
year in the preparation of the returns.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 21, 1899.
“The Push.”
During the late holiday season, as is usual
at holiday seasons even a little more than at
other times, there was much moan and press
lamentation, especially in Melbourne and
Sydney, about “ the push.” The aggressive,
yelling, soft-hatted “stoush” brigade was
about equally rampant in both cities. It
wasn’t much worse than usual, for the suffi-
cient reason that, even in its Christmas and
New Fear festivities, it couldn’t be much
worse than it always is. But it is noticed
more at such times, because then the placid,
guileless person from the country comes into
town and gets his stomach bounded upon
unexpectedly by sixteen feet, and he isn’t
used to such treatment as the city man is,
and doesn’t take it so philosophically. Owing
to the lament he raises about the sixteen
feet on his stomach it- looks as if the push
was worse than usual at holiday seasons, but
in reality it keeps up much the same reign of
terror all the year round. It did one or two
casual homicides in Sydney within the last
few weeks, and booted one or two people out
of their usual semblance of humanity. And in
Melbourne it practically holds possession of
Carlton, right alongside the business centre
of the city, at all times, and it has taken to
committing occasional outrages in Bourke
and Collins streets early in the evening, if
not actually by daylight. But it has been
doing that sort of thing, more or less, for
several years now.
* * » *
Few things are more remarkable than the
patient, mild, cringing acquiescence of the
Australian citizen in this state of things.
The push is a mere handful at best,
keeps up something akin to a reign of terror
in the great cities of Melbourne and Sydney,
year in aaid year out. No one seriously
attempts to extinguish it. The law allows
the magistrate and the amateur J.P. a wide
latitude in their dealings with push outrage?
For the most brutal form of assault and in-
timidation, and conspiracy to batter unoffend-
ing citizens, they may do almost anything
from 2s. 6d. fine or Christian remonstrance
with the offender, up to his committal for
trial—in which case a judge may give him a
long term of gaol. Generally the magistrate or
J.P., with that curious sympathy for brutality
which is one of the characteristics of Anglo-
Saxon law, lets the push off with a small fine
and a Christian remonstrance. Parliament,
being built of the same material as the
sympathetic “ beak,” never attempts to apply
the obvious remedy—which is to take away
the discretion which the lower courts use so
badly, and to fix a heavy minimum penalty
for assault, particularly assault in company.
The people, being built of the same material
as Parliament, never insist on its doing any-
thing. And no Government ever attempts
seriously to take the initiative in the matter.
Premiers George Turner and George Reid
are the accomplices of the push, to all intents
and purposes. They hold the power of sup-
pression, and won’t use it, neither will they
get out of the road and make way for other
people, who might be built of better mate-
rial. And if that isn’t being an accomplice,
what is ? Though the push has been a
notorious curse in Melbourne and Sydney
for a dozen or twenty years, there is not so
much as a single law on the statute-book
specially directed at its suppression. No
cities in the world have “taken it lying
down ” more humbly than these two big
capitals. To strangers who visit this country
the humility of Australia towards its few
dozen or few hundred loud-mouthed pariahs
is a source of wonderment.
* * * *
The general attitude of this country is that
the evil must be endured because it can’t be
put down. Considering that no Legislature
has yet made any attempt to discover whether
it can be put down or not, this attitude is
curiously unenterprising. At present the
situation is that the punishment for gross
and hideous outrages, committed by gangs
which exist largely for the express purpose
of outrage, is anything from Christian re-
monstrance by the “beak” up to a pro-
longed period of hard labor. Generally the
“beak” strikes an average and makes it
40s. Sometimes, when some province has
felt worse than usual about the matter, it
has been suggested that the punishment
shall be increased so as to range from
pious reproof up to a more prolonged
period of hard labor. In that case, the
magistrate, being still the same old magis-
trate, will probably keep on making it 40s.
Occasionally, when the community has felt
very bitter indeed, it has been suggested
that the penalty shall be further enlarged so
that it will range from some very serious
remarks by the court up to three floggings,
and in that case the same old magistrate will
go on making it 40s. The idea always is,
not to make the magistracy use the powers
which it possesses and which it systematically
neglects, but to give it some extra powers
accompanied by full permission to neglect
them also.
* * * *
Some day, when The Bulletin is in a
worse temper than usual, it will draft its own
Bill for the Suppression of the Push. It
will be not so much a new idea in the
way of punishments as a scheme for mak-
ing the law authorities administer the old
ideas in a serious spirit. It will recognise
assault committed in company as a special
offence (push assaults are almost invariably
the work of two or three or more, which dis-
tinguishes them from the ordinary two-man
shindy in a public-house), and the minimum
penalty for a first offence shall be 12 months.
For a second offence the minimum shall be
two years ; for any further offences, three
years. This isn’t very different from what
the law allows now ; the real difference will
lie in the fact that the option now allowed to
a doddering magistracy, or a magistracy
which sympathises with this particular form
of crime, of not administering the law, will
be done away with. On top of this, and by
way of a novel experiment, it will be pro-
vided that when any person has been twice
convicted under this statute it will be an
offence under the vagrancy law for him to be
found in any public street between 8 p.m.
and 5 a.m. any time within three years from
the date of the aforesaid second conviction,
and if he is so found the minimum will be
three months. When he has been thrice
convicted it will be made an offence calling
for three months for him to be found in the
country at all at any hour. What other
country he will be found in is “his own
funeral.”
* * * *
Of course this scheme is not advanced as
a certain remedy for pushism. Nothing is
certain that is wholly untried. But the
reason of it is that imprisonment is practi-
cally the only thing the law has invented up
to date ; the law believes in it, at least till
it can invent something better; therefore as
the push is admittedly an evil, and requires
to be put down, this paper suggests, by way
of a wild novelty, that imprisonment should
be tried. It is tried for almost every other
offence ; why not for this also ? There
would be less of the push then, for a much
greater part of it would be away in gaol.
There would also be less of it because the
worst elements of it would be liable, if they
appeared in the highways in customary push
hours, to summary running-in. The primary
evil is the fact that the push pervades the
streets at all, and the shortest way to
deal with that is by making it illegal for
it» worst elements to pervade the streets.
It would probably require a much larger
police force to carry out such a law, but the
choice lies, in any case, between a much
larger police force or a continuance of the
existing state of terrorism. If the law was
too stringent, there is always the .rest of the
globe for the push to emigrate to. Australia
has tried for many years the experiment of
not being stringent enough, and a change in
the other direction would be refreshing.
And Australia has received, and still receives,
so many dubious foreign characters that it
could export a few on very fair terms.
* * * *
Such a measure as is here suggested would
doubtless be a serious inroad on the liberty
of the subject, as it is commonly understood
in Australia. But the liberty of the subject
may be viewed in two ways. One view is
that the larrikin, no matter how often he is
convicted, has the same right to pervade the
streets as anybody else, provided he isn’t
actively engaged in assault and smash.
Also, that he has a right to gather at
street corners, and expectorate, and say
“ blanky ” in moderation, and to do any-
thing short of actual law-breaking. That is
the liberty of the subject as it has been care-
fully preserved in Australia up to date, and
the results are loud and numerous. But the
subject that the writer is thinking about is
the other one—the one whose liberty is
never even considered under existing condi-
tions. His position is that he doesn’t want
to be compelled to listen to “ blanky ” at all
hours, or to be jostled at street corners by a
crowd whose chief mission in life is stoush,
and which is liable to break out and do more
stoush at any moment. In fact, the choice
lies between preserving the liberty of, per-
haps, 1000 persons of disagreeable habits and
offensive expectoration, or of the other
400,000 members of the population, and it
is simply proposed, as a strange and reckless
innovation, that the liberty of the larger
crowd should be considered—just a little.
The idea is offered in a tentative fashion. It
is something quite opposed to Australian
traditions, and it is a gross outrage on Aus-
tralia’s cherished hoodlum. Still it might
possibly be worth consideration.
Turner Receives Notice.
The Man who Owns Victoria has given notice
to the Turner Government that the graduated
tax on large incomes must he reduced, and that
Fat will no longer stand the treatment it has
met with during the last few years. David
Syme, of the Melbourne Age, as a man with a
large income himself, speaks feelingly in this
matter. The reason assigned why the income-
tax must be reduced is that, under the present
system, foreign capital is being driven out of
the country, and Victoria is no longer import-
ing debt with the same vigor as of old, and the
influx of embarrassments and liabilities has
seriously diminished, and, consequently, the
load of interest is not so awful as it was. And
these evils are ascribed by the great Syme organ
to the graduated income-tax, which, it is
solemnly averred, is “the highest in-
come-tax in the world.” As a matter of
fact, it is nothing of the kind, though pro-
bably that oratorical flourish wasn’t meant
to be taken seriously. In Victoria the tax com-
mences with the usual exemption of ,£2OO.
After that, if the income is derived from per-
sonal exertion, the rates are 4d. in the £ for
the first £lOOO above the exemption limit ; 6d.
in the £ for the next £1000; Bd. in the £ for
the remainder of the income. If the revenue
is derived from property the rates are doubled,
so a person with a comfortable revenue of
£2500 a year from property to keep the wolf
off the front verandah would pay £lO3 6s. Bd.
InN.S.W., where the Fat Man who lives on
his pile is more tenderly treated, he would
pay £57 10s. ; in Queensland or Westralia,
nothing; in South Australia, £115; in Tas-
mania, £125 ; in Maoriland, £B5. In Italy the
tax would be from £375 to £5OO, according to
the nature of the property, and in some Euro-
pean countries even more. The Victorian, with
an income of £2500 a year from personal exer-
tion, pays £5l 13s. 4d., whereas in Tasmania
he would pay £B3 6s. Bd. ; in N.S.W., £57
10s.; in S.A., £57 10s.; in Maoriland, £B5.
On incomes ranging from £4OOO upwards and
derived from the investment of capital the
Victorian income-tax is certainly, by reason of
the graduated principle, the heaviest in Aus-
tralasia (though not in the world, or anything
like it), but on persons whose affluence isn’t
bloated out of all bounds it is rather a moderate
impost and below the Australasian average.
Probably David Syme and about two dozen
other men at the most are so rich that they pay
a heavier tax, under the Victorian graduated
system, than they would pay in any other of
the Seven Provinces. Therefore, the wealthy
Age proprietor is an interested party, and
speaks from the bottom of his pocket as one
having authority.
* ♦ * *
But when the very wealthy gentleman tries
to persuade Victoria that it is going to ruin be-
cause he and a few more very wealthy gentlemen
are heavily taxed, the Age autocrat becomes
very unconvincing. He complains :
The most cursory inquiry into the subject will show
that this tax has been, if not the sole cause, at least the
primary reason for a large amount of capital leaving the
colony. Mr. J. C. Stewart, of Messrs. Malleson, Eng-
land, and Stewart, the well-known solicitors, who pro-
bably handle more English capital sent out here for
investment than any other firm in Australia, states, on
authority that has excellent means of gauging the situa-
tion, that the loss has not been less than £8,000,000. Mr.
W. Riggall, of the firm of Messrs. Blake and Riggall,
another firm entrusted with British investments, admits
the difficulty of proving that the income-tax has sent
capital elsewhere, but he points out what an essential
element it must be in determining investment. An Eng-
lish company, say, has £lOO,OOO invested here on mort-
gage at 4 per cent, in preference to taking 3£ per cent, at
Home. The money would produce here £4OOO a year, as
against U 3500 in England. Suddenly an income-tax up to
Is. 4d. in the ,£ is imposed in Victoria. That investment
is taxed to the extent of £2lO, and when to that must be
added the cost of exchange between here and London,
and other charges necessarily higher than those prevail-
ing in England, it will be readily understood that the
feeling of greater security in an English mortgage, the
fact that the conditions, industrial and financial, are
better understood, and that property on which the money
is lent bein<r under immediate observation, must turn the
scale in favor of keeping the money at home rather than
accept the little larger profit the colonial investment
might show. Mr. Riggall describes the property-tax as
the last straw of the boom. Capitalists had to accept
less interest than they were getting before, and this
abnormal charge of Is. 4d. in the pound—the highest
income-tax in the world —settled the point with many a
hesitating investor, and money was withdrawn whole-
sale. The same cautious authority observes that the
evil did not end with the taking away of money
already invested. Large sums were waiting to be
put into Australian securities when the country had
recovered from the depression. But the income-tax
stopped all that, and the money found its way into some
other channel. The immediate effect on Victoria is
obvious, owing to the income-tax being higher here than
in the other colonies. Now, if Messrs. Blake and Riggall
have any money sent to them for investment they advise
their clients to go to New South Wales, where the
income-tax is only 6d. On that £lOO,OOO at 4 per cent,
example we have previously quoted, the investor in New
South Wales would have to pay only £lOO income-tax,
whereas in Victoria he would be taxed to the extent of
£2lO, so that, other things being equal—and the return
for money is just as good in the mother colony as here—
there is a substantial reason for those looking for securi-
ties to go across the Murray.
Messrs. Malleson, England, and Stewart are still
more emphatic as to the evil effects of this heavy impost.
They estimate that in the last three years the amount
of capital invested through their office has dwindled by
nearly £1,000,000, surely as the result of the tax. They
have constant illustrations of it. Mortgages fall due,
and, instead of renewing, clients say that now the tax has
been put on they can do better with their money at
home. The same thing occurred at the Income Tax Office
in the first year of the operation of the Act. People who
had to pay Is. arid Is. 4d. in the £ accompanied their re-
mittances with an intimation that they would not submit
to such a thing again, and would send their capital where
it would not have to bear such a heavy burden.
* * * *
Supposing this is all true, those who lament
about absentee capital being driven out by demo-
cratic legislation, and about the evil results of
taxing the fatted absentee’s money, should go
and hide themselves in abasement behind a herb
for the rest of their days. Victoria, apparently,
owes Britain £8,000,000 less than it did, which
is a tremendous reduction of its liabilities. It
has to pay about £50,000 a year less interest,
which is a blessed thing to happen to any
country. There is £6,700,000 gold and silver
in the Victorian banks, which is only £750,000
less than they could show in 1893, when there
was no income-tax at all, and if £8,000,000 of
debt has been cleared off with a visible loss of
only £750,000 it is an arrangement which
ought to satisfy all parties. The Victorian
banks, which all went smash (the little Royal
only excepted) in ’93, when there was no
income-tax, are coming right again. The
income-tax for last year amounted to £2OOO
more than for 1897, so whether capitalists are
flying out of the country to avoid the burden
or not, the aggregate income of the people is
growing larger. The revenue began to improve
the very year when the Turner Government
started to judiciously discourage the great
absentee capitalist with an impost which ran as
high as Is. 4d. in the £, and it has been im-
proving ever since. The number of employes
in factories has increased from 39,000 in 1893,
when there was no income-tax, to over 50,000
so apparently the investment of capital in in-
dustries goes on well. And, curiously enough,
the man who wants to borrow money now on
decent “ shecurity ” can get it about 2 per cent,
cheaper since capital has fled out of the country
than he was able to do before when it hadn’t
fled. Also, it is noticeable that the Age doesn’t
attempt to show that a single bad result has
followed the alleged exit of all this alleged
money.
* * * *
Plainly, if foreign capital has bolted out of
Victoria, that province is getting on a great
deal better without it. And if the graduated
income-tax is the cause of its flight as the Age
—which ought to know something about it—
alleges, then Victoria owes a debt of gratitude
to its graduated income-tax. It was the
coming in of foreign capital which caused all
the country’s woes to begin with. The banks
got loaded up with immense masses of foreign
deposits on which they had to pay interest.
They had consequently to earn interest on them
somehow, and there not being enough safe in-
vestments to put the coin into they put a lot of
it into unsafe ones, and chanced the conse-
quences. The great downfall was only the
natural result, and Victoria sat on an ash-
heap and scraped its boil with a potsherd for
six years as the result of the arrival of foreign
absentee capital. If David Syme can substan-
tiate his statement that the deplorable sub-
stance is really leaving, the province should
raise a big brass statue to him, and another to
Turner as the man whose graduated income-
tax caused its departure. Also, Syme should
get his own income-tax remitted for life as a
sign of gratitude ; and Victoria should express
its regret that it didn’t know when the foreign
cash w'as going, so that it could be down to
light a bonfire on the shore by way of celebra-
tion. No doubt to the two firms quoted by the
Age the fact that foreign capital isn’t coming
into the country is an evil—they lose a lot of
commission. But neither they nor anyone else
ever attempts to prove that it is a disaster to
the country at large—they simply allude in an
off-hand manner to the stoppage in the impor-
tation of debts and financial embarrassments as
“this evil.” And any person who was in-
terested in the importation of germs or bubonic
plague or lunatics or diseased paupers might
allude to the stoppage of that influx as “ this
evil ” also if he wanted to.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Boot-Trunk Discrepancy.
The Yarra boot-trunk mystery is a little
darker since it has been cleared up—so far as
the clearing-up bad gone at time of writing—
than it was before. In its original uncleared
state the principal materials of it were quite
understandable. There was a dead woman in
a box, and a box in a river. The woman had
arsenic in her stomach ; therefore, presumably,
an attempt had been made at either murder or
suicide by poison. She had also been suffo-
cated, therefore, if the arsenic meant attempted
murder, the intending assassin had got tired of
waiting. As the woman didn’t look like the
sort of woman who would crawl into a box, and
cord herself on the outside, and then throw
herself into the river, she had evidently been
thrown in by someone else ; it was merely a
question of locating the woman and the mur-


wpt- And now the clearing up of the mys-
tery 'buries it in illimitable gloom It is
1 Wed. that the deceased died through being
t Piled for abortion. The treatment consisted
■ the application of a galvanic battery and the
Oondy’s fluid, and it is seriously alleged
That when the harmless Condy’s fluid was
rtlied to her she gave a wild scream and
dimpled up and died. How anyone should
pi of the application of Condy’s fluid passes
‘prehension. Then, the woman having died
f Condy’s fluid, the persons concerned crowded
her in a boot-trunk and tried to hide the crime
■ the river—goodness only knows why, for
there is no law against the use of Condy’s
fluid and besides it was applied under medical
dvice The doctor who suggested its use saw
the body after death, heard and disbelieved the
verv precipitous narrative about the fluid—and
'ent away. The arsenic in the stomach is
nuite unaccounted for ; the parties who are
responsible for the great Condy’s fluid discre-
pancy swear there was no arsenic in the house.
Anyhow, there wasn’t enough of it in the
stomach to cause death, so apparently it was
there only by way of ornament. The appear-
ance of death by suffocation was also quite un-
accounted for. According to the confession of
the various people implicated, the death took
place on 13th December, and the doctor who
took things so philosophically saw the body on
the afternoon of that date. It was found
about five days later, and the doctors who
examined it were, and still are, certain it
had been dead from 14 to 21 days, and they
all swear to death by suffocation. Also, though
the confession sets forth that the body was
thrown into the river on the 14th, a man heard
something heavy being thrown in on the 6th—
at the same place, too, where the alleged throw-
ing in took place on the 14th. Nobody seems
to have heard anything being thrown in on the
14th. The parties allegedly concerned were
Tod the young man who brought the deceased
girl ’into trouble ; Madame Radalyski, who is
said to have cured her till she died ; and the
latterVservant, described variously as Beatrice
Jamieson and Thekla Regina Dubberke. The
latter is said to have supplied the boot-trunk,
helped to crowd the body into it, and assisted
at carting it away. This calm and useful girl
alleges that her conscience troubled her so
badly afterwards that she couldn’t sleep, and
she had to confess. Yet she apparently didn’t
want very badly to confess. She was arranging
to leave for Maoriland, but before starting she
gave way to a yearning to gossip about
the affair, and threw out mysterious Dead-
wood Dick hints to an acquaintance re
her knowledge of the boot - trunk horror,
and he sooled the detectives on her and
thereby cut her retreat short. The head which
caused so much excitement at the morgue was
alleged to be, to all appearance, that of a half-
caste girl or one with at least a streak of
Asiatic blood in her, and the alleged deceased
wasn’t a half-caste ; also, there was a scar on
the head in the morgue, which isn’t mentioned
in the descriptions of Mabel Ambrose —the
alleged victim of Condy’s fluid. No doubt
Mabel Ambrose will somehow be crowded in
to fit the circumstances —apparently there is
nothing else to do but make her fit them some-
how. But it promises to be a very tight fit.
A Discreditable Situation.
David Syme’s organ, in one of its aggressively
Tory moods, states that “ it seems anything but
a creditable confession to make that the
Britishers in Maoriland require special protec-
tion against an alien race.” This is apropos
of the Maoriland Government’s resolve to shut
down on the influx of cheap Austrian peasant
immigration, and it is the same idiotic jibe
which 40 Tory organs have got off their chests
on the same subject. The burden of their wail
is : “ If Maoriland, with its boasted prosperity,
and its boasted socialistic policy, is afraid of
the foreign alien, and can’t hold its
him, the said policy is plainly a failure. It is
a very illogical groan, anyhow, but the Tory
isn’t naturally logical. It is just the country
which is most prosperous, and where wages are
highest, and the state of the masses most
satisfactory, which needs special protection
against the cheap alien. The fact that Maoriland
wants that special protection more than other
countries simply goes to prove that its socialistic
policy is a success. The circumstance that the
“ Britisher ” there needs to take special pre-
cautions against the low-priced foreigner is
eminently creditable to the “Britisher in
every way. If the Maoriland adult male worker
were a cheap and miserable slave in a hovel,
and his very highest wages were 15s. a week,
then he wouldn’t need to care about the influx
of a crowd of Austrian laborers who are accus-
tomed to live on 18s. or £l. The 20s. a week
worker couldn’t undersell and cut down the
wages of the 15s. a week man, and the latter
would have no need to worry ; he would simply
starve the Austrian out, and the influx would
stop. If the Maorilander was lower still —if he
was so utterly low and miserable that his earn-
ings were only 4s. a week, or half-a-crown
then he wouldn’t need special protection
against any race on earth. He could
underbid and starve out even the Jap.
and the Chinaman, and he could view the
influx of the Hindoo with composure, knowing
that the first set of niggers would promptly
write to the remaining niggers at home that
this was no place for any nigger to come to.
But because wages are comparatively liberal in
Maoriland, and the working-classes have a high
standard of comfort, therefore it is a place that
the cheap alien wants to come to ; and lie will
come in swarms, till excessive competition re-
duces wages and fills the labor-market with
unemployed and the country with distress—
unless he is shut out. It is just as discreditable
to the Maorilander that he requires special legis-
lation to shut out cheap foreign labor as it is to
Britain that it requires an army and navy
to shut out another kind of invasion. Other
nations want to invade Britain because, through
J. Bull’s energy, the country is worth in-
vading, so he has to go in for special protection
—which is eminently discreditable to Idtn. It
his country was as empty as Greenland no one
would want to invade it, and he could do with-
out the special protection, and that, by the
a—>„ would be eminently creditable.
And if the Maoriland worker was so low down
that no alien could undersell him in the labor-
market and live, then he wouldn’t want special
protection either—which would also be emin-
ently creditable.

“Our Reptile Contemporary.”
Barrier Truth (N.S.W. Labor organ) has a
grievance against this paper :
“ The Bulletin has an idea of a new kind of Upper
House,” modestly says that journal. Unfortunately for
The Bulletin’s' reputation for originality, anyone ac-
quainted with the constitution suggested by the Federal
Convention could have evolved this new idea. This won-
derful scheme is based upon the supposition that the two
Houses are to be “ equally powerful, equally democratic,
equally representative.” Tommy rot! If this threefold
equality could exist, what object could there be in having
the double representative machinery? The franchise
proposed for the Upper House is directly designed to pre-
vent this equality in fact, though it has a treacherous
semblance of it in form. This, like any other provisions
imaginable for the maintenance of a second chamber, has
only one object—that is to give representation to pro-
perty at the expense of persons. There is a lot of shoddy
democracy about The Bulletin.
This paper’s proposal was that the N.S.W.,
Upper House should be a paid body, elected
and dissolved at the same time as the Assembly,
with no property qualification for members, its
members to be chosen by absolute manhood (or
manhood and womanhood) suffrage in the same
way as the Assembly, no plural vote allowed, and
the only difference between the two Houses to
be that the Council should be created by the
whole province voting as one constituency, so
that there should be one chamber that would
be independent of the local log-rolling and
road-and-bridge interest, and which would
represent N.S.W. as a whole. And the Bar-
rier Truth (organ of a party which
supports George Reid, the man who says
he has a great respect for the present
nominee Council and is going to maintain it
with only slight alteration) actually calls this a
scheme to “ give representation to property at
the expense of persons.” Think of absolute
One-man-one-vote, with no property suffrage at
all, being a scheme “ to give representation to
property ! ” If it is, then One-man-one-vote in
the Assembly is, presumably, ditto, and the
Barrier Truth is a bloated organ of wealth
and monopoly. By the way, Barrier Truth’s
assertion that The Bulletin’s idea was bor-
rowed from the Federal Convention is a very
gorgeous perversion. The idea in question was
advocated in a leading article in the issue of
3rd August, 1895, and many times thereafter,
and it was included in a suggested Federal
Constitution published in The Bulletin on
23rd January, 1897. Possibly some members
of the Federal Convention borrowed the idea
from this paper, or possibly they got there
separately. Anyhow, this paper got there
first. There was a time when the Labor organs
"didn’t descend to mean perversions of this kind,
and very few of them do it now—but Barrier
Truth is evidently an exception.
Unclaimed Loot.
Wail of woe from the Bankers’ Magazine of
Australasia :
• Socialistic legislative experiments are being rushed
along at such a pace in New Zealand that conservative
people like bankers are simply aghast, and thoughtful
men shake their heads ominously when the subject is
referred to. In addition to State insurance, old age pen-
sions, resumption by State of freehold lands, is now added
the last piece of legislation, intituled, “ The New Zealand
Unclaimed Moneys Act, 1898,” which has passed both
Houses of Parliament. The Act provides for the handing
over to the Government by “ every company,” after the
passing of the Act, moneys unclaimed for six years.
“ Company ” is stated in the interpretation-clause of the
Act to mean every registered company, and to include
banks. Of course, the object is apparent, namely, that
the State shall in future collar unclaimed moneys left
with banks, there being a popular idea that the banks get
quite a mine of wealth from this source ; but, from an
inner view we had from one tolerably large bank, we can
say that-, whatever may have happened in the good old
times of the early fifties, there is precious little money
left unclaimed with any bank nowadays. It is not quite
clear either why the State should reap where it has not
sown, or even garnered, but, in New Zealand, the State
is going to take care of everjmne, so it is only right it
should take care of any stray money that is knocking
about.
Curious, when one comes to think of it, that it
should be such a shocking thing for the
State —the community at large—to “ reap
where it has not sown,” while the fact
that the banks have always done exactly the
same thing is quite right and commendable.
Also, that it should be such a violent rushing
along of socialistic experiments for the State to
take over unclaimed money left in banks ;
while for the banks to take the same
moneys isn’t a wild or dangerous socialistic
experiment at all. Of course, the State’s object
is apparent; it wants to “ collar unclaimed
moneys left with banks. And the banks
object is equally apparent: they also want to
collar unclaimed moneys left with banks. So
things are about even. And it is also interest-
ing to note (1) that the amount involved is so
small as not to be worth making any row about,
and (2) that the bankers and their organ are
making an awful ro.w about it. The banks
have annexed forgotten deposits for their own
use ever since banking started in Australasia
seventy years ago, and never complained once.
Why do they object to the State doing what
they do themselves ? Isn’t imitation the sin-
cerest flattery ? And wasn’t the State justified
in regarding the annexation of unclaimed de-
posits as an honest, respectable, conservative
proceeding when it saw a respectable, conser-
vative, and aggressively honest crowd like the
banking fraternity doing it ?

Clontarf Bill.
“ E.” in reply :
Dear Bulletin.— Your “ utter disgust ” at my apology
for the backbonelessness of the Carlton (Melb.) magis-
trates doesn’t make me in the least ashamed of it. Your
eomments will nor, convince anyone who has had practical
experience of the management of the ‘‘dangerous
classes ” by policemen. You say : “ Carlton became the
pushiest suburb because the local Bench advertised it as
the place where harmless people could be crippled with
impunity.” And I say, in reply, that Carlton has been a
larrikin district for the past 25 years, and has been
notorious, or otherwise, for the prevalence of ruffianism,
according to whether the police held the ill-conditioned
“ roughs ” in awe, or didn’t hold them in awe. Again,
vou say “the police can’t break up pushes by them-
selves.” And I sav that pushes never come to pass m
districts where policemen are fairly numerous and in-
dividually strong— i.e., masterful as well as musculai. 1
think I am correct in stating that Carlton, in these days
of retrenchment, has been left to the control of only/oar
Dolioemen who, more often than not, are young and un-
authoHtative. The larrikin, let me tell you, is a dog, and
a cowardly dog as a rule. He snarls and snaps at young
policemen, but when the hard, seasoned veteran in blue
scowls at him and bids him begone, he slinks away, dog
that he is. In the letter which excited your disgust I
said, “ Brutality flourishes around districts where ill-con-
ditioned Jims and Bills and Pats are allowed to exchange
swears on a large scale in the public thoroughfare, until
they acquire the confidence of numbers, and don t care a
blanky for the guardians of the peace, and form them-
selves into a prudential intimidation society.’ And I say
it again. Figuratively speaking, I stand upon a chair in
the editorial sanctum to repeat the assertion of what I
know to be true. The larrikin must be overawed in his
early snarling and snapping days, or he will grow bold
enough to bite. You smile contemptuously at my old-
fashioned doctrine. Some of your poets sing the “manli-
ness” of Clontarf Bill and his kind, and that’s where my
little bit of utter Disgust comes in.—E.
The Bulletin owns up to “Clontarf Bill
with apologies. All the same, “E.F. ” care-
fully dodges the principal issue. The push
trouble is very bad in Carlton, however it
started. The four policemen at least contrive to
arrest a lot of offenders, and the local J s.P.
practically refuse to punish them. “ E.li. s,
apology for the Carlton J.P. was that he wasn t
really to blame—he daren’t do more than fine
a brutal ruffian £5 for crippling and almost
murdering a harmless stranger because he is
afraid of being himself “ dealt with ” by the
push. And The Bulletin’s answer was, and is:
The honorary J.P. need not sit on the bench un-
less he likes. He wouldn’t lose any salary by
staying away, nor would he lose his precious
office. If he, being a resident of the locality, and
afraid of the local push, dare not do his duty
he can do one of two things—he can stay away
from the court, or he can come there and crawl
and toady to the local ruffian, and become, to
all intents and purposes, his accomplice.
Well, he chooses to crawd and toady, and he
chooses to be, practically, an accomplice. He
comes to the court gratis, of his own free will,
and, sometimes, to his inconvenience and per-
sonal loss, to let off the local brigand. And
that’s why this paper doesn’t sympathise with
him.

In case of reasonable doubt, The Bulletin
solemnly affirms that this country is Australia,
and the current year progress is 1899.
The present criminal outcrop in Queensland
makes a comparison of the state of crime in the
respective provinces interesting and instructive,
if any such comparison could be arrived at. but
it can’t be done. The motley statistics of unfet.e-
rated Australia collapse on almost every point
when one attempts to draw a useful compari-
son. We can say glibly enough that so many
persons per thousand are charged in the various
provinces, so many convicted, and so on. But
let us use “ larceny ” as a touchstone. Under
Yic. law, J’s.P. may discharge larcenists
“ when, in their opinion, it is inexpedient to
inflict punishment:” In these cases, no convic-
tion is recorded. In N.S. W., even when a lar-
cenist obtains the benefit of the First Offenders
Act, his conviction goesonrecord. Take 1 drunk-
enness.” Vic. law does not permit Justices to
convictfordrunkenness unless disorderly conduct
or some similar offence has been allied with it.
N.S.W. law compels Justices, to convict for
drunkenness simply. So the records again
mislead. Still further, in Vic. the lower Courts
can imprison up to three years ; in W-. 8. W.,
only for six months, thus vitiating records both
of minor and major convictions. In Vic., only
arrests are recorded; in N.S.W., summons
cases are added to arrests. Similar disparities
exist in respect of other crimes and offences,
and the disparities spread over all the pro-
vinces. At present each province tinkers up a
set of returns irrespective of its neighbors, and
no statist, do what he will, can produce a
proper tabular comparison. Under Federation
the Australian statist will be in a position to
compel uniformity, for he will represent all the
States and not oneonly.
The Vic. Anti-Sweating League’s programme
for this year is cordially recommended to the
consideration of similar bodies in other pro-
vinces. Special attention is to be given to
domestic servants, sailors, dressmakers and
clerks. None of these at present come
under the Factories’ Act, though there is
no reason why a dressmaker should not be pro-
tected from slave-driving employers as much as
a tailoress or a white-worker. An inspection of
the League’s books gives a striking insight into
Melb. methods, and shows what whited
sepulchres are some of the big houses and ie
most respectable church-going people. I he
League cites many cases of dressmakers work-
ing 17 and 18 hours per day for 45., of clerks
forced back during the hot weather before
Christmas night, after night, three and four
hours per night, for the paltry remuneration of
Is. tea-money. (In M.L., Bank, Insurance and
Govt, clerks are considered as other manual
workers under the Factories’ Act). The League
does not expect to get 48 hours and the mini-
mum wage conceded all at once, but it recog-
nises that it is to the advantage of a country that
all its workers shall be given the opportunity to
rest and recreate at least one day and a half in
every seven, and it is determined to do its
utmost to obtain first for all workers this one
day per week. Afterwards it will reach out
for extra half-day and shorter hours, and what-
ever else it can get. And if the Trade-Unions
and other workers’ bodies choose to stand by
one another and go unitedly for these things
they can get them.
A Bulletin man again writes about the
Afghan camel drivers at Bourke, who refuse to
continue in the service of the local (Afghan)
carrying company, and are being sentenced to
seven days’ imprisonment with wearisome re-
iteration :
According to local police court reports, the last time
that a batch of the Mahomets was charged “the Bench
drew the attention of the prosecuting lawyer to the fact
that in the agreement which was tendered and signed by
this man there was no term of service mentioned.”
Thereupon a clerk in the company’s service came along
“and said that when the agreement was read to the
accused the term was mentioned.” And the Bench, con-
sisting of members of the Vast Unpaid, seems to have
accepted the clerk’s mere say-so, and to have concluded
that it wasn’t necessary to put so trivial a thing as
the term of service down in the agreement, so long as a
clerk (who was probably not understood) mentioned it at
the time, or afterwards said he mentioned it. _ Anyway,
this particular Mahomet was fined 10s., or in default
seven days’ imprisonment; and, though on the princely
allowance made to the drivers, 10s. was no doubt a mere
circumstance, he, like the rest of them, chose to ‘ take
it out.” Nobody seems to be interested in keeping these
men out of gaol'; for their own part, they seem to regard
it as a place where they will get plenty to eat and won’t
have cans of hot tea thrown at them. But when the
white population whom they are chevying out of a billet
have to keep them, these agreements (with the important
parts left out) ought to be looked into ; and it might not
be amiss either to look into the qualifications of the par-
ticular members of the Vast Unpaid available at Bourke to
handle such cases. Also, while they are looking into
things, the Law officers might do worse than inquire as
to the qualifications of the interpreters. At one far-
western court lately, when similar cases were being taken,
the only man available for the job was an Afghan in the
employ of the co.—a sort of boss over the principal wit-
ness ! Certainly, so far as could be judged (which wasn’t
very far), he did the work well. But at the Bourke hear-
ings the prisoners have ceased to take interest in the pro-
ceedings ; several of them have obviously been unable to
understand the whole business.
Dear Bulletin. —There’s an awful Caledonian
outcry if a Scotch-named person is referred to
as an Englishman, but anyone happening to be
born or reared in this country is branded “ Aus-
tralian” as a matter of course. Melb. Argus
speaks of “ a young Australian musical prodigy,
only 11 years of age,” who has made a sensation
in England with his piano-playing. Name,
Raimund Pechotsch. He may be just as much
Australian, of course, as any little Sin Fat or
Wing Lee who makes a first appearance on
earth under the Southern Cross, but his pedi-
gree is presumably quite foreign to the land of
his birth. There are no Australian pedigrees
-worth mentioning except the aboriginal ones.
The A.N.A. man, begotten, maybe, by an
Irish-American father from a Scottish mother,
who goes to London and announces himself,
with a swelling chest, as a “pure-bred” Aus-
tralian, snorts at the casual cockney supposi-
tion that he ought to be black. Isn’t it an
absurdity for a child of British parents born
out here to insist upon his Australianism whilst
asserting his racial superiority over a son of
Chow parents who was also born out here ? He
must admit that they are equals, or drop the
name Australian, for he and the Cliow-born
native would have equal claims to be incor-
porated with the Australian Race, if there
were such a Race. There isn t, unless you
count the Chinkies and Indian hawkers’ off-
spring in.—E.F.
*
The Tory Argus doesn’t mind turning demo-
cratic for a time if it can thereby give its great
friend, the foreign importer, a lift. Therefore
it is of opinion (now and then) that a legal mini-
mum wage ought to be fixed in every protected
industry. And so it certainly ought, but in
addition there ought also to be a minimum
wage fixed in every Freetrade industry. The
Argus claims that Freetrade makes the coun-
try richer and more prosperous than Pro-
tection, and if so it can afford to pay a living
wage at least as well as Protection can. Why
the Freetrader wants a minimum wage in pro-
tected industries only is perfectly simple.
He quite approves of a 10 or 15 per cent,
tariff where he can fix the minimum wage
so far above the starvation rates paid in other
countries that the importer has a big advantage
after all, for then he can point to the failure of
Protection and fill Flinders Lane with his
whoop of triumph. By all means let there be
a minimum-wage for every man employed in
every protected industry. But let there be
also a minimum for the importer’s clerks and
employes, and the lumpers who haul his bales
ashore, and the sailor-men who bring these
bales out here, and the people who repair the
ships that bring them out, and all the rest. If
Freetrade can’t afford to pay a minimum wage,
what’s the good of talking so much about the
way it enriches the country ?
The rumor that Julian Salomons will be
allowed to re-arrange theN.S.W. Agent-General’s
staff so as to make room for a man with a prac-
tical knowledge of butter and eggs (a shirt-
sleeved A.-G.), gets credence among those who
have an eye on Sydney Smith.
“ Rick ” : The struggle for a crust.
Passed Melbourne city tip between 4 and 5 this
morning. Over 200 persons, men, women, and
children engaged thereon—men raking, women
and children picking up and carrying away bags
of stuff. Handcarts, wheelbarrows, and prams
galore. Passed again at 8.30 a.m. No humans
about, cattle quietly feeding.
Phil May’s Winter Annual (W. Thacker
and Co.) while well furnished with examples of
May’s best work, is also satisfactory from a
literary point of view, and embodies anew feature
in the shape of illustrating the stories Amongst
the contributors are R. H. Sherard, Francis
Gribble, and Julian Ralph ; also, of more special
note to Australians, Louis Beoke and Alex.
Montgomery.
