That hi storical boot-trunk horror, “ For
the Term of His Natural Life,” was fished out of
tli i river of obloquy and spread out on the slab for
identification at Sydney Cri. on Saturday. It is a
shuddery tiling—an old, pickled woe preserved in
gin and with salt on its raw back, and the sight
of it always makes The Bulletin look askance on
its vittles for days afterwards. Yet there lingers
around it a faint suspicion that it will ultimately
develop into a comedy, or a burlesque, or some-
thing cheerful of that sort. The funny convict
who pounds up one cubic inch of road-metal in the
course of the six tableaux, and Meekin, the giddy
sin-shifter in the tall hat and the sunset whiskers,
keep breaking in on its lugubriousness, and there
is hope that in the end they may take possession
of the drama, and crowd out Rufus Dawes and
Gabbett and Troke and Frere, and the great
flogging that is always just about to happen,
though it never really comes off in public. “ His
Natural Life ”is undoubtedly a great story, but
the swish and howl of the old Yandemonian ghosts
is an enduring sorrow all the same, and the public,
looking the vista of GO years at the state of
Rufus Dawes’ back, is sometimes inclined to wish
that that very harrowing spectacle would shift it-
self right over the horizon. All the people con-
nected with that dismal tale are dead now, and
most of them, presumably, in the place where the
long imperishable worm is always on deck ; and
why Dawes should yell his harrowing yell in the
ear of this generation
* * » *
Anyhow, Dam pier’s latest rendering of the
regrettable condition of Dawes’s back is a fine
piece of work pictorially, and rather good in other
respects. There are glimpses of bright sea and
land scenery in between the frequent flogging or
proposed flogging of somebody. Dampier’s Par-
son North is a character of which it is impossible
to grow weary; and Johnson Weir makes a
strong, manly figure as Dawes. The steady
improvement of Johnson has already been com-
mented on in this usually disrespectful paper,
and he promises to be somebody of importance
by and bye. John Forde and George Buller do
very excellent work, and Edward Duggan’s drinky
captain is a highly commendable study of wrath
and pimples. But the feminine element of the
company is distinctly weak. Miss Rose Dampier
is a very graceful statue in a nice, white frock, but
she puis no vigor into the character of Sylvia.
Her Sylvia is a gentle, sweet-voiced ghost who
stands around agreeably among the horrors of old
Vandemonia, and does practically nothing except
stand round agreeably. And Miss Katherine
Russell doesn’t look in the least like the dashing,
handsome, reckless young Sarah Rex—there is no
getting away from the fact that she looks a
motherly, middle-aged Sarah. Edmund Gabbett
Holloway he of the tremendous fog-horn voice—
is a convincing horror, but Gabbett is the most
oppressive horror of the lot, and the writer here
goes out in haste for a drink of something to keep
him up. Anyone who sees “His Natural Life”
right through needs a very considerable quantity
of drmk to keep him up.
** * #
“ Hamlet ” will be repeated at Sydney
Criterion on Friday, Dampier’s last week’s
attempt at giving a small instalment of Shake-
speare having been sufficiently appreciated to
induce him to again turn on the Bard at the
meter. Mr. Dampier’s Dane was very well appre-
ciated on the whole, and the Ophelia of Miss Rose
Dampier was the best thing that young lady has
done in Sydney up to now. There will also be a
“ Hamlet ” matinee on Saturday, and The Bul-
letin has fears that this is running the melan-
choly Dane into the ground, so to speak. It can’t
remember that Sydney ever showed signs of want-
ing a Shakespeare matinee.
** * *
The MacMahon firm pats up a fresh opera
at the Lyceum with the same frequency that it
displayed in its dramatic days—the days when it
lashed out with a fresh hair-raiser every Saturday,
and it was only an exceptional hair-raiser that
saw two consecutive Saturdays. The “ Bohemian
Girl ” filled the void last Saturday, and filled it,
on the whole, very admirably. Some fine mellow
scenery with a gleam of yellow sunlight in it has
been put up for the occasion; and Miss Lilian
Tree, Leumane, Farley, Bert Bailey and other
notable Bohemians pervade the same to good pur-
pose. Farley’s Devilshoof has been a notable suc-
cess any time these last twenty years. Nobody in
Australia ever did that garish, jovial, tawdry,
tuneful blackguard better than Farley—it is doubt-
ful if any other person ever did him nearly so
well. And his Devilshoof is as good to-day as he' was
m the early eighties. Farley has practically had
a monopoly of the character in Sydney so far hack
as the writer can remember, while about a dozen
people have taken Thaddeus in succession, and
the part of Arline has been everybody’s property.
Miss Tree sings Arline very finely, and acts her
respectably, and looks her not at all. But her
singing makes up for the other deficiencies.
Leumane is a good Thaddeus, though not the
best that Sydney has seen; Bert Bailey’s Flores-
tein is good in a reckless, burlesque sort of fashion ;
and Winn, as the Count, is at least respectable.
Taken all round, it is a better version than anyone
has a right to expect at the low Lyceum tariff.
* • * »
Judging by what symptoms there are, the
popularity of the “ Bohemian Girl ” doesn’t
appear to be waning. Anyhow, it appeals to the
world-weary fragment who writes these lines more
than any other of the operas of the pre-Gilbertian
period, and when he has occasionally foregathered
with other fragments, and collected their views on
the subject, lie has generally found the other
fragments of the same opinion. There is a
musical rhythm about the waves that beat on
the sea-coast of Bohemia, and it is a pleasant,
indefinite sort of place where everybody, except
Brunker, has been at some time in his life, with
joyful results as a rule. And the title of the
opera alone does a lot to keep it afloat, for every-
body who has been in Bohemia (except Brunker)
has discovered that there was a girl there—often
more. And the great trouble in the morning has
generally been to remember how on earth he fell
out of Bohemia again, and why on earth the man
who falls out of Bohemia always lands on his hat
in a muddy spot, or to recollect whether he
emerged without the hat and the girl threw it out
of Bohemia after him. Speaking generally, the
writer-is of opinion that the principal export of
Bohemia is squashed hats, and they are practically
all exported between 11 p.m. and 3 a.m. And
the principal imports are young men and their
cash, and liquor,. and golden hair-wash for the
female inhabitants.
** * *
There was great woe on the second night
of the “Bohemian Girl,” by the way, and the
curtain didn’t rise till somewhere near 9 o’clock.
This considerably disorganised the play. It is
understood that some essential people were on
strike, which caused the trouble. At time of
writing (Tuesday) the third night hadn’t yet
eventuated, so any possible joy and chaos attached
thereto must go unreported. At latest advices
the “Bohemian Girl” was advertised to perish
formally this week.
** * *
The pantomime at Her Majesty’s is in
good health, and the faithful mule is well, though
he hasn’t broken out in any new places since he
was last discoursed upon in these columns. The
well-being of that chirpy beast of lading is a
matter of great moment, for at least half the
panto, depends upon him.
** * *
Charles Holloway’s drama at the Royal is
in its fourth week, and it seems good for another
fortnight or so. The Jubilee Singers are also in
their fourth or fifth or sixth week, and are pros-
pering in their harmless African way about as well
as ever. This is doubtless very pleasant for C.
Holloway and the dark Jubilators, but it is
decidedly rough on the man who fills up this
column with more or less despondent ruminations
every week. What he wants is something new to
write about, and when three managements have
been running the same programme for a month,
and the others are putting up such wild novelties
as “His Natural Life,” “Hamlet,” and “Bohe-
mian Girl ” —well, where does he come in ?
** # *
Much the same crowd—mainly Ajax the
Boneless and the popular Peggy Pryde—is in pos-
session at the Tivoli. The Alhambra has changed
hands, a,nd is once more trying to make a living in
the variety business. And another new co. has
taken possession of the Royal Standard (which
has become rather unsettled since the origi-
nal threepenny show left for Maoriland), and
is playing something of a patriotic character
called “Victoria Gross.” Also, when last heard
of, the Opera House had repented in its battered
old age—had solemnly repented above the public-
house, and adjacent to the cheap fruit-shop and
the fish-emporium in dreary Lower King-street—
and someone was running an illustrated Sunday-
service there.
** * *
The rally in aid of the Distressed Actors’
Fund seems likely to lead to an Actors’ Associa-
tion, constructed on a businesslike basis. Last
week a meeting at Melb. Princess’s adopted the
proposal to form a continuous relief-fund by sub-
scription of a penny in the £ from all actors and
actresses receiving a weekly salary—the harmless,
necessary penny to be levied by the treasurer for
each management, and paid into the coffers of the
association. There was a previous proposal to
place the “ mutual-provident ” tax on a somewhat
sliding scale, but this was abandoned in favor of
the 2-5 per cent, rate, which, if eventually adopted
by all the managements in Australia, should go a
good way towards supporting one or more
“Homes” and supplying cash doles to “out”
applicants. Up to now, Melbourne has been the
headquarters of theatrical charity organisation (in
which Geo. Coppin was the first mover), nor is
there any Home elsewhere that The Bulletin
knows of. A question as to decentralisation may
crop up anon. The success of the penny in the
£ scheme—long ago advocated by this paper —
must depend considerably upon the goodwill of
visiting artists, who are not for long in this coun-
try, and can have only a charitable interest in the
welfare of any fund for benefiting impecunious
pro. residents. How does Rickards view the
matter ? Will the Chirgwins and Biondis be wil-
ling to contribute something like 10s. a week dur-
ing six or twelve months’ sojourn here, and what
about a “penny” clause in their agreements?
Given a mutual provident fund in fair working
order, the controlling power, which will presum-
ably be the Firm, should take steps to define the
meaning of the word “actor ” within the purpose of
the fund aforesaid. The purfeshun is haunted by
many able-bodied persons who are mostly out of
an engagement because —as J. C. Williamson tells
them—they can’t act, they only think they can
act. No right - thinking Fund can sympathise
with these incapables, who ought to be earning
money in some field of labor for which they are
adapted. There should surely be a clause in the
rules restricting the Fund’s assistance to actors
who have established a claim on it by frequent
engagements (and consequent penny payments) in
their younger and more prosperous days.
** * #
“ Madame Sans-Gene ” is now proceeding
steadily through a fourth week at Melbourne Bijou,
and that comedy-drama of domestic interest begins
to be talked about in many places. Also it is more
often admired than otherwise, although no two
people ever seem to agree in their summing up of
various points in the performance. Brough’s
Napoleon gets freely blamed for the actor’s man-
nerisms, whereas Mrs. Brough’s equally well-
known mannerisms are accepted as natural to the
name part by the average observer, who doesn’t
come along with any preconceived ideas on the
subject. The part is strong and popular, and Mrs.
B. plays it earnestly. The fact that she was not
born to realise the character of Sans-Gene is
seldom remarked, but everybody knows that the
stagey Napoleon of historical pictures was
quite unlike Bobert Brough. All the same,
the versatile comedian presents a natural
picture of the parvenu Emperor in the play—a
busy, irritable fellow, rather jealous of his young
wife, and much troubled by his two sisters. They
wrangle vociferously in his presence, and he bangs
them across their royal backs with the fire-irons.
Sans-Gene reminds him of the days when she gave
him “tick” for his washing-bill, whereupon he
slaps her bare shoulders amorously, and shows an
intensely human disposition to hug his loyal sub-
ject, without any thought for her being married
to one of his most devoted marshals. The tradi-
tional Nap. was a poseur in a cocked hat. The
real Nap., according to dramatist Sardou, some-
times laid his hat aside and felt like an ordinary
self-made man, who had led a victorious army up
and down Europe, creating new kings by the way.
Assuming that it was possible for this
shrewd, pushing speculator in the military line of
business to have been a Broughlike person, rather
than the prototype of Julius Knight in “ A Royal
Divorce,” anyone can feel satisfied with Brough’s
performance. It has humor befitting the un-
doubtedly ridiculous circumstances of Buonaparte’s
inner life. It presents to the audience a restless
little fat man (who has become an Emperor in a
hurry) trying to make his relatives and general
surroundings look “ respectable, ” in a monarchial
sassiety sense of the word. If a general’s wife
refuses to ape the ways of real lydies, Nap. orders
the general to divorce the old gal and marry some
hereditary royal highness or another. When his
(Nap.’s) own sisters forget the parts they are play-
ing, and' start to slang one another in good old
Corsican style, he whops them with the tongs.
The making of the Napoleon Dynasty had a farci-
cal side to it, and that is the only side where
Brough comes.
#* * #
“ The Geisha ” has reached its fifth week
at the Princess’s, and is doing bigger business
than in the first week, which means that “The
Geisha ” brought a reputation with it from London,
like Wilson Barrett and “ Sign of the Cross ” and
other fleeting booms. The outer uninitiated world
comes to sample the Firm’s show, and finds it a
gorgeous-looking thing for the money, much more
gorgeous than “La Poupee, ” and quite as good an
entertainment in other respects. Therefore the
tuneful tide of foolish ballads and monotonously
similar refrains is unlikely to ebb just yet awhile,
and the beautiful early gloaming effect in the
purple background of the first act will glorify the
name of George Gordon until further notice.
The principals of “The Geisha” co. don’t
appear any stronger as time goes on, bar
Lauri, who has improved his Chow lingo and is
very funny except when he tries to force the joke
beyond its strength. Miss Perry, perhaps, is even
sweeter than ever, but her lack of importance
can’t be‘denied. She’s a dear little Geisha, and
she rhymes nicely with Asia, and she sings daintily
without making a deep impression. It isn’t Miss
Perry’s fault that two of her songs are practically
the same song twice over, and one of the others is
the inane gold-fish narrative. She didn’t write
the lyrics, anyway. William Pauli, as the bari-
tone hero, has unhappily gained confidence of late,
and his new-born skittishness reveals a personal
belief in Pauli which is not in the least justified
by results. Miss Vane, Miss Musgrove, Sydney
Bracy, and others stand where they did, whilst
Howard Vernon is all the better for talking rather
less vociferously.
* * *- *
Fitzgerald Bros’, canvastown in Flinders-
street has taken root for an indefinite period, and
the big tent where piebald Commodore lies down
beside the motley clown, and the elephant and
lion go trike-riding together, is frequently too
small for the population. A new programme at the
Circus turns out to be different from the old pro-
gramme in many human and four-footed particu-
lars ; also, it sets up the uncanny spectacle of a
tiger-and-horse act. The tiger is a more nimble
artist than the lion, and would probably perform
the feat of picking up the gee-gee’s neck with his
teeth if the management didn’t put a cover over
the live horse-meat. On the other hand, the horse
can’t ride a tricycle, so the latest sensation doesn’t
put Lizzie’s trunk out of joint. As an example of
Herr Zapf’s animal-taming powers it is a remark-
able novelty, all the same.
** * *
“New Babylon ” is still happening in four
acts and twelve tableaux at the Royal, and up to
now there has been no mention of Last Nights for
Bland Holt’s hardy revival. The Alexandra panto,
made its final appeal to the metropolis on Satur-
day, and is said to be seeking consolation in
Geelong. At the Opera House, the magnetic per-
sonality of Cora Caselli seems to have struck the
multitude at a cold, unfeeling time. They listen
to her tuneful voice and watch the movements of
her delightful extremities without showing strong
symptoms of gratitude. Now, The Bulletin
feels truly grateful for Miss Cora.

pearance this season, together with acrobats from
i itzgeralds’ pilous, and a number of gentlemen
well-known in theatrical circles or sassiety, or
other branches of commerce. These are to assist
m a free-and-easy item, entitled “A Smoke Night
at the Orpheus Club.”
* * * -*
Signor Ugo Biondi, to The Bulletin from
Christchurch, M.L. :
Dear Sir,— Re an article embodied in “ A Woman’s
Letter” of Dec. 20th, ’9B, re my business and Fregoli’s
being reproduced in cinematographs, I would suggest to
the writer to properly inquire before making through the
columns of your widely-read paper assertions not in con-
formity with facts. I most distinctly and emphatically
deny having copied my business from anybody, nor have
I had a teacher or master. My turn is entirely original,
consisting of sketches written by myself, and impersona-
tions of conductors, of which I am the originator. My
artistic reputation is too well-established to allow of the
question of originality being raised. I have not copied
my business from anybody, but others have copied mine.
*# * m
That angular little Irishman, “ Hoppy ”
Williams, died the other day at Ballarat, where
he had been editing the Star of late. A good
many incompetent mummers will not feel grieved
at the news, for “Hoppy” was theatrical critic
for the Age in the days when Melbourne daily
journals allowed themselves to print the truth, or
a plain suggestion of the truth, concerning stage
shows. No manager controlled the utterance of
Hoppy Williams’s opinion. He asked no favors—
not even the favor of a place to rest his game leg—
and was rather inclined to stand at the back of the
dress-circle, whilst somebody who hadn’t come to
“do ” the notice would loom large in a press seat.
Williams held his own for a long time against the
slanderous attacks of the people he was too honest
to truckle to, but at last he got taken off the job
of notice-writing, and soon afterwards he left the
Age office. The Melb. critics since then have been
mostly young and impressionable, and their notices
in some cases have been influenced for “good ” by
golden-haired actresses —members of the very
company that was appearing at the time —who
escorted the self-satisfied “critic ” to the theatre,
and helped him to occupy the seats reserved for
the paper. There are ever so many means of nob-
bling a critic, and the best means of all is the
winning smile of a “grateful” actress—grateful
just as long as she gets unlimited jam.
At Poverty Point.
Tub Bulletin hears—with “ mingled feelings of pleasure
and regret"—that Johnnie Wallace has received by cable
an offer of a good engagement from George Musgrove in
London. Johnnie Wallace has been something more to Aus-
tralia than a fine stage-manager. He is affectionately
remembered by the older generations of theatre-goers
for his share in the funniest comic-opera scenes that ever
made this country shriek— i.e., the Harwood and Wallace
scenes in “Tambour Major.’*
Bland Holt has secured the services of a new leading
man for “New Babylon." He leads on the prize cow in
the agricultural-show scene.
Juxtaposed amongst the “set-pieces” in Pain’s recent
Sydney fireworks-show were “The Right Hon. G. H.
Reid, P.C.," and “The Tumbling Monkey.” Malice
aforethought, or only “ eternal fitness of thiiigs ”?
It surely cannot be true, as reported, that veteran Fred
Maccabe, first and for a long time best of monologue
entertainers, has “ gone down,” and is finishing miserably
in Ormskirk workhouse. “ Begone, dull care ! ” Mac-
cabe earned in his time heaps of money, always seemed a
careful man, and, a few years ago, was reputedly in very
comfortable circumstances indeed.
Clara Mongredien, a sweet contralto singer formerly
well-known in Melb., was recently married to “Sol”
Solomon, one of the ablest of Dunedin (M.L.) lawyers.
The engagement, it is understood, was contracted many
years ago—when both lawyer and vocalist were still
schooling in Dunedin. The ex-Miss Mongredien, by the
way, is related to the well-known political economist of
that name, and her brunette mother's beauty is still raved
about by Melbourneites of 25 years ago.
The trike which elephant Lizzie rides in Fitzgeralds’
Circus is getting worn out. The Australian quotation
for a new trike is £5O. It seems a lot of money for such
an unpretentious vehicle, but the management can’t very
well wait for prices to come down, for meantime Lizzie
might have a smash-up, and the possibility of that
mountainous female falling whop against the side of the
cage with the lion on top of her is too awful to contem-
plate.
Time was when Maggie Moore could have been irresist-
ible as Madame Sans-GSne.
The agitated young man from the back-blocks (when
he isn’t a sailor from the briny ocean) who leaps upon
the stage and starts to bang the villain for behaving
cruelly to the betrayed girl in a drab dress, made his
app arance at Bendigo the other night. He was seem-
ingly unintoxicated and quite genuine in his indignation.
All the same, The Bulletin will be glad to hear of a
mummer villain administering “ bpans ” to the next
earnest young hero who breaks loose from the auditorium
with a cry of “ Leave the woman alone.” When a person
has deliberately paid his money to be a spectator of a
melodrama he ought to behave as such.
The bright little girl who comes along in the race-scene
in “ New Babylon,” and sells a k’rect card to Bland Holt,
is a cousin of Robert Ingersoll.

The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately called for
competitive designs and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinders
17" and 38" by 60" stroke, and will indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of 151 b. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably the
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, &c., of Melbourne,
Adelaide, and Perth, the sole agent in Australia for J. and
E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride refrigerating and
ice-making machinery, is at present showing a large stock
of these machines, which have been got ready for the
summer season. The cost of refrigerators to produce
6cwt. of ice per day has been reduced to £lOO each.
Special prices are quoted for machines to make up to 50
tons of ice. The patent carbonic anhydride system has
been adopted by the Victorian and other Australian
Governments ; also by all the leading steamship com-
panies, meat companies, dairies, and butchers of the
world.*

COLUMN
Out of the latest issue (£15,500) of 3 per c-ent.
bonds of the Vic. State Land Bank the public took £525
and the Saving's Bank had to take up the balance. Up to
date the State Land Bank has raised £682,225, of which
the Savings Bank has had to take up £540,500. Evidently
something will have to be done to popularise these securi-
ties when the Savings Bank can hold no more of them,
anil one obvious thing is to raise the interest to 3J per
cent. It is the old old story of expecting the local in-
vestor to take a rate of interest which the English investor
(despite the fact that interest is lower in England) scorns
to look at. The cheapest loan which Victoria ever raised
in England cost £3 7s. 4d. per cent., and the best price it
could borrow at in London now is probably about £3 5s. —for
the temporary burst of cheap money which recently
enabled N.S.W., Westralia, and S.A. to raise the wind at
very reduced rates has gone by for the time being. The
cheapest Victorian loan ever raised in Melbourne was at
something under £3 percent. If the Victorian Govern-
ment wants to borrow at ss. per cent, less interest in
Melbourne than it would have to pay in London —well,
the loan doesn’t go off ; that’s all. But why the lowest
rate of interest should always be reserved for the local
investor, Heaven only knows.

Victorian railways, for the second half of ’9B,
show a revenue of £1,459,262 —£71,605 more than for the
corresponding half of 1897.
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co. shows a loss of £4516 for the year ended 30th June,
1898. This is the fourth year since the new reconstructed
Loan and Mercantile got into operation, and the results of
these four years have been as follows :
1895 (15 months) .. Loss £28,393
1896 (9 „ ) .. „ 5,670
1897 (12 „ ) .. „ 2,216
1898 (12 ~ ) .. ~ 4,516
This year, it was confidently hoped that there would be a
profit, as there was £12,542 less interest to be paid than
last year, but the continuous drop in the earnings swal-
lows'up all savings as fast as they are made. The gross
earnings for the four years have been :
1895 (12 months) .. .. £254,514
1896 (9 „ ) .. .. 195,194
1897 (12 „ ) .. .. 231,351
1898 (12 „ ) .. .. 196,611
In one way, too, the situation is even worse than it looks,
for no interest is paid on the Third Lien Debenture Stock,
of which the public holds about £375,000. If that had
been paid the loss would be over £19,000.
The N.Z. Loan and Mercantile is an interesting
study. Since 1893 it has written off as lost £330,000 re-
serve fund, £5649 profit balance, £240,000 capital,
£1,042,142 more capital, and £1,448,325 of the debenture
liabilities. This last item relieves it of an immense
burden of interest, and in addition, as already men-
tioned, it has failed to pay interest on another £375,000 of
its debentures. And on top of all this it show’s a serious
loss. It has now a paid-up capital of only £136,087, of
which £6733 is lost, making the shareholders’ funds in the
business not much over £129,000. And it owes £3,311,363
to its debenture-holders, so that it is working almost
wholly on borrowed money at 4 per cent., and it can’t
earn enough to pay the 4 per cent. There is £1,860,000 of
capital still uncalled, but it is mortgaged for the security
of the Prior Lien Debenture-holders, and can’t be called
up for the ordinary purposes of the business. The visible
way out in such a case would be to call up as much
of this money as can be got in, and pay off the Prior
Lien crowd with it so far as it w’ill go. By a very
peculiar arrangement this crowd, when it is paid off,
is to receive £lO2 10s. for every £lOO (and the other
debenture-holders are to be paid off at £lO5 for every
£100), so the operation would result in a serious
loss ; still it would reduce the tremendous burden of
interest and give the Loan and Mercantile some kind of a
show. Apparently the co. can’t even adopt the useful
expedient of buying up its depreciated securities in the
open market arid extinguishing them, whereby it might
make enough profit to keep itself square. The unlucky
reconstruction scheme provides that all the debentures
shall be redeemed at a premium. And as this premium
must be paid some day, there is an indirect liability which
doesn’t appear in the balance-sheet, and which nearly
equals the paid-up capital.
The Bank of North Queensland looks a little
better this half-year, though even in its improved state
there isn’t very much to boast of as yet. The profits for
the six months ended 31st December, 1898, were £l4BO,
which compare with some previous profits thus
December, 1894.. £1674 June, 1897. ... £2003
June, 1895.. .. 1736 December, 1897.. 1559
December, 1895.. 1182 June, 1898.. .. 587
June, 1896. . .. 1703 December, 1898.. 1480
December, 1896.. 1717
The capital has now' been written down to £lOO,OOO, and
on this reduced amount the profits are equal to £2 19s. 2d.
per cent. Six banks doing business in Australasia showed
worse results than this in their last balance-sheets, and
16 showed better results—still, it is a very distinct im-
provement. The present half-year’s profits, _ added to
previous balance, leaves £2066 to credit of profit and loss,
and the best feature of the case is that the directors pro-
pose to appropriate 2000 of this as the beginning of a new
reserve-fund. This may be taken as evidence that, in the
board’s opinion, the long process of making provision for
losses is definitely finished, and that the Bank of North
Queensland is starting again with a clean sheet. It has
not possessed any reserve since 1894, and it lias written
off, in all, £170,928 of capital and reserves in the past six
years, so the clean-up ought to prove final this time. It
would be a good thing if the management would start
the new era by a resolution to set aside a certain propor-
tion of the profits regularly to strengthen the reserve till
it reaches at least £lOO,OOO, and to invest at least half of
it separately in State securities. The profits ought to
show a steady improvement now that the old incubus has
been extinguished, and when they reach £2500 for the
half-year—enough to pay a 5 per cent, dividend—there
will be a great temptation to pay 5 per cent, dividends
and let the reserve slide. The B.N.Q. shareholders
haven’t seen a dividend for a long while.
One notably good point is that the gross earn-
ings are larger this half-year than they have been since
June 1895, and but for a small increase in working ex-
penses and deposit interest this would be the best half
since then. Deposits have increased by nearly £14,000
during the half-year, and the reserve of cash and State
notes is very substantial, being equal to about 35 per cent,
of all liabilities to the public. Everything considered, the
Bank of North Queensland seems to have a reasonably
prosperous future before it. The present management
can, at least, take credit for having met its difficulties
manfully, and written off lost capital with much vigor.

The Royal Bank of Queensland shows a net
profit of £7579 for the half-year ended 31st Dec., ’9B. The
profits for the last five years have been :
June, 1894 .. £6360 I December, 1896 .. £6515
December, 1894 .. 6477 June, 1897 .. 7463
June, 1895 .. 5946 j December, 1897 .. 7440
December, 1895 .. 6033 I June, 1898 .. 7460
June, 1896 .. 6445 I December, 1898 .. 7579
The Royal of Queensland pays its invariable 2.1- per cent,
div. which, with div. duty, absorbs £5075. Another £2OOO
is added to reserve, making it £41,000, all separately in-
vested in State securities, and all accumulated since the
’93 reconstruction. Deposits show an increase of £42,000
during the half-year. Cash and liquid securities (State
bank-notes and Government stocks) are £299,514 —an in-
crease of £44,000 as compared with the amount six
months ago, and equal to the very liberal figure
of 33 per cent, on all liabilities to the public.
The profits are equal to £3 11s. 3d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds, which is above the
average of at least 12 of the banks doing business in Aus-
tralasia. The conspicuous good points about the Royal
Bank of Queensland are the steady increase in the profits,
the fact that the dividend is kept down to a moderate
figure, the other fact that every half-year sees a solid
addition made to the reserve-fund, and the separate in-
vestment of the reserve in State securities. In the ab-
sence of any State audit, a g’ood deal has to be taken for
granted about the value of every bank’s advances, but on
its balance-sheet the Royal looks sounder than the
average. And it has a large capital for the size of its
business, which leaves a very liberal margin of security
to depositors. In this respect the small bank generally
has an advantage over the big one.

The Haymarket Permanent Land, Building,
and Investment Co. (Sydney) reckons that it has got
through the year ended 15th Nov., ’93, with a profit of
£477—equal to 13s. sd. per cent, per annum on capital,
or 9s. per cent, per annum on shareholders’ funds (capital
and reserve). The net return in ’97 was £452, so there is
at least an improvement of £25. Still, considering that
the Haymarket Co. professes to have a paid-up capital of
£71,030, reserves £34,648, and a profit balance of £5963
total shareholders’ funds, £lll,64l—the profit of £477 is
pretty miserable ; the same funds, invested in 3 per cent.
State securities, would yield £3349. Either the assets are
largely “ book values,” and want writing down by some-
thing like £BO,OOO to bring them to their real present
value, or else the co. has woefully bad luck in the way of
unoccupied land and premises which are yielding no im-
mediate return. The fact that £115,439 is set down as
“ cost of sundry estates and improvements ” seems an
argument in favor of the latter view; £115,000 worth of
“ estate” is apt to be rather unsaleable these times.

The Port Jackson s.s. co. is about to build
another steamer—of the Manly type, but larger than that
smart vessel. Some shareholders growl that ship-building
inevitably means embarrassing the company —but the
men now at the head of affairs know better. The P. J.S.S.
Co.’s trade has developed into a noble thing. On one
recent day the co. bagged about £430 in 6d. fares ; and
last year, in addition to paying off £17,000 overdraft, it
kept a good sum in hand and put its steamers into better
condition than they have been in for years. What a for-
tune was missed by the myopic dodderers who, a few
years ago, held out for Is. 6d. fares and wrecked the com-
pany ! m * *
Broken Hill’s metal output for ’9B was valued
for export at £1,666,502—the lowest for some years. But
the return for the second half was far greater than for the
first— £977,647 against £688,855. Further, most of the
export is now either crude ore or concentrates ; and both
are valued net. So stuff which, if smelted on the mine,
would be declared as of the value of £2OOO is, because it
is reduced at the seaboard, declared to be of the value of,
say, £lOOO. In the same way, though the ’97 export was
set down at under £1,900,000, the Mines Inspector re-
turned the gross value at £3,620,611. Comparisons,
therefore, between recent years, when all the smelting
has been done on the coast, and former years, when most
of it was done on the mines, do not show the relative
value of the mines’ output, or anything like it.
One result of the inadequacy of the official re-
turns of B.H. production (for N.S.W. Mines Dept, takes the
Customs figures, and all others follow the Mines Dept.) is
to make foreign statistics ridiculous. Thus, because no
separation of the lead from the silver has been made in
N.S.W., the province is credited in authoritative English
and American publications with producing a comparative
trifle of lead. It probably doesn’t matter much ; but
seeing that statistics are issued, they might as well give a
reasonably correct impression.

*** ■ *
The report and financial statements of the
Sulphide Corporation, which was formed to take over the
B.H. Central mine and the Ashcroft electrolytic process,
are ancient history when they reach Australia. Those
just out cover the twelve months preceding July last.
But in that period the Ashcroft process had been
tried, found wanting, and discarded; and the value of the
mine with the product treated by the common processes
of concentration, roasting, and smelting, had been deter-
mined. Apart from the huge cost of the Ashcroft works
at Cockle Creek, Newcastle, the co. was £44,779 out of
pocket by running them ; but the profit from concen-
trating and smelting reached £45,117. Nor was the latter
for the full year ; while expenses have since been brought
down from £6 16s. per ton of concentrates to £5 14s. lid.
Also the output is now 50 per cent, greater. The co.’s
capital has been reduced from £1,100,000 to £962,500 by
the simple process of cancelling ss. upon each of the
550,000 ordinary shares, representing the sum “ which
has been lost or is unrepresented by available assets ”;
and the name has been changed from Sulphide Corpora-
tion (Ashcroft’s process) to simple Sulphide Corporation,
But even on the reduced capital there is not more than
7| per cent, per annum in sight.
* =i= « *
Struggle going on between the representatives
of the Exploration Co. and the other directors for the
control of the Sulphide Corporation, for the promotion of
which the Exploration Co. got £75,000. One side says
the Exploration floated the Corporation recklessly so as
to make this huge profit; the other side, that the respon-
sibility involved in the adoption of the Ashcroft process—
its inception, and the experiments to test it, rested with
Messrs. Gibbs and their partners, who played the leading
role in everything that was done ; whereas it was through
the Exploration Co. that the Central mine was secured at
a price much below its intrinsic value. Though seeming
to have the best of the argument, the vote was carried
against the E. Co. Meanwhile, one hears nothing of Ash-
croft, to whom, as the man who had solved the mining
problem of the age, the papers once devoted whole pages.

White Cliffs (N.S.W.) opal output for the year
is estimated at about £llO,OOO. Which puts opal fifth
on the list of N.S.W. mineral products.
Cobar-Chesney (N.S.W.) : Lode driven right
through at the 460 ft. level, and another crosscut going
through it at the 560 ft. Average of the first, slightly
under 4 per cent., which, considering that a large higher-
grade ore body is known to be to the south, is encourag-
ing. Manager reports that the ore at the 560 is the best
yet mined, indicating that the good ore south of the shaft
in the upper workings is dipping towards it. The big
compressing-plant and Lancashire boilers are about to
leave the foundry, and, as soon as the system of reduction
has been determined, the works will go up. Time and
money should make the Chesney-Cobar a big mine ;
especially if copper stays anywhere about TOO a ton.
* * * *
The fall in Cobar Chesney shares is attributable
to a variety of circumstances, but mainly, no doubt, to
the fact that the directors refrained from keeping cer-
tain promises made by them, the non-fulfilment of which
pro nises led to calls on the contributing shares. Also,
there was in the last mining report an absence of the en-
thusiasm anil detail which might have been expected had
the lode at the 560 ft. cross-cut looked quite as well as was
hoped for.

“ Malle- ” : lie Eisler’s Gallymont experience.
An expert visiting a western field was offered an interest
in a big gossan deposit, alleged to go 15dvvt. per ton, for
11030. He went out and was told to sample wherever he
liked— “ The whole hill was the same.” He took samples,
locked them in his bedroom, and went out with one of
the vendors to see the big mine of the locality. In his
absence, someone climbed through his window, removed
the samples, heated them on a forge, and poured purple
of cassius over them ; the heat drove off the acid, and
left the chloride of gold in the ore. The expert wired
later from Sydney : “ Will take interest. ; cheque posted.’
Subsequent trials revealed not a color to the ton. With
memories of the Last Call, Taranganba, Mount Hoaxley,
and countless other things of the sort, it is not too much
to sav that Australia is the unfortunate possessor of the
adroitest and most conscienceless mining-fakirs on the
face of the globe.
The unfortunate Gallymont affair gives yet
another illustration of the oft-exploited Bulletin theory
that Jews are especially unlucky gold-miners. So un-
lucky that they really have no business to dabble in such
matters at all. The Hebrew’s proper gold-mine is in the
pocket of the Gentile, and he should recognise the fact
and lay strictly to his book. Fools build houses for wise
men to live in. Gentiles sink shafts to get gold for the
Jews. This paper isn’t arguing from the text that Gally-
mont was managed by Eissler, who is of the Chosen, for
E.ssler is a man of almost unique experience and widely-
recognised ability, who has managed many successful
gold-mining ventures —it is only superstitious enough to
believe that had Eissler been managing for John Smith
instead of for the unhappy Marks crowd he would not
have had such awful luck.

The mingled snort and howl which greeted the
Lake View Consols’ Christmas div. of a beggarly ss. per
share when a lordly guinea was expected, was probably
heard by Andree at the Norih Pole. The previous
div. was in May last ; and, since then, calculations that
couldn’t possibly be wrong have been made month by
month to show that another 10s. could have been paid in
September and another 10s. in December. The 11 months’
work resulted in 92,3210 z. gold being won from 61,418
tons stone ; value, about £390,000, at least. Working ex-
penses are estimated at £125,000. The co. has, however,
erected much machinery'; and, previously, the directors
had shown a disposition to build up a substantial reserve.
How they invest this latter is a matter of great import-
ance ; their powers under the articles are .very wide,
whereupon the rumor, circulated with a sinister signifi-
cance, that they have acquired large interests quite apart
from Westralian mining—a very proper thing, too, of
course, if it is to be a reserve-fund worth having. The
curious thing is that the shareholders of not one mining
co. in a dozen insist on knowing how the reserve is in-
vested.
*** . . *
Just a score of Westralian mining companies on
the div.-list. But Westralia looms large in both output and
divs., through the bunching of all the fields. Both t’other-
siders and J. Bull get the whole province’s figures lumped,
whereas, in Queensland, the Towers, Gympie, Mt. Morgan,
Croydon, and the rest each does its little screech. Same
in Victoria with Bendigo, Ballarat, Gippsland, and the
others. And the Westralians never give you a balance-
sheet, setting out calls and draws on capital as well as
divs.

Hannan’s Brown Hill : 3059 tons, 70520 z., and
a div. of 7s. 6d. a share declared—equal to £41,250. Aus-
tralasia has never seen a group of mines equal to those
of Hannan’s, though the Brown Hill is outside the very
rich cluster, and is surrounded by some of the poorest on
the field. Long before it became a producer shares
reached £8 ; now it is producing at the rate of 7000 oz. a
month they are under £6. Bottom of mine the trouble.
Pettigrew, who was actively connected with it before
flotation, declares that the richness of the schistose
formations on the surface was discovered on the Brown
Hill before it was known on the Great Boulder or else-
where.
* * * *
A gold discovery made 400 miles out from Cool-
o-ardie is headed by the Argus, “ Great News of a
Wonderful Show.” A prospecting expedition, equipped
by O. F. W. Palmer, and headed by his son, started for
the Peterman Ranges some months ago, and the old man
wires to a friend in Melb. : “ Great news from son.” Tune
was when Coolgardie would have started out en masse,
but now its denizens “ are awaiting results.” Palmer,
the elder, in his day a fine fellow and a good sport, was
once a Vic. western district squatter, but having been
badly hit, made for Coolgardie in the early days and
started a pub. at Bulla Bullin, the last coaching stage en
route to that desert town. He and Ernie Brodribb,
another reckless man with a big heart, backed a horse to
win them £lO,OOO one day at Flemington. Palmer (who,
by the way, bears a perfectly marvellous physical resem-
blance to Capt. Semmes, the renowned Alabama captain)
incidentally mentioned that he would ask Brodribb to be
good enough to jump on his hat if they won. The moke
got home by the barest head—a circumstance so unusual
with E.B. that in his suppressed excitement he forgot his
part of the contract. Not so Palmer, who, on the grand-
stand lawn, threw his belltopper on the grass and bawled :
“ Ernie, jump on it !” many times, and as that sport re-
fused the jump he jumped on the tile many times him-
self, and then walked off hatless but satisfied. Brodribb,
having got through £lOO,OOO or more in about Aus-
tralian record time, also went to Coolgardie. For months
Mt. Burgess was the head prospecting station, and often,
after a remarkably quiet meal of tinned meat, preserved
spuds, and a raw onion for dessert, Brodribb would re-
mark to his mate, “ How I would like to make a few
quid.” But they did not get much gold—they got Barcoo
rot instead. Writer wishes Palmer good luck in this
find. Brodribb is somewhere or other on the con-
tinent. Since he dropped his money there doesn’t appear
to be quite the same interest taken in his whereabouts.
* » * F
The reduction-plants of the Mt. Monger and
Haycraft co’s., Kalgoorlie, sold by auction to the Lake
View Consols. Great things were said of the Haycraft
process when it was going up—not a word now it is com-
ing down. *
A start has been made with the Konemann pro-
cess on the Great Boulder tailings. One or two break-
downs occurred, but these were soon remedied, and the
plant is said to be running smoothly. A wash-up is pro-
mised some time in Feb. It will be watched and no mis-
take. Well-informed people predict a lower extraction at
a higher cost than the common or garden cyanide-process,
and a warm time for all responsible for its introduction ;
while manager Hamilton nails his flag' to the Konemann
mast, predicting complete success and great economy.
So no less a personage than D. L. Doolette
thinks it " about time that the old idea re the Boulder
being- a swindle from the jump was finally scotched ”
adding, as proof, that 16oz. was dollied in a fortnight
from the cap of thd lode, and that he had seen as good
stone in the lode at the surface as he had seen in it since.
Not so fast, Mr. Doolette. The Boulder has not been
“regarded as a swindle from the jump.’’ The general
idea is that it was a “ wild-cat” when floated in London,
and for a considerable time afterwards, and the finding of
unexpected lodes and gold has gone far to “ scotch ” that
idea, but not to kill it. If the lodes were known of and
their richness proved, how was it that shares were sold in
London, as well as on the field, at less than 25., which, on
the present number in the co., would be equal to about
21d. ? Dollied 16oz. in two weeks —why, the Londonderry
owners dollied upwards of 5000 oz. in a few days. That
mine was not a swindle, hut who is prepared to say it was
not a “ cat” to float for, say, £700,000 in London before
the slightest attempt was made to prove whether this
phenomenal pocket was like every other yet found in the
world—short-lived ? A few men connected with the deal
—mining amateurs such as Lord Fingall, for instance —
no doubt thought that there were tons of gold there, and
that it would go down, but the general scheme was to
take the public down ! No, Mr. Doolette, the Boulder,
the writer affirms, was as wild a feline as hundreds of
others in Westralia, and though the sin of its flotation
has been long redeemed by its gold—“nothing succeeds
like success ” —the fact can never be forgotten by those in
the know.
A Rockhampton syndicate is reported (by its
members) to have a big thing in copper at Moonmera, on
the Mt. Morgan line. London capital has been secured,
and the syndicates smile discreetly when questioned as
to the future.
* * * »
Spurred by local cyanide successes, Charters
Towers syndicates have been scouring the country after
tailings’ areas. A strong contingent swarmed round Mt.
Morgan and pegged rights all along the Dee, but has re-
tired defeated by the mundic tailings.
At Opalton (Q.) two miners named bhillington
and Greenwood, who had just succeeded to a claim pre-
viously worked by two Germans who had done well, are
said to have found one of the biggest blocks of opal ever
discovered —lift, long and in places as thick as a man’s
thigh. When broken four men carried it to the main field.
The value of the piece is estimated at between £7OOO and
£lO,OOO. Mr. Clancy, a southern opal buyer, states that
so great is the quantity of opal on the field that he will be
unable to purchase it all. Clancy on the opal overflow.
William TJsher, of Mt. Usher fame, is said to
hold another trump in the shape of a find near Rock-
hampton. Secrecy is to be preserved till London has
been consulted, for which purpose Usher goes ’Ome
shortly. The curse of Australian mining is this yearning
to go ’Ome and consult the bulls and bears in London,
who only mine in each other’s pockets and in the pockets
of the public.

As Dowell is about, Vic. may consider herself lucky in

has alleviated the sufferings of these poor brutes it has
already justified its existence.

The Australian Star in a late issue :
. . . the N.S.W. Legislature introduced the doctrine of
equal rights by an Act which allowed a wife whose
husband was domiciled in this province at the time the
suit was instituted to petition for divorce on the single
ground of his adultery.
The Star’s reading of the Act is supported neither
by the Act itself nor the decisions thereon.
As a matter of fact the N.S.W. divorce law winks
THE WORKINGS OF THE SPIRIT.
MISSIONARY : “ Now, my dear brother, as a test of the sincerity of your
repentance—can you lay your hand upon your heart and say
truly that you love your enemies? ”
PENITENT : “ Yes, sah, if properly cooked .”
at polygamy but frowns at polyandry; the
husband is allowed more latitude than the wife
(no doubt on the ground that whereas a man can’t
deceive a woman as to whether a child is hers or
not, it is easy for the woman to deceive her hus-
band as to who is her child’s father). In N.S.W.
a wife cannot get a divorce on the single ground of
her husband’s adultery (she must prove domicile
as well)—or desertion, or habitual cruelty, or
drunkenness, or some other of the matrimonial
offences enumerated in the statute. If, therefore,
a husband not having the animus revertendi, move
his domicile outside the jurisdiction, and there
commit adultery, the wife cannot obtain a divorce
unless she prove his domicile in N.S.W. at the in-
stitution of the suit. In a case where the husband
is petitioner the law does not recognise a corres-
ponding privilege on the part of the wife and will
grant him a divorce on the ground of adultery
alone. Temperance for men, total abstinence for
women, and no such thing as “ equal rights.”
* * * *
The tale of'» Westralian legal row :
For months there has been trouble at Perth over the
publication of a daily writ-list. The matter was men-
tioned in Chambers to the Chief-Justice, who told coun-
sel to mention it in Full Court. Mentioned accordingly,
a mere acting-justice, the sportive James, being with the
permanent, knighted Chief, the saintly Onslow. The
Chief gave a direction. Party appealed, and the Crown
Law Office relied upon the direction of the Full Court!
But, when the appeal came on, Judge Hensman and
acting-J. James were sitting. “Direction of the Full
Court ” was mentioned. “ ’Souse me,” said ’sonah James.
And then he explained that, though he had been on the
bench, sitting as a Full Court, with ’sonah the Chief-Jus-
tice, ’sonah the Chief-Justice hadn’t even turned to him.
Then, as the mere acting-J. was proceeding further, the
Crown Law Office promptly took its foot off the scandal’s
tail, and begged to amend the statement on which the
affidavit was made by substituting “ direction of Chief-
Justice.” Which was done, amidst Judge Hensman’s
punctuating remarks of “ Strauge, strange ! ” Then Pro-
priety breathed a long sigh of relief—and business pro-
ceeded. The Law is not only an ass, but a most expen-
sive and pompous ass into the bargain. The question in
Perth was whether the publication of a daily-writ list was
permissible. The C. J., relying on English practice (he is
knighted) said No ; and that was accepted by the Law
Office as a direction, and acted on. The publisher ap-
pealed. Judges sat and were divided ; the argument
must be repeated before a Full Bench. And so, the C.J.
(whose direction had been adopted) was called in next
day, and the Court went through the solemn farce of
listening to the arguments over again. Of course, the
O. J. sided with the Law Office. The dissentient, Hens-
man, had Judge Holroyd with him. But, then, Holroyd
is a Victorian.

Scientist O’Connor, who took to England
the Queensland ceratodus, has, by way of com-
pensation, rewarded Bananaland for its gift by
bringing back the edible frog—with intent to
pursue the culture of aforesaid amiable batrachian.
According to O’Connor, West End clubs and high-
class restaurants in London use great quantities
of these frogs, so that Bull is no longer able to
hurl the reproach of “Frog-eater” without in-
curring Crapaud’s “ Tu quoque.”

It is expected that the notorious Harrold
Bros.’insolvency case (S.A.), over which Premier
Kingston has, in his Q.C. capacity, been worrying
himself for the last two years, will be ended this
month. In his final address on behalf of the
insolvent Arthur Harrold (ex-president of the
National Defence League), Kingston wept copi-
ously, although there is no jury to melt into pity
on behalf of his client..

One Australian Governor and his crowd
have earned such a reputation for sickening
liberality, and so on, that no one would agree to
accommodate them on their more or less recent
visit to a certain country town ; and they would
probably have camped out (“such a delightfully
new experience, and so cheap, don’t ye know”)
had a tourist - agency not guaranteed ex-
penses. Country people now treat that Govt.
House party in same way as other travelling
troupes, and demand payment in advance.

A Sydney magistrate’s vitriolic comments
on the prurient curiosity that attracts presumably
decent women to listen to the details of indecent-
assault cases recalls the story of a Judge who,
during a spicy hearing, smiled” blandly round his
courtful of scented, feather-nodding femininity,
and remarked : “ I must ask all respectable women
to leave the court.” There was no response, and
for a full minute there was dead silence. “Or-
derly ! ” thundered his Honor, “ turn out the rest.”

“Jep”:iie the laxity of the Gattonians
in assisting the police over the recent horror,
Gatton lay awake o’ nights wondering whether its
son or cousin or brother was implicated in the
crime, and got up in the morning convinced that
its particular son or cousin or brother was bad
enough for anything. Hence the remissness and
apathy.
* » * *
When the girl Dubberke, or Jamieson, of
Yarra boot-trunk horror, states that in turning
Queen’s evidence she is in no way actuated by a
desire to get the (,£550) Govt, reward, she ex-
presses, sincerely or not, a largely-entertained
feeling that a participator in a crime who after-
wards informs should not be allowed to earn
money by his information. The old Roman law
never treated an approver as an ordinary witness.
It distinctly laid down that no criminal was to be
put to death on the evidence of a self-confessed
fellow-criminal, and reduced to a maxim the
opinion that no person should stand to benefit both
by the perpetration of a crime in company and
later on by informing against his associates for a
sum of money.

Grim unintentional humor of recent S.M.
Herald. Half-column “Gatton tragedy,” and
half-column “ Another Queensland tragedy,” fol-
lowed by another half-column gush over New
Guinea missions. What about Gatton as a field
for the Lord’s laborers 1
* * * *
Queensland’s Gatton promises to become
as notorious in Australian history as another Gat-
ton was in English. Before the Reform Act
pocket-boroughs were common. The Lord of the
Manor nominated the Parliamentary candidate,
and in he went, the few voters taking it for
granted that the Lord was right. Gatton was
a mere geographical term. It didn’t own a
voter of any kind, yet the peer who carried the
term in his pocket sent up a M.P. for Gatton at
every general election until the Reform Bill
knocked him out.
* 4« 4« *
Sydney made a weak effort to get up a
local horror last week, the Gatton murder and the
Yarra boot-box having put the other provinces
distinctly ahead. Its trouble concerned a nice
girl who went out to answer an advt. —short
hours, good wages, not much to do, and other
attractions which looked too good to be real—and
didn’t return. Inquiry proved the address to be
bogus, and the inference was that girls were
abducted as they turned the corner of the street
and led away to be dissected, and that their hair
was afterwards sold. But the alleged missing
girl turned up before the dreadful item had time
to take root, and the whole thing fizzled out
quietly.

Again, the heads of the victims are reported to have
been smashed with a “ billet of wood.” If this means a
bludgeon, recently cut for the purpose, the manner of
cutting might be a clue, as most men cut wood in
a style as distinctly their own as they write or talk. No
description seems to have been given of this “ billet"
that played so prominent a part in the murders. And
here are' two other circumstances tending to strengthen
the opinion that the deed was the work of those who, if
not actually resident, knew the place well.
Ist. Despite the number of police and others now in this
locality, the residents are so terrified that they leave their
homes as night comes on for those of their neighbors;
this points to a suspicion—perhaps even to a knowledge—
that the murderers are still at hand and likely to attack
others to whom they may owe a grudge. The other
point is that the bereaved mother, over the bodies of her
murdered and outraged children, should have prayed for
the souls of the guilty ones. Either Mrs. Murphy is an
absolute earthly saint, or she had in her mind, as the
criminals, certain persons known to her. The natural
first, feeling in the circumstances —especially with the
emotional Irish blood—would be an intense desire for re-
venge upon the culprits.
Finally, the absence of any attempt to conceal the
bodies was probably due either to the danger of acquiring
tell-tale stains, to lack of time, or to a feeling of vindic-
tive contempt.

The sudden and unexpected solution of the
hoot-box mystery anguished many a Melbourne
literary bosom. The prayer 3 of the scribes were
for a month’s postponement; but the secret is out,
and a dozen half-completed shockers go into the
dust-bin. The litterateurs were writing against
time to provide a story of the mystery in book-
form while the sensation lasted, but now the real
denouement has chanced along the servant-girl and
the young shopman would not give sixpence for a
cartload of fictional elucidations.
• * * *
Clairvoyants, palmists, and their kin are
allowed to flourish brazenly in Sydney because of
the assistance many of the crowd give the detec-
tives in the search for malefactors. Malefactors’
women-folk are nearly all superstitious, and can
be drawn easily by a skilful futurity-monger.
Then the “ d.” gets “ the office,” and a “ clever ”
capture ensues.
• * * •
Nothing has ever been published in a
“ family paper ” to equal the hideous process-
blocks of the head of Mabel Ambrose, the victim
of the boot-box tragedy, printed by Melb. Punch.
The head was evidently photo’d after being lifted
out of the spirits in which it was preserved and
stood on a board.
• * • •
It is a remarkable circumstance how,
through all the rush of distracted parents to
report that Sairey Ann was missing, and to see if
she was the lady in the Yarra boot-box, the real
parent stayed at home and placidly scrubbed her
floor, and whitened her front step, and fired the cat
out at night, and suspected nothing. The boot-
box tragedy was roaring past her door for nearly
a month and it never attracted her attention.
Her daughter had stepped out about a month
previously “ to look for a place ” and didn’t come
back ; but this attracted no particular attention.
The police even lighted on the old lady’s weather-
board cottage and wanted to drag her out to look
at the body, the local bobby having a suspicion,
but she was almost the only woman in Melbourne
who took no interest in the body whatsoever. Her
daughter had such a habit of stepping out forhalf-
an-hour or a few weeks that it might have rained
boot-trunk mysteries without arousing any great
suspicion in the old lady’s mind.
* * * *
‘ ‘ There was again a large attendance at
the Morgue yesterday, numbering over 1500
people. The total number of callers since the dis-
covery of the woman’s body on 17th December
has been over 8000. Public interest in the crime
which has become known as the Boot-Trunk
Tragedy remains unabated.” — Melb. paper.
Young man Tod, the hero of Melb.’s Boot
Trunk Tragedy, can advance one good plea in
palliation of his giddiness, anyhow. He was a
house-agent. It is hard for a house-agent to be
strictly virtuous. The temptations are so numer-
ous, and so pretty in many cases, and the oppor-
tunities that offer themselves to a mid-day caller
in Tod’s line of business are exceptionally diversi-
fied. There’s the lady who pays her rent with an
engaging smile ; and the lady who is fascinatingly
backward in paying it; and the lady who can’t
pay it at all, but would like to be put into another
house where the agent will not press her—
in a financial sense. Sometimes the agent
steps naturally into the position of friend
and benefactor; more often he is cajoled into
it; not infrequently he finds himself meeting
circumstances half-way —so to speak. The
“ gay ’’-housekeeper comes to him and he goes to
the “gay ’’-house. This is not a danger that crops
up at long intervals, as with the fire-insurance in-
spector. Rent-collection is a weekly occurrence.
To the ladies who “call about a house” at the
office there is no end. In an ordinary way of
business, Tod first called at the three-roomed
cottage of the woman whose young daughter sub-
sequently provided material for the Boot-Trunk
Tragedy. It was as a house-agent, also, that he
became friendly with the other parties in the case.
The Bulletin now exhorts young men in general
to join it in a prayer for deliverance from house-
agency.
* * * *
Emily May, charged at Flemington (Melb.)
Court with the theft of a fowl, begged for leni-
ent treatment, on the ground that she had stolen
the bird for her wedding-breakfast, due next day.
And then the bride-elect, being fined 405., begged
for three months to pay the money in. The world
will laugh—but how tragical is poverty !

THE JEWEL OF ASIA.—(FROM “ THE
GEISHA.”)
It did not occur in one of the Solomon
Islands, but took place in Chalmers Church,
Sydney. A thousand unemployed had met there
to hear Rev. G. D. Buchanan deliver to them a
message. The D.T. report says: “ Then, in a
few simple words, Mr. Buchanan explained to his
unfortunate hearers the meaning of the Lord’s
Prayer.”
That ancient Melbourne land-mark—the
gum-tree which over-looked the Elizabeth-street
side of Sti Francis’ Church enclosure and which
was cut down a couple of years ago—is now being
made into an altar-cbair for said church. The
operator says the heart of the tree is as hard as
stone, but there’s nothing wonderful in that. It
was Melbourne’s chief place of assignation right
up from the time that Little Lonsdale-street got a
reputation until its removal from the hardly less
smellful thoroughfare. What “facers ” that chair
could give to some of the worshippers could it
only speak!

profoundly than
usual. The mur-

something
like system-
atic cheek,
and, more
wonderful
still, it has been
decided to pro-

vers of dilapidated
cabs. Attention might
at the same time be given to the extraordinary
fact that in a large number of cabs the windows
won’t open—whioh means Sheol for the occupant
on a Sydney summer day. In such a climate
every cab should be instantly openable at back,
front and sides.

‘ ‘ Morgue parties ” are the latest Melb
fashion. “Wading bees” and water picnics
have been tacitly abandoned in favor of the
attractions of the Severed Head at the morgue.
Since the beginning of the year every day has seen
increased “business Saturday week culminating
in a record of 1025—exclusive of children, though
nearly every housefather dragged into the ghastly
place a row of gaping youngsters who played
“ tig” round the marble slabs while their parents
picnicked on the morgue lawn.

“ J. J. Mac” :—•
Another version of that Manly (Boxing Day) incident.
It seems that the young woman who tried to rush to
Neptune’s arms was about 24, not 14 ; and that the elderly
man who first jumped to her assistance was no swimmer.
Both would have been drowned had not a young man
immediately followed the ancient and kept them both
afloat, until eventually the three were hauled on to the
steamer. I believe this is the true story—so what has
“Face” —who talks so big about the absence of chivalry
in Australian men —to say to the elderly man’s action ?
And what was “Face” doing that he didn’t dive in
himself?
* * * *
“ C.S.” turns things upside-down-—which
is often a very excellent thing to do, though he is
apparently wrong this time :
Apropos the three Gatton victims, strange that no
one has suggested that a jealous woman or two
may have been implicated in this hideous affair. Two
females might easily have asked for a lift in the
trap, no suspicions would have been aroused —then a
bullet and two heavj* blows on the girls’ heads. Struggle
with girls—do not forget the scratched faces and limbs ;
and then the mutilation. As for the “ outraging ” : con-
sidering the seemingly perfunctory way in which the
doctors diagnosed Murphy’s injuries and overlooked the
bullet ; and seeing how the bodies of the girls were
hacked, the question of “ outrage ” in the sexual sense is
—well, questionable. As no male ravishers and mur-
derers have been found up to date of writing, perhaps
the detectives might do worse than hunt for clues of
“ lady ” furies.

“ Constance Clyde” re a Royal hausfrau :
Once more a daily paper informs us that the Crown
Princess of Something or Other has baked delicious.cakes
for her Royal grandpapa, who has been graciously
pleased to approve of her efforts in that direction.
Strange how feminine virtue seems bound up in the
A LESSON IN KISSING.—(FROM “ THE
GEISHA,” AT ME LB. PRINCESS’S.)
work-box and the baking-board quite irrespective of their
utility under the circumstances. Same in literary life.
No penman is expected to apologise for his brains by
patching his own trousers, but unless a literary woman
consents to waste half her time in doing what any com-
mon garden woman could do, and do better and with
more pleasure, she isn’t “ womanly.” As a matter of
fact, the penlady who knows even the meaning of needle
and thread deserves to be hounded out of decent society.
The woman who wraps her talent in a napkin won’t
escape hell-fire because she has embroidered said napkin
with the latest stitch in moutmelluc work.
A BALLAD OF MELBOURNE MORGUE.
Do come an’ see the ’Ead
Of the donah who is dead ;
It lies upon a marble slab
In a buildin’, you’re aware,
By the Yarra over there —
Suppose we take a four-wheel cab ;
For the cab can carry eight,
And will land us at the gate
Of the villa where the gal’s on view ;
Though we can’t say who she is
From the photos, of her phiz.,
Thank Gawd 1 she wasn’t me or you I
Come along and ’ave a treat —
Bet your life she’s clean an’ sweet,
They say they’ve ’ad ’er soaked in gin
For a pretty longish while,
Which explains ’er ’appy smile —
Hurry up / or else we shan’t git in.
It’s the rummest bloomin’ go
In a free-an’-easy show
As yer ever ’eard abowt, or read—
Don’t purtend ’t’ud make yer sick :
Now, then, Lisa, dear, look quick—
Cabby, drive us to the same old ’Ead.
In Chalmers Church but lately
They sat, with stomachs void,
And pipes and pockets empty,
The Heathen Unemployed.
The prayer unto the Father,
That recondite strange prayer,
The Reverend Buchanan
Explained it fully there.
They felt, those hapless Heathen,
Their hearts within them stirred,
To hear his explanation
Of each exotic word.
Alas, alas, the pity 1
Alas, and woe is me,
That in this Christian city
So many pagans be !
Yet, from the Church of Chalmers,
They went forth overjoyed
To know God was their Father,
Those Heathen Unemployed.
