Drapers Everywhere.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
» , r Sydney, January 16, 1899.
My Dear Myee—
Just now Society imagines it is having a splendid
time m the country. The summer game of the
season is chasing grasshoppers. This was insti-
tuted at their Sutton Forest retreat by the Vice-
regal maidens, whose love of outdoor sport has
always been marked. Moreover, Bishop “Bob ”
Smith has just been visiting at Hillcrest, and
something moderate in the way of revelry was
probably necessary. Then, too, every one has his
• own idea of what is jolly. A small boy away in
the country for his Xmas holidays, an only son
wrote home lately to his mother, “Dear Mother’
I am having a splendid time up here, we sight
about three snakes a day in our garden. ” How
jolly for the nervous mother of an only hopeful!
N.SAV. lancers start for Fngland in
March—our Guards, don’t you know. In the in-
terests of pure Australian swaggah, let us hope
they are all sound fruit, with no taint of, say
socks or vulgar trade among them. Years ago
there was a good-looking fellow who wasn’t
allowed to go and like a soldier fall because the
othahs objected to falling’ with a man who sold
neckties, bai jove. But isn’t sixty an awkwardly
big number to get together if the sell-’em restriction
is to be severely enforced all through the regi-
ment ?
Sydney loses a veteran by the death of Colonel
Rowe. The deceased gentleman was one of Aus-
tralia’s best-known architects, and as he designed
Sydney Y.M C. A. Hall, the Synagogue, ° the
beautiful Walker Hospital, Newington College
and many other fine buildings, he is a man of
many monuments. His military position was
evidently dear to him, for one of' his last wishes
was for a military funeral. This wish was carried
out last Sunday, when the biggest funeral since
the late Gov. Duffs thronged the streets for over
a mile on the way to Waverley Cemetery.
This week, and for several weeks to come what
is left of Sydney world is spending its time at
bargain-sales, buying up everything it doesn’t
want. For the sake of those who find themselves
stranded on acres of small draperies I am going to
give you three ideas for converting remnants into
useful investments. The first is a smart walking
dress, made of a shortish length of dark-blue or
green linen. The skirt is to be cut in two deep
flounces, each falling flat over the other, ed<>-ed
with tiny black satin ribbon worked in a scroll
pattern. The bodice is wrought in black glace
silk, tucked all over, the sleeves tucked and very
tight-fitting, and is brought into harmony with
the skirt by a little pretence at a bolero in
green or blue linen creeping out from
under the arms, and caught lightly across
the chest with one jewelled button. The black
satin scroll pattern also runs round the edges of
the bolero, and the waist-belt must be of black
velvet, fastening with a turquoise clasp. The
Princess of Roumania lately sported a gown like
this, and was voted particularly smart, even by
Mrs. Aria, who is becoming more and more auto-
cratic since she started the World of Dress
magazine.
My second suggestion will suit anyone who
combines some black lace with the desire to be
particularly toney. It imitates the gown in which
the Princess of Wales fancies herself most; a
simple little black lace frock with a four-flounced
bkirt. Ihe bodice is trimmed with rather narrower
flounces falling down from a transparent lace or
jewelled yoke. The inevitable black velvet neck-
band, of course, finishes off everything, as H.R.H.
is never seen without it.
The third remnant might be some white-spotted
not and a length of white glace silk. This will
turn into the prettiest theatre blouse imaginable
if the net is made up over the silk, and the sleeves
are allowed to be full bishops ending in tiny cuffs.
Everyone is rushing into full sleeves now for lace
or net bodices. And everyone is trying to give up
tying her veil under the chin and accustoming her-
self to letting it rest on the under-lip without
biting it. For the rest, fashions are halting, and
the only really loud announcement is that the
streets are still to be cleaned with skirts.
K. S. Ranjitsinhji certainly has an understudy in
Australia, and that personage is undoubtedly
Clem. Hill. The attitude and gestures of Hill
when in the field recall vividly those of the Indian,
while his sensational, but uneven, performances at
the wicket carry the resemblance still further, to
say nothing of the increased excitement in the
ladies’ reserve when he emerges from the pavilion.
The German National Fete came off at Cabarita,
last Saturday, quite successfully. All the
manns” and “hoffs” of the Sydney-German
circle steamed up the river to music, and the
festival was strewn with speeches, and sports, and
much national German song, and many tender
allusions to the Vaterland. The fete was by way
of commemorating the foundation of the German
Empire, and was particularly well patronised by
homesick Germans from various steamers just now
in port.
‘ ‘ Hamlet, ” warranted by Baconspeare’s admiring
friends to resist the ravages of the worst actor
that ever lived, was played by Alfred Dampier
for one night last week, and went so well that a
repetition is to occur at the same place next
t riday. Everybody knows Dampier’s Hamlet—a
gentleman in black, just a little inclined to be
underdone, but beautifully- different from this
city s last attack of melancholy Dane. This play
used to give Wilson Barrett his biggest chance to
ue a nuisance, you remember. First, there was
the sudden strut backward that led you to believe
he was going, then the highly dramatic pause fol-
lowed a long time after by a renewal of the strut.
By then he would be at the curtain, and had every
opportunity to part the hangings and walk through
out of sight. But just there began the play of
\V llson Barrett (no connection with Shakespeare),
-ineie was the slap at his chest; the windy gurgle •
a sham step backward that brought his counten-
ance into a brighter light; a series of dry laughs ;
a long, agonised glance all about nothing, and
finally, one great, spread-out pose with the eyes to
trie pea-nut heaven, and the passing of Barrett was
accomplished after long grief and pain. To go out
°f oi 6 l -00111 like a crab in a fit is a common disease
hakespenan actors, from which, I hasten to
add, JJampier does not suffer.
Who says the gods don’t like Shakespeare ? The
upper circle of the Criterion on Friday overhung
everywhere with vociferously applauding gods. Of
course there are gods and gods, but the two sec-
tions were represented here—the genuine article in
gaudy costume of no collar and a fringe,
and the spurious imitation that goes up there to
improve its mind on classic English at the lowest
possible rate. A large d.c. also gathered together
intelligently, and gazed between intellectual graft
at a pretty young actress eating chocolate out of
the same, bag as her well-known theatrical manager.
Ihe circle evidently felt the desecration of bring-
mggew-gaws i nto the presence of the immortal
Jiill there seems to be some strange sentiment
abroad that you dress for Shakespeare as vou
would for one of the Y.M.C.A. festivals, and
everyone was clothed solemnly up to their ears
and down to their knuckles.
Miss Rose Dampier’s Ophelia turned out to be
a nineteenth-century maid in a not-too-lovely gown
ot old-gold shot with maroon, and lifted to reveal
a maroon petticoat. Her hair was dressed large
and wide, and reminded me of the Coogee trams
in an entirely irrelevant way. A few sartorial
blemishes marked her impersonation—otherwise a
painstaking piece of work—such as the florist-like
arrangement of various bouquets that draped her
corsage in the mad scene, and the unmoved car-
mine in her cheeks, and extreme dryness of her
robes when her corpse is carried in dead from
drowning.
Speaking of florists, the great Edict of Maitland,
foi bidding Roman Catholics to deck funerals as
tor weddings, will lighten death to the poor, who
are nearly all related. These masses of flowers
cast over tombstones have been responsible for
° ne of _ the ghastliest snobbisms of the cen-
tury. lo report, in a social kind of way, that
Eady de Jones sent a silver service, and Mrs.
Snooks a pair of hand-made pillow-shams, for
Miss Low’s wedding, strikes some people— Mrs.
bnooks among ’em—as bad form. But to publish
long lists of senders of wreaths and crosses to a
corpse, arranging the names of senders on the
whos who?” system, is quitb the crack diaboli-
cal outrage of the age.
To be in some theatres when the play refuses to
start is livelier than to have some plays begin and
go on as usual. The second night of “The Bo-
hemian Girl ” brought a fair house to the Lyceum,
but at eight o’clock the curtain remained niotion-
-11 orc k f, stra had not appeared, and no squeak
of fiddle gave hope that things were proceeding
behind the scenes. The house took things calmly,
and waited—for good behaviour you can’t improve
on an English-Operatic audience. It has neither
the cheek of the comic ojiera, the passion of the
legitimate tragedy, the frenzy of the melodrama,
nor the loud, complacent purr of the pojiular con-
cert running in its blood ; in fact, it wears the
.cowed, crushed aspect of a little boy eating bread-
and-butter in sight of plum-cake. After a long
time, some decorous stamping and yelling began,
''ken at last it looked as if the Bohemian
Girl had quite decided not to show herself, the
house began to wake up to the situation and
wonder if it would get its money back. The
stamping began in earnest then, but the appear-
ance of All press, looking worried and worn, fol-
lowed by his little band, finally brought things to
quiet again. This brief opera season hasn’t been
too lucky, although everyone has worked hard,
and some excellent work has been achieved, if in
patches.
Miss Tree sings spiritedly all through the
Bohemian Girl, ” and looks her very best as
Arline in a spangled rose-tinted silk, covered with
little black velvet stars, with sjiangles running
round them. Her figure assumes a slimness, if it
has it not, in this simple but fetching frock. The
dull blue plumes in her hair contrast pleasingly.
Little baby Scott, who plays Arline in the pro-
logue, is the nicest little stage-child I have seen
for a long time. Leumane strikes his best note in
a hussar’s jacket, worn with a pair of Knightish
legs; and I arley makes up the most magnificent
pair of eyes imaginable for Devilshoof. The
choristers remained in the sulks all the evening,
and lent a lop-sided look to the poor little per-
formance that none of the principals could
straighten.
From Nicholson and Co. conies a batch of new
music, thickest of which is the Firm’s Musical
Magazine. Every class of inspiration and other-
wise is stowed away here, from Schubert’s “Wan-
derer ” and other classic gems down to (or, in
accordance with the renaissance of musical taste,
up to) “ Where is the harm if she met him ? ” It
seems that realism is getting into music allright,
for one song here is all about the Divorce Court
and how the Judge said, “I will part you for your
hearts are strangers now, the boy can with his
mother always stay, and if the girl is willing she
can with her father go.” Next century, at this
rate we will find ourselves singing Williams on
Real Property. From the same firm I receive two
senn-sacred songs carefully composed for Sunday
afternoons—“ The Lord is Love,” by Theo. Bon-
heur, and “From Cross to Crown,” by Godfrey
Marks. “The Governor’s March,” by Giebel,
comes out at an appropriate time, and has the re-
commendation of not being dedicated to anybody.
The fashion is to dedicate to a big-wig in such big
letters that the public mixes up the wig with the
composer and feels aristocratic when it plays that
particular composition. lam inclined to think
that Mrs. Brody’s Langley Waltz would never
have been written only for a certain other popular
waltz. “ Les Zouaves,” by Charles Clay, is a gay,
giddy little march that doesn’t pretend to be
. classical, and isn’t. An elegant Mazurka de Salon
by 1 rederic Gamier has more frills, sounds hard,
and is useful for a slow pupil who has to make a
splash.
From Glen and Co. comes the Federal Music
Book, furnished with an excellent set of dance
music. This firm also sends “Love’s Song,” sung
most effectively by Phil Newbury. The difficulty
is to remember that you are only singing Love’s
Song by Pietro Gileuth, arid none of the others
that suggest themselves. Another pretty song
from the same publishers is Leon Caron’s “Love
is Gold,"’ which Miss Cesca Vollugi is still rather
nervously interpreting at the Firm’s Pantomime.
Apropos of music, a young imported musician
has lately been mysteriously announcing in the
papers that he will receive no more communica-
tions by telephone. He has had to do this to
secure himself from pretty girls who have fallen
under the spell of his long hair, and have been
ringing him up at all hours of the day to have “ a
little talk.”
From Paling and Co. comes the affable “ Dante
March,” by George Jones, conductor of Dante’s
orchestra. “The Voyage,” by Nicholas Gehde,
is a windy, billowy song, full of foes and glory and
fusto, that has been well interpreted at the
aedertafel concerts by Herr Staedtgen. Two
more local compositions come from Paling’s. “The
Spoor of the Lion,” by Ernest Trumann, described
by the author as a spectacular, international song,
and YViegand’s dainty little ‘‘Orange Flower
Serenade,” dedicated mysteriously to the Countess
of Jersey, who is not going to take another hus-
band as far as anybody knows.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
One sympathises with Australia’s most
economical Governor. Spending money is palp-
able torture to him, but in his peculiar position he
must snnle on, even though his heart grows heavy
and his pocket light. Still, ‘ there’s ways of
doing things, ’ as Tyson said to his lady-friend in
explanation of his camel-like preparations for
droughty journeys. When first His Ex. dropped
in to say his Sunday prayers at a little church in
an obscure corner of his province, wardens were
disappointed to find the offertory unenriched by
banknote or gold coin. Now they’ve got to know
that the sky won’t fall if the plate passes the
worshipper-in-chief without annexing a three-
penny bit to bless itself with. All the fault of a
careless valet, it seems, who frequently forgets to
put Ins master’s purse in the pocket of the latter’s
Sunday-go-to-meeting coat.
i Worshippers in an Australian country church
lost all joy in their devotions t’other Sunday,
owing the blunder of their deaf-and-dumb
verger. The province’s Governor, arriving unex-
pectedly m time for service, asked the afflicted
official referred to to show him a seat. The mute,
hearing nothing, of course, mentally patted the
stiangeron the head as a decent old party, and,
as such, condescendingly assigned him a sitting.
.Before long, the lawful owner of the pew swept
up the aisle, so the verger, knowing her of old as a
firm upholder of vested rights, skipped ahead,
rapped his prayerful Ex. violently on the shoulder,
and waved him imperiously out of his corner,
100 late, the horrified pew-proprietress recognised
the distinguished trespasser, and wildly signalled
the unconscious evictor. lie was busy hustling
the intruder into less luxurious quarters, nor
would the turned-out tenant, in response to the
apologist s pleading gestures, resume his patronage
of her premises.
American and 1 reneh preference for scantily-
populated nurseries is apparently shared by Aus-
tralian ‘Naicest,’ though the church here, as
elsewhere, the blessedness of ‘quiver-
fulness bv precept and practice, especially
practice. While the Anglican shepherd-in-chief
boasts neither chiek nor child, one of his arch-
deacons rejoices in a family of thirteen. The
eldest of this patriarch’s seven girls is already a
parsoness. Her curate brother is engaged to one
of the eight daughters of an eloquent suburban
vicar, while a younger one, not in orders yet, has
plighted his troth to the descendant of a hardy
pioneer dame, who in her day was more expert
with bullock-teams and crosscut-saw than any
mere man in her district.

“Mention of churchy love affairs recalls the
recent Summer School’ at Mornington (Vic.)
Ihe week before Xmas in residence thereabout
were 160 summer scholars— i.e. delegates from
respective ‘Christian Unions’ of Melb., Sydney,
and Adelaide Varsities, and fromsecondaryschools
of same provinces. Not half a bad picnic for holi-
day-makers, willing to spend mornings and even-
ings.at Lible-class and ‘ Christian conference ’ re-
serving ‘ afternoons only’ for ‘God’s glorious
oxygen Each member put his guinea into the
pool which boarded and lodged the school for
a week in Mormngton’s various caravanserais •
boats bikes, buggies, sea-baths, tennis, and simi-
lar afternoon frisks were ‘ extras ’ arranged for
as economically as possible by ‘recreation com-
mittee.
All sorts and sects of parsons led the summer
sheep by still waters. Archdeacon Langley Prof.
Harper, and the Wesleyan master of ‘Queen’s."’
1 hen there were girl-graduates, minor clergy, soft-
goods Beath, his daughters, and the prettiest
one s fiance—a budding medical missionary. The
proposer and promoter of the gathering was on
hand, of course. Such an eloquent American
bachelor, ex-student of Yale ’Varsity (U.S.). In
America, it seems, these ■ ‘Chautauqua’ meets
atlect camp life as a protest against sinful luxury.
But it s different over thar, for the sexes never

A blessing left unplucked on the tree of life till
her bloom was faded and her age jibbed at her
everlasting efforts to turn it back to the twenties
was pushed into a nasty corner lately, but hopped
out without a scratch. The health officer called
at her lodging-house early one morning to seek
particulars of her reported diphtheritic sore-
throat, and sent up the landlady to her bedroom
to ask, among other questions-what was her age’
With much rambling, and—well, the sort of
nervousness peculiar to landladies, which isn’t the
common kind of nervousness at all—she arrived at
the embarrassing statement, “And he wants to
know how old you are, Miss Jones ? ” The answer
may be a joy for ever to some other persecuted
being: Please tell him I don’t know. Mother
never told us our age.” Far-thinking mother
wasn t she ? ’

“Housemaids and pages are quite ‘out a
present in Melbourne doctors' households. Every
self-respecting doctor keeps a professional nurse to
answer the doorbell, interview the patients, and
help m their surgical treatment if required. The
idea has not caught on yet to any extent in Syd-
ney, but there is a deal to be said in favor of the
system.
Knighthoods, like kisses, evidently go by
favor. Melbourne’s Mayor in Diamond-Jubiloe
year is plain ‘Mister’ to this day. Not that
Mayoralty, with its pomps and perks, isn’t good
payment even for the most patriotic of councillors.
Jiut if one Chief Magistrate souvenirs his brief
authority by a name-handle, why not all? A
giatoful Sovereign, ’tis said, would have been
delighted to honor her trusty servant aforesaid
had his pelf qualifications been more dazzling.
However, as the father of one of the prettiest
girls in Melbourne and of six athletic, expensively-
educated sons, all coming to the front in their
respective professions and businesses, the passed-
over patriarch in question can show good cause for
the comparative thinness of his gilt.

“ That favorite ballad-singer, bentupon European
training, had a steep hill to climb before attaining
her present popularity. As an employ 4 in a
hnders-street drapery firm (carried on elsewhere
since the big fire), she began to be talked of as the
possessor of a voice. Her teacher took her to
Govt.-House to show her paces so far back as
.Lady Loch s time. Somehow, for long enough she
carried by storm neither audiences nor critics
perhaps because, having married a fellow-em-
ploye, her art played second fiddle to household
cares and interests. Then her husband’s health
toiled’ till he could no longer work at his trade.
Ihe wife became family bread-winner, and for
years past has been a prosperous teacher and the
best-liked and most generally-accepted vocalist in
Victoria. Miss Maggie Sterling (in private life
Mrs. Riddell) is Gov. Brassey’s favorite among
local singers. As soon as her turn comes he
throws off his habitual concert stupor, and beams
and smiles most genially on the handsome, tuneful
performer.

No. 2.—The Postman.
A rural postman writes : “ I desire to add this
unsolicited testimony to the sterling qualities of
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa. lam a rural postman and
exposed to all kinds of weather. I have used Dr.
Tibbies’Vi-Cocoa daily this last eleven months,
and I believe that I can do my work with less
fatigue since I have used it. I can honestly
recommend to my fellow postmen Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa for its sustaining qualities.”
Of late years many new manufactures, rejoicing
in a multiplicity of names, have been brought
before the public notice, but there are none which
have bounded into favor so readily as has Dr.
'Dibbles’ Vi-Cocoa. Throughout the country its
sales have increased with great rapidity, and an
article which only a few years back was unheard
of has now become one of the most popular
beverages of the people.
Merit, and merit alone, is what is claimed for
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, and the Proprietors are
prepared to send to any reader who names The
Bulletin (a postcard will do) a dainty sample tin
of Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa free and post-paid.
There is no magic in all this. It is a plain, honest,
straightforward offer. It is done to introduce the
merits of Vi-Cocoa into every home. Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, as a concentrated form of nourishment
and vitality, is invaluable ; nay, more than this,
for to all who wish to face the strife and battle of
life with greater endurance and more sustained
exertion, is is absolutely indispensable.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.*
Still the benefactions of S.A.’s gilded
cattle-man, J. H. Angas, continue. Hie latest
gift is to the Adult Deaf and Dumb Mission —the
free use of his Parafield farm, a few miles out of
Adelaide, on the understanding that he will hand
it over as a gift if the management satisfies him.
The idea is to form a Home of Industry for aged,
blind, and infirm deaf mutes.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buver of all other Australian gems.*
An up-to-date Melbourne chemist has
started the Yankee plan of a side bar for the sale
of “soft” drinks. The other day a Bulletin
man counted 48 persons ranged up. at one time at
the “ soft ’’-bar in Washington Soul’s big Sydney
drug-store. A representative assortment of citi-
zens, too —including a doctor and a couple of bar-
risters. The rapidly-increasing vogue of these
harmless beverages certainly doesn’t square with
the reckless charges of collective drunkenness re-
cently made against the Australian native. The
time is near at hand when some of the big hotels
will have “ soft ’’-drink counters.*

An Australian Governor’s daughter has
just turned Roman Catholic. Cherchez Vhomme.
Lord Augustus Loftus is the oldest retired
Australian Governor, having been bom in 1817.
Bowen comes next, bom in 1821.
It is said to have been a toss-up between
Salomons and Pilcher, Q.C.’s, as to which would
take the N.S.W. Agent-Generalship.
Parry Okeden, the police dictator in the
Queensland murder boom, is a Snowy River
native, of squatter stock. He is now in his 68th
year.
Rumoi has it that W. J. Lyne, M.L.A.,
was approached with a mark of Royal favor on
Jan. 1, but preferred to wait till he becomes head
of a victorious political party. If this is true, the
long Tasmanian does not always blunder.

“ Molb.” : After all, what it humbug exor-
cise is ! Our Press father, David Blair, is 79, and
yet he never moved out of a library, unless driven
with a pitchfork.
An elderly undergrad, attends Sydney
University. He is probably within easy reach,
one way or the other, of 50, and is vamping up
knowledge in “ Arts.”
At Geeveston, on the Huon River (Tas.),
there is a big colony of Geeveses, all cold-water
drinkers. There are 19 “Mrs. Geeveses ” and 190
Geeveses of both sexes, all told.
W. F. Digby, who died recently in Eng-
land, was, 30 years ago, head of the largest stock
and station firm in Queensland. He was brother
of Kenelm Digby, Permanent Under-Secretary of
the Home Office.
B. E. Minns, The Bulletin artist, had,
at latest, shifted from Chelsea to Iver, Bucking-
hamshire, where he has taken a house for five
years. It is- quite untrue, as printed elsewhere,
that he thinks of returning to Australia at present.
Gov. Buxton always repudiated the
brewers when he was busy governing Adelaide,
but it is significant that the first address of wel-
come received by him on reaching England was
from the employes of Truman, Hanbury and
Buxton.

Judge Bundey (S.A.) is so ill that his early
retirement from the Bench is prophesied. The
choice of a successor would rest between Insolvency
Commissioner Russell and John Downer, Q.C.
Symon, Q.C., leader of the Bar, who refused the
position when Bundey was appointed, has too
large a practice to take the post.
“ Scotty the Wrinkler ” threatens to give
Melb. Society a bump this month. At the A.N.A.
fete and bazaar in Melb. Exhibition he will appear
outside a whaler’s tent, cooking and selling
damper, tea, &c., and discoursing in wild and
woolly Bohemian lingo on things in general.
A movement was set on foot in Sydney
last week to recognise by a leather medal the un-
usual modesty of Colonel Williams, M.P. The
colonel is a member of the British Commons, yet
he passed through Sydney and resided for weeks
on the Blue Mountains without once succumbing
to the interviewer. His invariable reply was :
“ You know your own affairs best. How can you
expect me, a stranger, to pass opinions on your
laws and customs ? ” He left for England a few
days ago, and is probably the first Englishman of
military or political note who didn’t set about,
putting Australia right half-an-hour after landing.
Many hundred signatures have been ap-
pended to a petition for the release of Willoughby
Andrew, sentenced to death in 1890 for the shoot-
ing of draper Bartlett, in King-street, Sydney—a
sentence afterwards commuted to life-imprison-
ment, on the ground of insanity. But it is alto-
gether unlikely that Andrew will be set free.
Julian Salomons’ promise to abstain from
brief-work while Agent-General kills the chance
of accomplishing at an early date a desire he in
years past often expressed—to argue a case before
the Privy Council. Pilcher is the only N.S.W.
barrister who has done so. Griffith (Q.), Wrixon
(Vic.), and Way (S.A.) have also “ been there.”
Travice Tod, of the Boot-Trunk Tragedy,
is a very blue-blooded person, according to Melb.
’Erald. In a respectful undertone, the ’Erald
informed the public that some of the “best”
families in South Australia are connected with
Tod, and then went on to mention that “his
uncle, a police-constable, received a medal for
heroism.”
“H.” : Hamilton’s “Life of O’Connell,”
published in 1888, states that the Liberator had a
natural son, after marriage. An Australian
legend has it that that natural son attained high
distinction in the law-courts. Certainly he was a
man intellectually worthy of such a father; also
he closely resembled O’Connell in physical ap-
pearance.
Spanish Bishop Salvado, of New Norcia,
W. A., is said to be the wealthiest man in the pro-
vince. Related that an old and respected Perth
firm was recently tottering, and asked the Bishop
for assistance. “Certainly,” was the answer,
“ will £50,000 help you ? The oredit of the pro-
vince must be maintained.” Only .£30,000 was
asked for and obtained.

Nowadays it is easy to
work through insolvency;
in times past that juris-
diction was a hard road to
travel. But even then
cheek did wonders. Writer
remembers seeing Fro s t
(Chartist rioter) drive tip
in his dogcart to the old
Hobart Insolvency Court.
Went in, and was examined
by Commissioner Browne,
the politest old gentleman
who ever wore shoe-buckles.
The case was fishy. “I
commit bankrupt to gaol
for six months,” said
Browne. “Eh ? ” exclaimed
Frost. “ Six months in
£aol, sir.” “ Good morn-
ing,” said Frost, and out he
walked. “ Stop him 1 ”
screamed the Commissioner.
But nobody did. Frost
drove home. On the way
met an acquaintance. “ Any
news, Frost?” “Yes,”
said the bankrupt. “ I’ve
been sent to gaol for six
months.” And that was
all that came of the busi-
ness. There was some flaw
in the statute which made
it impossible to arrest Frost
—only every previous insol-
vent had gone to gaol when
ordered like the mild spring
lamb, so the leak wasn’t
noticed.

The late Duke of Northumber-
land (he is in the peerage as a
Percy, but his proper family-name
is Smithson —“Duke Smithson of
Northumberland ” remarked the
Anti-Jacobin) was the man who
sold his town-house, in Trafalgar-
square, to the London Met. Board
of Works for a million pounds sterl-
ing. The big stone lion that stood
over Northumberland House gateway
was shifted to the top of Duke Smith-
son’s place on the Thames bank, near
Brentford. He was famous for nothing
save that lion. Twenty years ago he
achieved a billet in Disraeli Beaconsfield’s
Cabinet, but everybody seemed to know
that the appointment was merely an item
in “ Dizzy’s ” cynical scheme of self-
gratification. The onetime - despised
Hebrew had made up his mind to drive
a political team of tame dukes and mar-
quises, and this particular duke was
included among them for the sake of
his high-sounding title.
The late Vie. Judge Redmond
Barry was a Brummell without the
cosmetics, and frequently vented his
wrath on a law-clerk who, in chambers,
would persist in appearing before him un-
clean and untidy. Said Barry to him one
morning : “ Mr. , in the adjoining apart-
ment you will find water, soap, and towel.
Retire and make use of them and when you return
I may hear you.”

Another old Western pioneer has de-
parted—William Ravenscroft Smith, builder, who
died at Bathurst last week, aged 90 years, leaving
a host of descendants. He arrived in N.S.W. in
1830, and went westward the following year.

A recently “fired” Sydney fire-official,
noted for pluck, irascibility, and “ language,”
once, at a wharf fire, attempted to “ boot ” one
of his men (an ex-sailor) for not shifting himself
about quicker. The sailorman’s fist went home
hard, and the portly body was temporarily bereft
of oxygen. The subordinate was ordered to report
himself to the “ super. ” next morning. Accord-
ingly, when the time caine—“ Yer told me to
report meself ter yer this mornin’, sir.” “Ah!
did I, Smith ? Haw, go over to Blank’s hotel and
tell him to give you a drink at my expense.” To
forgive is divine, especially when you’re in the
wrong.
Harry Congreve Evans, who died in Ade-
laide last week, was editor and part-proprietor of
Quiz. When chief of the Advertiser staff (which
he joined when but 16 years old), he was considered
about the fastest stenographer in Australia, and
he has performed the feat of taking a Treasurer’s
budget and writing it up unaided afterwards. He
was a clever journalist, and did much for the re-
form-party through his little paper, but his real
bent was dramatic work. His death came as the
result of unremitting toil, for he took no rest, and
slaved on his paper as he had done on Hansard.
The Quiz published the day before he died was
almost entirely the work of his own pen.
A QUIET DAY'S FISHING IN THE
BEAUTIFUL YARRA.
The Presbyterian church at Windsor,
which George Bowman, ex-M.L.A. built and
“ perpetually ” endowed, has been pastor’d this
last 42 years by Rev. James Cameron, his own
son-in-law. It almost looks as if old George, who
arrived an infant in Windsor with his parents in
1798, and grew up a close-fisted man and indiffer-
ent politician, foresaw that his benevolence would
stay in the family.
A death really worth cabling down-under
was that of F. G. Wolseley, one-time squatter,
father of machine-shearing in Australia, and
brother of the British Cominander-in-Chief. Mr.
Wolseley, who died of internal cancer after terri-
ble suffering, pursued his object with the most
indomitable perseverance, and his well-deserved
success only came after innumerable failures and
the expenditure of a huge sum of money.
Ex-District Court Judge Dowling, now
living in retirement at Woollahra, is probably
the oldest living Australian P.M., having pre-
sided at Sydney City Court in the fifties. Mr.
Dowling, who is a son of the late N.S.W. Chief
Justioe Dowling, was oalled to the Bar in 1843,
and with the exception of Alex. Gordon (now in
England) and Archibald Miohie, of Melbourne, is
the oldest member of the Australian Bar.
Nosey Bob, the N.S. Wales hangman,
composed a speech 24 years ago when he entered
the Sheriff’s service, and has fired it off at
each subject sinoe without variation of cadence.
Walking in to pinion the victim he stands an
instant erect, and looking straight into the un-
fortunate’s eyes drawls, “My poor man (or
woman) no one regrets this more than I do.”
Then he proceeds with his job “in a workmanlike
manner.”
“ Attorney ” to The Bulletin :
When Premier Reid at length loses his political posi-
tion, he cannot resume practice at. the Bar. It would be
interesting to know whether, when he took a Privy Coun-
eillorship at Jubilee-time, he overlooked the praotical
result of his acceptance. Mr. Reid, before he became
Premier, was accustomed to receiving a modest pittance
from his Bar - practice, chiefly as a libel-and-slander
counsel, but he could not eow resume practice, for the
Bur is legally and by precedent closed to him as a P.O.
(Mem.: His disability ceases with the death of the reign-
ing sovereign ; should the Queen die, all existing P. 0.-
ships are cancelled.) As a Privy Councillor, he is a mem-
ber of the highest and final court of appeal from colonial
decisions. When the legal maxim is that “no man can
be a judge in his Own cause” (and a client’s case is the
case of his counsel), how then can Mr. Reid plead a cause
which may ultimately go for its final adjudication to the
Privy Council, of which he is a member? Dailey saw
this, and, on accepting a P.C.-ship, retired from the Bar.
Stephen, long before obtaining a seat in the court of
appeal, had retired from N.S.W. Benoh.
Probably Reid and Turner and Kingston, who
are all in the same boat, will simply disregard the
mouldy precedent. They don’t really sit on the
Privy Council, and are members of that Court in
name only. Turner and Kingston, anyhow, con-
tinue to practise.

JANE’S PROPER PLACE .
ROSE: “You proposed to, eh? Well, what did he do when he proposed, Jane?”
JANE : “ Oh, nothing much!"
ROSE: “I suppose he went on his knees —as they all do.”
JANE : “How foolish of you! I was in his arms as well as on his knees.”
There was to be an inquest in the morning,
and the body lay in the bar parlor, so the talk in
the bar was restrained and painfully lugubrious.
The old man with a ragged swag said the sight of
a corpse always upset him ever since a certain
affair, and, yielding to pressure, explained that he
was padding it on the western plains, making for
Hamilton, one day, when he came across a man
lying beside the fence. Closer inspection con-
vinced him the man was dead. “ I jist took a
casual look over him,” said the veteran, “ but he
didn’t seem to have nothin’ of value, so 1 left him
as he was an’come on an’ told the police. Next day
the traps brought the body in, an’ I’m _ bio wed if
they hadn’t found thirty quid in his hip pocket.
“Since then,” continued the aged swaggie, turn-
ing sadly to his beer, “the sight of a corpse has
always made me miserable.”
Various and astonishing are the methods
for turning the inexpensive kanaka, that abounds
on the Queensland coast, into a follower of the
Lord. From one of the great Northern sugar
centres (Ingham) comes the yarn of a missionary-
attempting to demonstrate to his dusky audience
the equality of all men before God. Quoth the
preacher: “Me white man; you blackfellow.
Me alleesame batoe cane ; you alleesame rappoe
cane. Bymeby big fellow God he come along, cut
’im cane, cane he die. Bymeby God he crush ’im
batoe cane, crush im rappoe cane, make him
alleesame first-class white sugar I ” A Queens-
land version, as it were, of “ The Christian,” by
’All Cane.

Related that the festive heir of a back-
blocks squatter, during a lurid “ tear ” in Sydney,
severely injured his leg and sent for a prominent
medico, who presently brought along his amputat-
ing instruments. The young bushman viewed the
cutlery with dismay. “ What are the knives for,
boss?’’ he asked. “It’ll have to come off,” re-
plied the doctor briefly ; “ nothing else for it.”
“ Oh, no, dash it all, don’t go and spoil a cove for
riding,” protested the lively young man from out-
back ; “just you ring-hark it.” But the doctor
said ring-barking wouldn’t suit the case, so the
youth discharged him and sent for another saw-
bones “ with some colonial experience.”

“ Phil ” : Never, when on foot, go near a
single bullock, unless he is a worker. He has
probably pleuro and will rush you. Dill, of
Tugambie, was nearly killed thereby. I was sent
to rouse a white bullock, as a joke, being told it
was “ Smiler,” one of the workers (Colombo
Plains). He charged me, and I threw my billy
and straw-hat at him. The latter stopped him
till I got to a boree sapling. The yahoos were
laughing on the other side of the fence, whilst I
was yelling, “Fetch a horse, you dogs!”
Bullock died next day. Kangaroos in a dry
season can be run down on foot. A Frenchman
at Narrow Plains killed dozens that way in ’77.
But the old man is dangerous even then. The
carpenter (Bramhall), at Womalong, got his biceps
cut to the bone by a ’roo who was quietly pro-
specting for water at the door of the men’s hut.
* • * •
A cockie’s wife in N. Tas. recently found
the freshly-cast skin of a tiger-snake beneath her
husband’s pillow when about to make the bed in
the morning. The snake was afterwards found in
a cupboard. It is refreshing to be able to tell a
snake-lie which is absolutely true.
They were twins—Victorian natives
—and so like that even old acquaintances
couldn’t always tell t’other from which,
and they were shearing at a Vic. western
district station. The shed-boss was an
old Scotchman who didn’t know good
shearing from bad, though he thought
himself a prime judge. It was a
“square” board, 10 pens on each side.
Tom Ashley was in No. least, and Harry
in 16 west. Both doing about same
tally and pinking them, but old Mac.
got,' it into his head that Harry was
roughing them, while he declared that
“ Tom ” was the finest shearer of the
lot. Used to chip Harry about three
times a day, and told him that “only for
the sake o’his brither, he would gie him
the sack.” “ Tell you what,” said
Tom to Harry one night, “ we’ll ring
the changes on old Mac. In the morning
we’ll change clothes and I’ll shear in
number 16 and you in my pen. It was
agreed on, and the other hands promised
to keep it dark. After first count-out
next day Mac. came up to Tom (in
Harry’s pen). “ Look here, Ashley,
ye’re getting rougher every day. Why
canna ye shear like yere brither ? ” “ I’m
shearing them as well as he is.” “ Oh,
ye are, are ye ? Come ower an’ I’ll
show ye the deeference.” This was too
much for Tom, who burst out laughing,
whereupon Mac. sacked him on the spot.
But when Tom went to the head boss
for his cheque he explained the little
ruse, and Mac. got the sack himself. —
Fred. W. H.
* * * *
In a Q. agricultural district are
two brothers, who own adjoining farms.
Each was married, and had a family,
when the extraordinary arrangement was
come to of swapping wives. Now each
has another family by the other’s wife,
all registered as if no deal in wives had
taken place. Now, what complications
of relationship are here ?

“ H.M.T.” : The “dulgite” is
the most industrious burrower in W.A.,
not excepting the gold digger, and in
sandy soil, that is pretty nearly every-
where, he can travel underneath as fast
as a flock of sheep on the war-path
through a poison-patch. He is about
nine inches long, with coarse greyish
hair, sharp snout, and claws like an Act
of Parliament.

MATRIMONIAL SIDELIGHT ,
ONLOOKER: “ What’s the row about? ”
TOM : “ Jim Juggins ’ere says the missus ’ enpecks
me.”
JIM: “So she does. I seen her bash yer over the

His billy never boiled, but that made no difference
to him. With a little hooked stick he removed
tbe empty billy, having previously sprinkled some
dust into it, and set it back to cool. After a
joyous swig he unrolled his swag, and, filling an
old clay with scraps of bark, wrapped his blanket
around him and puffed himself to sleep. He was
the most perfectly happy swaggie I ever saw. He
never spoke, but the serene smile on his ancient
face was a tale of contentment. I reckoned that
he built 7300 camps a year, and, as he had been 12
years on the same journey he had boiled that old,
tealess billy 87,600 times, which is about the record.
And he may be going still.
* * * *
“Steele Rudd”: Be the “recent dis-
covery” that the jew-lizard runs on its hind feet.
Certainly it can get about on its hind feet, but
you’ve got to hit the dog with it first, or toss it
over the top of a tree to wake it ; and then nine
times out of ten it won’t. And when it
does mount its hind feet it doesn’t run,
or walk either. It dances. Liz. lays from 12 to
30 eggs, mostly deposited in a bank —but has any-
one ever seen more than two of its young at a
time ? [Whatever may be the case with the jew-
lizard, instantaneous photography has demon-
strated the fact’that some lizards do run at times
on the hind-legs only.— Ed. B.]

wrong, lgr. strychnine will

running in the bush, if fat,

lean, much less is fatal. Pigs
at large eat many stryehni-
ferous roots, and are thus
rendered partly immune, but
why it takes a larger quan-
tity of strychnine to kill a
fat pig than a lean one, can’t

“ D.S.” : So hot was it
at the Barrier lately that we
spread out steak on the gal-
vanised-iron roof of the hut
and let it fry there by the
sun’s rays, catching the
gravy as it ran down the
ruts of the roof. Then came
a heavy hailstorm, which
perforated the roof and
turned it from a frying-pan
into a gridiron.

Toni, an aboriginal,
lately speared Charles Beat-
tie, 90 miles west of Alice
Springs. On arrest Tom
tried to kill Constable Cowle,
but was shot by a native
trooper. He lived but an
hour afterwards, and died
repentant, saying, “Me
blully rogue ! Me spear poor
old Beattie.” Tom had been
a missionary pupil, and his
end justified the fact.

A SNAKE YARN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ You talk of snakes,” said Jack the Rat,
“ But, blow me, one hot summer,
I seen a thing that knocked me flat —
Fourteen foot lontr, or more than that.
It was a reg’lar hummer !
Lay right along a sort of bog,
Just like a log 1
“The ugly thing was lyin’ there
And not a sign o’ movin’,
Give any man a nasty scare ;
Seen nothin’ like it anywhere
Since I first started drovin’.
And yet it didn't scare my dog.
Looked like a log !
“ I had to cross that bog, yer see,
And bluey I was humpin’;
But wonderin’ what that thing could be
A-lyin’ there in front o’ me
I didn’t feel like jumpin’.
Yet, though I shivered like a frog,
It seemed a log !
“ I takes a leap and lands right on
The back of that there whopper 1”
He stopped. We waited. Then Big Mao
Remarked : “ Well, then, what happened, Jack?”
“Not much,” said Jack, and drained his grog.
“ It was a log ! ”
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

“ Ignotus ” : As to whether galvanised-
iron poisons water. I got a, new galvanised tub,
and left about 2in. water in it for a week, the re-
sult was a copious sediment of white powder that
turned yellow when heated, and resumed its origi-
nal color when it cooled. It would be interesting
to know the exact chemical reaction, bo far as 1
know, the only, factors were (1) iron coated with
zinc, (2) clean rainwater, (3) the atmosphere, with
its usual percentage of carbonic acid.
.£550 Reward.
“ Cockney ” to The Bulletin :
The story of the Melb. Boot-Trunk Tragedy is valuable
to the public both as a further revelation of pompous
police dullness and another reminder of the means by
which newspaper readers are bluffed into giving credit
for astuteness to certain detectives. The acquaintance,
amounting in some cases to friendship, of newspapei
reporters with the Grim. Inves. Dept, is a P o 'nt to con-
sider in this particular case where a reward of ±-550 was
offered for the discovery of the persons criminally con-
cerned.
Five hundred and fifty pounds are to be paid
away by the Victorian Govt., as per promise to
the community in general, and the public at-
large is interested in seeing that none of the
cash falls into the wrong hands. Every man,
woman or child is a potential discoverer of a
golden nugget in the form of an Orful Mystery,
and we all have an interest in securing fair play
whenever there may be a nugget to pick up. it
certain detectives, owing to the la w f ul advan-
tages which their position gives them, hate
learned to regard themselves as a Chosen People
in the distribution of these Government boun-
ties, and if newspaper-reporters are determined
to encourage their error, it is timei one
periodical to protest. The writer doth hereby
protest that an official enrichment of any police-
man on account of the Boot-Trunk Tragedy will
be a scandalous job.
The story brings to light some almost astound-
ing illustrations of police success in thei art of
how not to do it. To begin with, two detectives
were put upon the track of the girl who helped
to drop the corpse into the Yana. They went
to the P corpse’s late residence, they interviewed
the corpse’s friend and undertaker, Regina
Dubberkk, and when she gave them a wrong
name they knew she was lying b e e au S eLheyhad
been provided with a photograph of her, under
her proper name. But they did-nothing. Three
other detectives were put upon the track of a,
young man who had given Rf.gina the boot-
trunk aforesaid. They interviewed hum He
told them that the boot-trunk, with his name
written on it, was similar to the one he had
given to Regina D., but that the head at the
morgue wasn’t her head. Th n the three dete-
tives made their precious report to Superintend-
ent Brown, and whilst they were making it one
of the detectives, who had interview ed the gir ,
chipped in with the information that they rmi
found her alive and well. Here were five de-
tectives, together with Sup. Brown, discussing
the subject of the girl who was the actual pos-
sessor of the boot-trunk, as well as the sweet-
heart of a young man whose name wasi inscribed
thereon—and not one of the crowd struck the
idea of her having been connected with tne
tragedy in anv other capacity than that of tne
corpsef She wasn’t dead, and so they did
nothing. The house where Regina with
Madame Radalyski was again visited by dete , ctl , ®
-strange to say! Different ’tecs this time, and two
of ’em. They called there in the course of enf l” lr y a
stolen cinematograph, says an Argus reporter. ‘
tive Bear found that the man he sought was not tnere,
but, from certain observations, he remarked to
detective Murray, as they left, ‘ I’ll wager that house s
‘ crook.' ” The sapience of Bear is merely a casual item in
the story, but the fact that the Argus man dragged it in
by the heels is worth mentioning. Madame Radalyski,
nominally a masseuse and a palmist, has been reoe g
“ gentlemen friends” of late. The character of the
was so palpable that even a detective noticed it. iod,
the ally of Madame and Miss Regina in the B.T.T. amused
himself by reporting to the police a burglary at his office.
Seemingly there had not been a burglary. Tod acted out
of joyful devilment, perhaps. Or, maybe, he thought
might as well attract police attention to himself, so as to
disarm suspicion. Let Tod pass.

FROM START TO FINISH.—A STORY WITH A MORAL.
Madame Radalyski, Tod, and the girl then held a conference, and the man proposed that they should
crush the body into a box and throw it into the Yarra. This was agreed to. Dubberke’s boot-trunk was
seized upon, two addresses were scratched off the lid and side, and, overlooking the words “T. R. Akins,"
which she had herself written, the girl assisted to strip the body and put it in the box. After it was doubled
up sufficiently to make it fit, it was tied with a clothes-line and left in the bed-room. Tod remained in the
house all that day and the same night, and the following afternoon he went to a livery-stable in Commercial-
road and hired a buggy. This he drove up to the door of Madame Radalyski's house after nightfall on the
evening of 14th ult., and the box once placed in the vehicle Tod and Dubberke drove off with it. The girl had
previously lived in Williains-road, and knew the whole locality, and Tod accordingly directed the horse down
the side of Como Estate until they reached the quarries. There a piece of stone was picked up, fastened to
the wire clothes-line, which they had in readiness, and with one holding each handle they carried it with
difficulty down the steep slope to the banks of the river. Reconnoitring carefully, Tod found a pair of lovers
sitting on the bank not far away, and, fearing that their movements would be detected, he and the girl
dragged the box to a clump of bushes and hid it there. They then returned to the buggy and drove back
rapidly to the stable from which the buggy came, arriving there about 10 o’clock.
January, he was despatched with all the other plain
ordinary policemen to view the head at the morgue, he
thought it looked very like the head of the erring spinster
in question. He called upon her mother at once, from
her learned that her daughter was supposed to be at
Madame’s, and straightway Constable Organ— in igno-
rance of the house having been visited weeks before
—went to Madame’s and saw the same Regina
whom the ’tecs had seen, and who told him a vain
which would in due course have been reported to the
superintendent. Meanwhile the girl Dubberke called
upon a respectable married man (named “Mum . ) in
Collins-street and told him things sufficient to put him in
communication with the police, and someone came and
took down Dubberke’s confession in a book. The de-
tectives, from first to last, failed to discover anybody or
anything connected with the Boot-Trunk Tragedy. They
were put upon the right scent, and they could smell
nothing. Not one penny of the £550 reward is due to
them for their want of sagacity. Organ might have
solved the mystery on his “ own ’’ if one of the principals
hadn’t anticipated him. Also it is evident that if
the original boot-trunk clue had been communicated to
the South Yarra constables they would probably have
made more vigorous enquiries re missing females in that
neighborhood. One of them lives only two doors away
from Madame’s house, where the girl met her death.
A few newspaper-attempts to make out a case for the
’tecs, as regards the £550 reward, are certainly worth
quoting’. The actual informant, Dubberke, professed her-
self unwilling to “ touch a penny," but—said the Argus
—“ others who have assisted the detectives in bringing the
mystery to an end are not so scrupulous.” The Age said :
“The man to whom Dubberke unburdened herself is un-
doubtedly entitled to the £5O offered for information that
would lead to the identification of the deceased, y
not to the whole amount (why any question about it.)
the informing accomplice having agreed to forfeit her
claim. In the distribution of the £5OO it is thought likely
that the Government will take into consideration the
work done by Detectives A. B. C. D. E. F. G., and, per-
haps others.” This present scribe, remembering this
same Government’s objection to dealing liberally with
poor old Graham Berry, hopes it isn't likely that 4.550 of
public money will be divided among a crowd of utterly
undeserving objects. The detectives had their chance
and let it slip. Public opinion is emphatically unfavor-
able to the suggestion that men who were put in the way
to do a certain thing should receive a bonus for failing to
do it If the man and woman actually entitled to the
whole of this money are not advancing their claim, all
well and good. Victoria has no money to throw away.
Let it stop in the Treasury and count as a small set-off
against the railways deficit.

say-so Yardman : You make a “short story ”of what
is merely material for a paragraph. You have no graces
of style to warrant this T.K. : Sleeping in pure, dry
country air would, no doubt, benefit his lung-trouble
Meora : “ Pass an Act to stop that tote.” When the
palm-oil stops that tote-shop stops. —and that’s exactly the
whole situation E.S.A. : Don’t want any blanky.
Your matter is all blanky A.W. : Don’t care for
“ Mesalliance.” C.B.A. : Bulletin simply abhors “dia-
lect” yarns and verse; also, it loathes parodies. One
parody in 48,000 is passable —not more Eko : Hiecup-
cure story too much like many cold-cure yarns Talger :
Yes, your place was infested with cockatoos, so you bird-
limed a giant tree. But they gave one deafening shriek
and flew away with the tree. Heard that 25 years
ago—and it was probably 400 years old then
Nosnibora: The story you send along as true has
been true in hundreds of other cases. It is quite an aged
story. When a married man is threatened with exposure
by a base and venomous Flossie he makes the best of a
bad job by giving it all away to his wife if his wife is
sterling metal, and the lawful little woman enjoys the
satisfaction of sneering at Flossie’s discomfiture when
that rampageous, yellow-haired humbug fires off her
stale “ revelation,” Any intelligent wife will forgive an
erring husband who puts her in the way to triumph over
the other cat. She can excuse the past if she sees no
danger in the future. A jealous woman reasons more
logically than a jealous man Louis M. : “Compari-
son,” idea not new, and verse very ricketty. “ Fairly
good ” did not mean acceptance. Other probable
E.S. : Neither merit nor demerit..-....W.M.F. : Sonnet too
unequal. Other item, brainy but dry Montey:
Dream-surprise-device hackneyed F.E.M., : No
“ scathing” called for, though cannot say there ! s much
promise Dab. : Rather late in the day for unexcit-
ing gibes at Melbourne’s Metropolitan Board of Works
New Chum : Particulars of “ Sturt’s Rite ” unprintable.
Vide Dr. Roth’s Ethnology of Central Queensland
Ragnar Redbeard : You have no right to such a pen-
name, unless you are the original American owner
thereof —which, judging from your MS., you assuredly
aren’t Ox.vmel: No glaring faults and no pro-
nounced merits .... J.P. : Returned......C.P. (Q.) : Con-
trary to general opinion, we’re not infallible; and it’s
almost worth erring to be corrected by you .... Zampa :
Refreshingly naive B.M. : Thanks; we can copy that
out of the office encyclopaedia F.K. (Narrabri): Base
imitations of the man you scorned J.W. (Brisb.) : Too
theological R.C. : Verse too rough and crude ; com-
ment good in spots only Carini: All weak: Match-
head : Won't strike on this box Dido: Not quite up.
Backblocks : Perhaps “Hatter.” W. G. Selby
wants address and probable route of Probasco’s circus......
M.L. : Can you invent an automatic blind that will shift
up and down as the sun goes in and out? It would pay
you far better than poetry, and save us many curses
W.P.P. : And why shouldn’t real religion laugh its full ?
It’s the mostly hypocritical pietist that has such a grue-
some lack of humor Tang Anyut: Please call.

From the MAYOR and JUSTICES OF THE PEAC R, and the Rev. Mr. JONES, of INVERCARGILL, where Mr. Palmer lived tor about twenty-five
years. Read what they say :
THIS IS TO CERTIFY that we, the undersigned, have known the bearer, Mr. S. A. Palmer, as a resident of Invercargill, New
Zealand, for a great many years. We .know he has been a great sufferer during the past fourteen years, and he never was expected to
recover, but as the result of taking a course of VITADATIO he has quite recovered his long-lost health, and now appears to us to be quite
a new man. We have pleasure in recommending him to the public, wherever his lot may be cast, as an honorable man, and worthy of the
confidence of all with whom he may come in contact.
JOHN SINCLAIR, Mayor, Invercargill.
JNO. W. MITCHELL, Merchant, J.P., Invercargill.
PETER WRIGHT JONES, Primitive Methodist Minister, Invercargill.
G. FROG GATT, J.P., Invercargill.


HOT WEATHER DRINKS.
Man’s thirst in days of heat and toil
is boundless as the sea;
"But once the BILLY ’gins to boil,
The fragrant BII.LY TEA ;
Cures cark and care, and stills turmoil,
And satisfied is he.
Then, if on Social pleasures bent,
When friend would meet with friend,
You seek for means of quiet content
A pleasant hour to spend,
Of all the gifts that gods have sent
None beats “ White Heather ” Blend.
JAMES INGKLIS & CO.,
SYDNEY and BRISBANE;
Agents for “ White Heather ” Whisky,
Oranuma and Fodah Porridge Meals,

TheN.S. W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bmr. 1 his bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
ohow gave tt first prize against all competitors.
What has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is ho fun
m summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should eom-
mumcate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
mincer.

POLITICAL
POINTS
. Brisbane Courier vainly tries to galvanise
interest in Premier Dickson’s tour. Byrnes, the
orator, had Florrie Schmidt to fill in the gaps, ft
would take Patti and a circus combined to en-
liven the speeches of the dismal Queen-street
rent-collector.
Last session of N.S.W. Parliament com-
menced 16th August. Towards the end of Sept,
two small Bills were sent by the Assembly to the
Council ; three were sent up in Oct. ; none in
Nov., and 23 in Dec. So when the Council left
eight of them unfinished at the end of the session,
and all the labor spent on them was wasted, it had
some kind of excuse for its conduct.
A big case looms before S.A. law-courts.
S.A. Govt, has been negotiating with Glenelg
Bail way Co. for over a year for the purchase of
its line, the only one privately owned in tlie pro-
vince. . The co. refused the Govt, offer of £120,000,
and demanded .£160,000, and has declined all
proposals of arbitration. Moreover, it denies that
“ e Govt. has power of compulsory purchase.
Adelaide lawyers already scent the good thing.
Lawyer- Marshall Lyle, in a letter to the
Age, recounts 26 push-outrages perpetrated in
Melbourne and suburbs inside six days. Top
sentence, three months. Bottom sentence, a warn-
ing from the Bench not to do it again. In most
cases no arrest made. Push now lurking for
Marshall Lyle with a large brick, through that
well-advertised gentleman’s habit of putting his
full name in large letters at the bottom of his fre-
quent outpourings in the press.
The appointment to N.S.W. Leg. Council
of ex-Treas. J. F. Burns is taken by all sides as a
near certainty. And yet “ Cocky ”is just the
man whose use for the purposes Reid harangues
about will be of least value. That he is a Free-
trader sums up all his political virtues. And he
never was even a Freetrader-only a Revenue-
Rarriffist of the old school. - One of the oldest
sitters in the Assembly, his record of general use-
fulness is beggarly and of democratic usefulness
nil.
The serpent of old Nile was guileless
compared with the Boodle wraith when cornered.
Distance : Q. Opposition stonewalled the noxious
Elections Tribunal Act for 40 hours, the other
week, and let go when Premier Dickson promised to
move an amendment that the Bill should not take
effect till Ist May (after coming general election)
Dickson duly moved the amendment, but five of
his colleagues were missing on division, and the
Govt, followers voted solidly against Dickson and
the Labor men. Result—Bill passed, amendment
lost, and Dickson oilily explained that the five
Ministers were ‘Testing.’-’ This moral little Bill
allows a Pol. Mag. to reject or attest claims, and
then to sit on the bench (where he can> be cross-
examined) and review his own work.
N.S.W. Speaker Abbott regrets that
Maoriland shows no sign of taking a hand in the
new Federal Conference, even if that province
doesn’t federate immediately. It will have to
come in some day, the Speaker reckons, and then it
will have to accept a constitution that it had no
voice in framing. There is another cause for
regret. The last Draft Constitution was built up
by a conference in which the democratic element
slightly preponderated, W.A. and Tasmania being
almost wholly Tory, while there was a slight
leaven of Toryism about the delegations of the
other three provinces. If Queensland comes in
the next set of Constitution-makers will consist of
three Tories, two Democrats and a Wobbler, so
the balance will incline towards Toryism. ’ A
Maoriland representative might have turned the
scale in the progressive interest, but in his
absence the prospects are not bright.

article on Federation (13th January):—
Had Mr. Kingston given any consideration to these
facts he would never have dreamt of inducing the Parlia-
ment of N.S. Wales to accept a Bill which has already
been rejected by the people, and which, at the present

Even the lelegraph, which has put up a record
for persistent, brazen lying on the Federation
subject, should be a little ashamed of that Ana-
nias. But ft doesn’t seem to be. The official
records of N.S.W. tell that the people of that pro-
vince accepted the late Federal Constitution by a
majority of' over 5000, though the! treacherous
fang in office annulled their decision. Still if the
'elegraph’s boss perjurer tells his lie often enough
probably the N.S.W. public will begin, some day
or other, to have a vague idea that there was
really a majority against the Bill on June 3.

ceived and adopted, satisfaction being expressed at the
labors of the section having received the approbation of
the Government in connection with suggestions made on

aid of the alleged labor-members in finding out

If Premier Reid succeeds in drawing
Queensland into the Federal Conference the finan-
cial difficulty will be worse than it was before.
Last time there were five provinces to build a
U1 !!, 0 ™ 1 tariff for, and the biggest trouble was
with lasmama, which wanted a tariff that would
produce £2 3s. lid. per head, per annum to keep
its finances square. The N.S.W. Govt, bucked
oti_ tine ground that it wasn’t taking any £2 3s.
lid. t a r>ff, and couldn’t devise any other way out
ot the difficulty. If Queensland comes in it means
a^I ent of * P rov . ince that wants a tariff of £2
14s. 7d. per inhabitant per annum to keep it
square, so the difficulty is so much worse. More-
over, Tasmania wants a high tariff mainly because
it is making stupendous efforts to pay off its
deficit; if it was content to spread the payment of
its shortage over a generation, as N.S.W. is doing
it could do with almost as low a tariff as N.S.W.
has. But Queenstand actually needs the money.
The spectacle of the Norddeutscher Lloyd’s
Shipping Co. and a pair of Sydney lawyers trying
to compel Maoriland to submit to an invasion of
cheap Austnan laborers is amusing. They may
bluff the boneless Governments of N.S.W. and
Leperland, but they will find that the men who
reti the earthq 1 1 ake islands are made of sterner
stuff. Ihe community which, almost in one act
wiped out the power of the wool-kings, and
created a social system which enforces an 8-hour
day makes employers pay overtime for extra
work, cares for women and children in factories,
ffattens out the registry-office-keepers who swindle
servant-girls, gives almost everybody a half-holi-
day per week and sees that they get it, that issues
liie-policies with State guarantee at lower rates
than private offices issue theirs without any
guarantee save their own, and provides old-age
pensions is not going to be bullied by any pair of
'Sydney legal gentlemen and a foreign shipping
co even if its chief shareholder is the great war-
god Kaiser Bill. The N.D.L. had better paste
the fact in its hat. Bun Tuck Seddon has
®P2. ket ?; [Since this par. was written the
difficulty has been settled on Premier Sed-
don s own terms. When the batch of nearly
penniless aliens, who caused all the recent anguish
first turned up on the coast, the shipping co. was
informed that it could land them only on condition
that !t guaranteed their maintenance for three
months. The co. refused with loud contumely,
but after much delay and expense in cablegrams,
and after feeding and lodging the Austrians for
some time, and humping them to and fro on the
race of the earth, it has at last accepted Seddon’s
terms. So the international complication is off.]
SOME NSW. NOTABLES.—HOW THEY SPENT THEIR HOLIDAYS.
Apropos of the loud congratulation about
the diminution of crime in Australia, as evidenced
by the decrease of prisoners in gaol, the decrease
means one of three things. Either crime is
diminishing, or a very large proportion of crimi-
nals are not captured, or a lot of those captured
got off with very inadequate sentences. For
instance, if 10 larrikins maim a man for life and
are sent to prison for a long term, that increases
the prison population. And, if they are fined 405.,
that doesn’t increase the prison population. And’
if they are not captured at all, then they don’t
appear in the crime statistics, and crime is sup-
posed to diminish.

It seems almost certain
now t ha t Queensland will be
represented at the new Federal Con-
ference of Premiers, which will make things even
a little more mixed than they otherwise would be.
1 remier Dickson will be present to represent his
province—without any authority from Parliament
or people. And Premier Reid will be there
appointed by himself, to state that the people of
N.S.W. rejected the last Bill—which everybodv
knows to be false—and to ask, in the name of the
popular minority, for two sets of amendments
which, m some important respects, directly con-
tradict each other. Also, Reid will be there cosay
that he wants a new financial scheme, but doesn’t
know what kind of new scheme he wants—only he
must have it. What a burlesque it all is !
Melb. : The biggest humbug going is com-
pulsory education. Truant officers seem to be abo-
lished. All the children coming out of school are
nice and clean, spick and span. None without
boots—not one anywhere. But such abound in
the streets—they aren’t wanted in the schools.
Plain old Brunker, who bullocked away to
the extent of 144 attendances at N.S.W. Assembly
divisions, had the best record for last session.
Brunker is never of the slightest use when
he is there, but he is there with the
stolid persistency of a cow in a big
paddock. Anderson and Hawthorne tied
for second place with 138 votes each.
The worst record was that of an unknown
alleged person named Edwin Richards,
from Mudgee, who voted at 15 divisions.
In the Leg. Council two members had
leave of absence, but among those sup-
posed to be present the worst records were
those of Mort, Stephen, Suttor, Thornton,
Walker, and James Blanksby. None of them
voted even once during the session. Hoskins and
Hing ran them very close with two votes a-piece.
Owing to S.A. Leg. Council adjourning
Without passmg the Appropriation Bill, Treas.
Holder declines to pay public accounts, and as a
result merchants are cursing the Tory majority.
Many of them are on the Tory side politically,
P. jVi s unexpected development seems to make a
big difference.

Aguinaldo—-cognomen of the Filipino
leader—-is Spanish for “ New Year’s gift.” Just
before the commencement of ’99, Aguinaldo gave
himself up to the Y ankees near Manila, explain-
PaP he feared treachery. A few davs later,
the New Year's gift ” dodged back to the insur-
gents camp, probably with complete information
and mental plans of the American position, and
immediately began to issue threats and manifestos
against his quondam protectors. Aguinaldo must
be a very smart, as well as very bold, man.

u°lu all the Australian writers who talk of going
fc •P e, j says the Bookfellow. “Lawson, who
wished to travel last year, says April. E. J.
Brady says June. Victor Daley says 1900 and
1 aris Exhibition. Dorrington ditto. And there
aie others less known. But we have our own way
of translating Cicero’s famous sentence. Abiit—
he fondly dreamed of Europe. Excessit— he told
his friends, and collected envious congratulations.
Evasit— he got married and couldn’t go. Erupit—
he went, and was mighty glad to get back again.”
“A genius of extraordinary personal ap-
pearance, labelled Horace L. Short,’’ writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ has invented
a wonderful voice-magnifier. It is to bear for
miles, by sheer volume, strongly and intelligibly,
the ordinary voice tones. The message to be
sent is spoken into a phonograph ha ving special
cylinders. The magnifier is attached, and the
words roll out in a deafening roar. The whole
secret lies in a new discovery in acoustics, and,
alter personal experiences of the machine, I think
its possibilities are illimitable. One fixed in the
.rarliament House would carry the speakers’ voices
over the city of Sydney or Melbourne, and wipe
out payment of members in one night. The ordi-
nary phonographic record of a brass-band becomes
a hying, screaming, thumping Coldstream with
the listeners in the drums. Short was in Australia
about six years ago ; has a Goliath-like head that
requires a size 10 hat ; and no one who had once
seen him would ever forget him.”

Government Notices.
A
Publio Service Board,
60 Young-street, Sydney,
17th December, 1898.
COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of CANDIDATES for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE
. the COLONY will be held in Sydney, and at various
cm ntrv centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to
onit the convenience of candidates, on the 20th MARCH
next and four following days. The estimated number of
anointments to be made is 50. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
nf examination, of good character, and in sound health.
Persons already employed in the General Division of the
Public Service will be admitted to the examination, pro-
vided their age does not exceed thirty years. The sub-
ieots in which Candidates will be examined are : -Hand-
wri tin"- English (Dictation, Composition, Prtas writing,
Parsin'" Analysis, and derivation of words), Arithmetic
ffull course), Geometry (Euclid, first Book), Geography
foutlines of physical and political geography, and the
p-eoirraphy of the Australasian Colonies in moderate
detail), English History from the Middle of the Eighteenth
Century to the present time, especially with reference to
the Colonies and Dependencies. Candidates will also be
allowed to take any two, but not more than two, of the
following subjects Mathematics, English, a prescribed
Author, viz.: Shakespeare’s “Richard lll.,’’Latin, French,
German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary Geology.
Applications to attend the examination must be made
on a form for the purpose, which can be obtained at
the office of the Board, and must reach the under-
signed not later than the Ist March. Candidates
will be required to pay an examination-fee of 10s.,
which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions or
the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded, to the
Secretary to the Board with the form of application. . A
list of registered candidates with centres of examination
will be published in the Government Gazette, after the
time for the receipt of applications has expired, and can-
didates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined. Candidates may be examined at
any centre (outside the Sydney radius) where persons
approved by the Board of Examiners are willing to super
intend the examination, and a form containing the signa
tures of such persons should be forwarded to the Secretar;
of the Board of Examiners not later than Ist March, 1899
Forms for the purpose may be obtained from the Secre
tary of the Board of Examiners, 7, Richmond-terrace
Domain, Sydney. The requisite stationery will be sup
plied by the Public Service Board. The Regulations and
copies of the papers set at the last examination can be
obtained at the Government Printing Office and the prin-
cipal booksellers.
By order of the Board.
9a 60 J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.
Land and Income Tax Office, Custom House,
Sydney, 20th December, 1898.
LAND AND INCOME TAX ASSESSMENT ACT
OF 1895.
NOTICE is hereby given, under section 30 of the
above Act, that Returns of Income received during
the year 1898 for the purposes of the assessment of income
Tax for the year 1899 are required to be furnished to the
Commissioners of Taxation, at their office, on or before
the 4 th day of February, 1899, and all persons who are in
receipt of income, and all persons liable under the provi-
sions of the Act in any representative capacity to furnish
returns for the purposes aforesaid, are hereby informed
that the prescribed Forms of Returns are now available,
and may be obtained (except those for Companies) on ap-
plication at any Post Office, Police Office, Custom House,
Railway Station, and the office of any Clerk of Petty Ses-
sions in New South Wales, Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-
street, Sydney, or at the Taxation Office, Custom House,
Sydney. Forms for Companies can be obtained at the
Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the
Taxation Office, Custom House, Sydney. All persons car-
rying on any business, trade or profession on their own
account are required to furnish Income Returns irrespec-
tive of the amount of their income ; but no return of in-
come is required from any person in receipt of salary or
wages only if the gross annual amount of such salary or
wages does not exceed £2OO. All employers of labour,
whether they themselves are liable to make Income Tax
Returns or not, are required to furnish to the Commis-
sioners of Taxation the name, occupation, and address of
all employees whose salaries or wages for the year 1898
aggregated £l6O and upwards. Public attention is called
to the fact that a penalty not exceeding twenty pounds
is imposed on any person neglecting to furnish the Return
require;! of him within the time abovementioned, and
that persons failing to furnish such Return are not re-
lieved from liability to the penalty by reason only that
they may not have received a notice to furnish the Re-
turn, or that the prescribed Form may not have been de-
livered to them. The attention of owners of taxable lands
subject to mortgage is invited to the necessity for furnish-
ing the requisite particulars in order that they may de-
rive the benefit of the deduction to which they are en-
titled in respect of the interest on the mortgage of their
lands, including improvements thereon. Without these
particulars the Commissioners will be unable to make the
statutory deduction from the tax on the unimproved
values. Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained
on application at any of the abovementioned offices, and
should be furnished on or before the 4 th day of February,
1899, before mention ed 1
N. LOCKYER,
R. N. JOHNSON,
L. S. SPILLER,
Commissioners
of
Taxation.

and directions with the Forn Return.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep Bp, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
