Sydney newspaper item (10th Jan.) :
Camden.— A serious charge was made against a member
of a travelling' theatrical co. on Saturday, at the police
court. The hearing occupied all the day. The Bench
dismissed the case on the ground that the girl’s evidence
was most conflicting, and could not be relied upon. Ac-
cused was discharged.
And, as is always the case in Australia, the girl
isn’t prosecuted. It is a recognised thing in this
country that any girl can lay a false charge of
criminal assault against a man with absolute im-
punity—that the perjury law is entirely abrogated
in favor of the girl-romancer who tries to get a
man hanged, or gaoled for a long term, on false
evidence. And then the administrators of the
law lament about the frequency of this kind of
perjury in Australia. Doesn’t it strike them that
any crime which always goes unpunished is apt to
be frequent?
The English syndicate which has bought
the 40-acre Woollahra House property (lately the
residence of Mr. W. Cooper) is cutting it up into
blocks for sale as building allotments : for these
sites there will undoubtedly be much competition,
and knowing people reckon that the syndicate
will come out with a profit of certainly i£30,000,
and very likely .£4o,ooo—which might just as
easily have been secured by Mr. Cooper aforesaid,,
had he been a “ sport.” The splendid house itself
(to which several acres of ground will remain
attached) will probably be run as a residential
hotel, and with its unique location, right on the
water, a few minutes from town, with the electric
tram at its back-door and a steamer-wharf at the
front, it should never have a room to spare.
Sydneyites will be edified to learn from
current Cassell’s Queen’s Empire that their water-
supply travels some 600 miles to reach them.
Lila Spring artesian bore, in the Warrego district
of Queensland, is credited as the source.
Some playful but harmless members of a
notorious Bendigo “ push,” about which various
unpleasant things had been said in a local paper,
called round one night at the house of a reporter
suspected of daring to write these things ; and,
having battered his premises, it proceeded to
gouge his eye out with a stone. The (unpaid)
bench (which sells groceries when not expounding
the law) fined the “ push ”-ite who used the stone
£7 or 14 days, with a fortnight to pay up in.
The next case was that of an infamous malefactor
named Smith who had got a handful of goods by
false representations. And the sacred rights of
property demanded a month’s gaol “without the
option.” So Smith got it.
During the month of last Sept, the New-
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
supplied the German warship Falke with 6000
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use on the
voyage to Samoa and Apia. During the vessel’s
stay in our harbor 600. gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute to the
excellence of the local lager comes from the Ger-
man warship Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart
bottles of Sydney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk Lager daily.
Both ships’ crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney Lager. As the firemen,
officers and crews come from the country that has
always been famous for lager production, it is
clear that the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
deserves all that has been said by every con-
noisseur in favor of its lager.*
Thekla Dubberke, the girl who gave the
Melbourne boot-trunk tragedy away, is a neatly-
built and pretty, but demure-looking, damsel of
20, who only wants her dark fringe hidden by a
white band to look like an ideal nun. Thekla it
was who, according to her own showing, jammed
the body of the dead girl into her boot-trunk and
cut off her hair to hinder identification. She also
alleges that afterwards she and Tod, the pious
young man who bossed the affair (he “ took a
great interest in religious matters from an early
age, and was till lately secretary of a church
good-fellowship society ”), carried the trunk con-
taining the body from a buggy over a three-rail
fence and down a steep slope to the river-bank.
Apparently the fair Thekla had no qualms of
conscience at the time, but after a lapse of
a month, during which the police acquired
a nasty habit of calling to interview her, she
became so unnerved that she had to rush off and
tell the whole game to a close friend, who happens
to be a married man. The said friend, who went
promptly to the d.’s, is now the most agitated
man in Melb., wondering whether he’ll chance the
row with his wife and go for a reward, or hold his
tongue and miss the cash. Thekla appeared
before the City Court the most calm and self-
possessed person in the room. As “ Mick ” Ward,
the detective in command, stated the evidence for
a remand she kept her big eyes fixed on him,
stood well in the sunlight where everybody could
see her, and arranged her face so that the two
dimples in her cheeks might be properly manifest.
When asked if she had any objection, Thekla
threw up her head to the bald-headed Jaypees on
the bench and made her pretty little mouth into
an O. Each toothless beak took Thekla’s glanced
admiration for his very own, and the strange part
of it is that they didn’t acquit her on the spot.
Detective Dungey, who took Thekla in tow, is a
tall, thin, dark, ascetic-looking individual with a
face consisting principally of moustaches. He
it is who is awaiting trial himself on a charge'of
conspiracy—the notorious Dora Bromley case,
where an abortion subject was readied up in order
that the vigilant officer might come out of a cup-
board and catch the operator in the act.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Clem. Hill is easily Australia’s champion
batsman this year. Clem, has made 502 runs in S.A.’s
four shield games; average, 62. The next nearest aggre-
gate batsman to Clem, is Worrall, with 264 runs in three
matches. Hill’s record, since he first played for 8.A., is
unparalleled in cricket annals, and it is marvellous to
think that he is about to go to England a second time
although not yet 22 years old. i i

K< jv, 170 runs, 42-5; M. A. Noble, 196 runs, 39’2 ;J.

28 wickets; average, 28.67 ; Noble, 11 wickets; average,

bowlers.
• » * •
Strange that C. M‘Leod, one of the Australian
Eleven certainties, picked for his batting, has only made
27 runs in 3 innings this season, although Laver, who has
made 237 runs for three times out and has top average in
shield matches, is not even considered. Another man
who should have an excellent chance is Stuckey, who has
made 230 runs for 5 innings, and is a splendid field.
• * * •
The papers of each province all loyally barrack
for their division’s candidates for the Australian Eleven.
S.M. Herald s way is to include the fixture v. Tasmania
as a first-class interprovincial match in computing the
averages. Though this places Kenny Burn at the top of
the list, still their mammoth score naturally inflates the
N.S.W. figures -Iredale’s and Trumper's more especially.
Herald’s figures give Iredale’s average 54, Trumper’s 66 ;
without Tasmanian match, they are 31 and 17. Kelly is
top of the N.S.W. averages with 42.
» * * *
For the next two months Australia must make
up its mind to get nauseated with the self-congratula-
tions of certain oracles who talk about cricket in the
“ great” journals. Every daily or bulky newspaper will
publish persistent articles by its Cricket Authority—its
“ Short Point,” “ Cover Leg,” or what not —re the selec-
tion of the Australian Eleven, and each of these awful
persons, looking down upon the public from the lofty
height of his “ expert ” eminence, will announce at fre-
quent intervals that the team is being Selected in accord-
ance with his advice of such-and-such a date. .
* • * *
Melb. Punch makes cricketer Noble a dentist,
just to ring in the joke that the Britishers will have to
look after their stumps. Noble was a clerk in one of the
banks, a position which he resigned the other day.
• * • •
Victor Trumper, on Saturday, playing for Pad-
dington against Redfern, ran up 103 in dashing style.
His failure in the Interprovincials may, perhaps, have
been owing to his over-anxiety to make a score with the
English trip in view.

Dear Bulletin.— Re Howell’s treatment in the
late interprovincial all fair judges will agree, but the
same people, while admitting Howell’s undeniable claims,
also think it would be a big mistake to leave out the
Bathurst bowler. M‘Kibbin and Howell are the two best
Australian bowlers (this in face of Trumble’s recent suc-
cesses). However, it not only seems that influence will
shut out the unassuming N.S. Welshman, but that the
same thing will be the means of finding a place for the
no-longer-brilliant Giffen.—E. M. D.
* • • *
The Sydney Cricket Ground new ladies’ tickets,
as punched, look like small targets after pea-rifle prac-
tice. It is stated that at one of the last test-matches a
very prominent member was bowled out taking in 13
ladies on his two tickets. Being a bigwig, the matter
was hushed up. The new style of ticket prevents double-
banking.
* * • *
The increasing number of serious accidents in
flat races on our racecourses calls for enquiry as to the
cause. Scarcely a Saturday but some poor devil is carted
off to hospital, but as it is the “ Sport of Kings”—and
toughs—the death of a jockey, more or less, is of small
moment. At the inquest on Geo. Durston, killed off
Bona-Fide, at Newcastle, a witness said he heard another
jockey caution Durston not to ride Bona-Fide, as the
horse was sure to fall. However, the usual verdict of
“accidentally killed” was recorded. Tuite, the other
jockey whose mount fell in the same race, still hovers
between life and death.
* • • •
- Canterbury course, Sydney, duly brought off its
little smash-up on Saturday, when Jasper and Retford
Queen fell in the Park Stakes. Crockett and Kuhn were
the jockeys, the latter being so much hurt as to necessi-
tate his removal to hospital.
• « • •
“ ‘ Mr. Jersey ’ has just made a big move with
her racing stud,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “ and this presages so much that your chances
of seeing her mumming and posing are now at any price.
In a few diffuse words : Lily, after a very unprofitable
season, has taken Aurum, Merman; Maium i and a long
string of this-siders’from Fred Webb’s train, and trans-
ferred them to that of Robinson, another ex-jockey. The
grounds for the change may be conjectured with many
conjunctions and interjections, but, thank the gods, there
is a libel-law that saves ink. Nevertheless, it is known
that, through the autumn, all the big attempted coups
went wrong, and Merman in particular, after his Cesare-
witch third, wilted so witheringly that, in the Cheveley
Cup, he seemed 'to say, ‘ Let ’em all come,’ and was
beaten by average hurdle-racers. Now the Jersey has
thrown in her lot with the heaviest gambling stable in
England. Every patron is a red hpt, shirt-and-spx-betting
goer. In five years of existence they have scooped the
boodle with shovels in the big handicaps. Their com-
missioner alone lias garnered in’ over’£l00,«00 as his little
bit. So the Lily means siroccos in ’99, and Aurum will
be given the best of opportunities.”
* * ' • * •-'
“Fossicker”: At Reef-ton (M.L.) races a horse
was driven around the course and made to win by seven
men, each with a horsewhip, and another two with kero-
sene-tins ; four horses ran in the event, and the losing
owners objected not. Thie : Woods-Williamson dramatic
co. and an Australian. drummer, who had backed the
second horse, waxed strong at the officials, and this
notice was the outcome—“No horsewhipping or other
unfair means of forcing a horse to win will be hereafter
allowed on this course.” ' At the meeting each start was
an hour late, and the last "race was run at 6.45 on both
days, each start being delayed until enough money was
put on the tote.
* * - * •
“F.C.”: Instead of employing undersized,
incompetent boys to-ride powerful and often unmanage-
able horses, why don’t racing owners train a few aborigin-
als, who could nearly always scale the weight? At Yarra
Glen (Vic.) Boxing Day meeting, out of five flat races, a
black rode four winners and only lost the odd race by a
short head.

At the recent big M.L. trots horses well-known
this side carried off their full share of prize-money.
Fritz took £lOO prize for his record mile of 2,13. -Sun-
shine won a 3-mile handicap of 130 sovs., and Kola, the
Childe Harold mare, won a 2-miles handicap of 120 sovs.
* * • •
The future of the Australian thorougbred looks
more glboiny every day. 'Mr 1 . S. Gbllan’s Tirant d’Eau,'
winner of the last N.Z. Cup, has now been bought by an
English buyer. If everything above mediocrity, male
and female, is to go to England, the race is doomed when
our present crack sires and. dams die out. It would be
hard to say how our stock has deteriorated already by
the expatriation of the mighty trio—Sir Modred, Trenton
and Carbine.
* * * *
At a recent Towers meeting a field of four went
out for the hurdles. The first and second favorites fell,
and were taken off to the stables, when it was found that
only one of the remaining horses was making for home.
The other refused to take the jump. Thereupon the
fallen two re-girthed, got out again, raced round the
track, and seeured. second and third places respectively.
Time not officially taken. Distance, two miles —about
half-an-hour.
* * . * *
The following, apparently written by a horse-
owner, is the cheekiest thing The Bulletin has met with
this year:—“l noticed in The Bulletin of last week
several paragraphs re Tattersajl’s consultations. I offer
a suggestion which, if taken up, would, I atn sure, prove
beneficial to both owners of horses and patrons of these
consultations. I hold that if Ist, 2nd, and 3rd prizes
were paid, less a standard sum fixed by ‘ Tattersall ’ for
owners of Ist, 2nd, and 3rd horses, it would save a great
deal of trouble to drawers of horses in wiring to owners
re the usual ‘ bit to nothing,’ which is now essential if
drawers do not want their horse to be scratched, besides
ensuring a straight run for their money, as all horses
would be getting the same amount of sweep-money laid
on them. Also, that the sweep be drawn on the day of
the race, which would prevent an owner of two horses in
the race from trying to get an extra piece of the sweep
about one on the plea that he would try with the other.
I think it is only fair that an owner should get a certain
amount of the sweep if his horse runs into a place, as he
has to bear all the training expenses, &c., but I do not
think that one horse should get more than another. This
suggestion, if carried out, would be the means of pro-
ducing much larger fields, for the stakes would then be
very valuable and all horses would be ridden out, as the
3rd*money would also be well worth having.”—J.C.W.
Query : If the horse-owner, because he chooses
to bear the expense of training, should get a bit of the
money of the man who gambles per medium of “ Tatter-
sall,” why shouldn’t he also get a percentage of the
money of the man who bets per medium of the book-
maker? Why shouldn’t every bet be paid less 10 per
shent for the horse-owner because he has to “ bear the
expense of training”? Rats! He chooses to go to the
expense of training because he expects to make a profit
out of it. If he doesn’t like it, let him give up training.
“ Tattersall’s ” sweeps could go on just as well if all horse-
owners gave up training, and racing died away, and the
horse became as extinct as the pterodactyl. Then they
would flourish simply as money lotteries ; in fact, they
are largely that now. The grasping horse-owner isn’t of
the least importance in the matter ; he isn’t even the fly
on the wheel, though he poses as the whole vehicle. And,
by the way, why should all this commiseration be spent
on the man whose horse has got a place ? His training
expenses are mostly paid by the stake. If any horse-
owner deserves anything because he has to pay training
expenses it is the man whose moke came in last and
didn’t get any stake.
* * • •
When Paddy Piggott, winning jockey of two
Melb. Cups, landed in Perth, “ broke,” one of the first
men he met was “Scotch Harry,” the stable-boy who
nursed Haricot the year he won the big event with
Piggott up. “ Glad to meet you,” said Harry, as he
extended his hand ;“how’s the funds?” “Broke,” was
the answer. “ Come and stay wi’ me,” said the ex-
stable-boy ; “ I’m cleaning boots for a living, and doing
well, but I can’t forget that £5OO you gave me. after
Haricot’s win.” Piggott is now driving a Perth ’bus.

“ Jimmy ” Anderson, crack Brisbane footballer,
recently presented by the Q. players with a gold watch
and £l6O, has been a votary of Rugby football since ’72,
when he was a podgy-faced little kickist in Auckland
(M.L.) College team. It is not given to many men to re-
tain the physical qualities necessary to be a front-rank
exponent of Rugby football for 27 years, “Jimmy’s”
father, Capt. Anderson, of Auckland, is one of the most
active and best-set-up men of his age in Maoriland.
• * * *
English trout are. prospering in the Monaro
(N.S.W.) waters. Mr. Jeffries, manager of Delegate station,
caught two magnificent specimens at Bibbenluke last
week—one 41b. and the other 51b. As trout are becoming
numerous in the Bibbenluke and Bombala streams, fish-
ing will soon be a favorite pastime in the neighborhood.
* * # *
The Melb. crowd would be delighted to see
Walne win the A.N.A. Wheel Race. He is the only cham-
pion left for them to swear by; and they do swear by him
in language of lurid admiration every time he gets home
an inch or two in front of his less consistent rivals. Con-
sidering that a fair amount of hero-worship is of vital
importance to bike-racing, it would surely be adyisable
for the L.V. W. to give bigger prizes in their high-sounding
classic events. An International Mile Scratch, which
Walne won at the League’s meeting last Saturday, was
worth only £lO to the boss representative of “form."
Why not run the sport on Turf 1 lines, and give the Car-
bines of the track an occasional plum?
* * * •
On Saturday the Australian won Sydney Flying
Squadron’s 18-footer championship, followed home by the
two Newcastle boats, Federal and Ariel. Geo. Holmes
started the Donnelly, but she again ran nowhere, and it
really looks as if Joe Donnelly has at last managed to
build a failure.
* * * •
An exciting mill took place on Saturday night
at the Golden Gate Club, Sydney, when Dawson and
Dunn fought the final of the middleweight novice tourna-
ment. The “ novice,” as a rule, doesn’t know enough of
the game to rig-up a “crook”; so the pair fought like
tigers till the fifth round, when Dawson sent his friend to
sleep.
* * * *
“Kid” M‘Coy, as might have been supposed,
was beaten by the hurricane-fighter, Sharkey—about the
worst kind of man the clever stripling could have bumped
up against. Sharkey has now done all that even the
exacting Fitzsimmons could with reason demand, and it
is hard to see how even Fitz can blulf him off a meeting
any longer. Sharkey is upsetting the idea that the
modern glove-fighter must of necessity be a lengthy man,
as he stands but sft. B]4in. He is a second edition of
our “ Ironbark ” Jem Burge as regards taking punish-
ment, is rising 28 years of age, and shows improvement
each time he does battle—as against Fitzsimmons’s in-
creasing years and lengthy absence from the ring. All
things considered, another meeting between Fitz and
Sharkey promises none too rosily for the Australian.
« * » *
Apropos the death of “Yellow Jimmy,” re-
corded in The Bulletin, retired champion Larry Foley
tells an amusing story. He, Jack Thompson, and Jem
Mace were visiting Newcastle on business bent, and
Jimmy paid them a social call. Before* leaving and
after having got outside sundry rums, he brandished his
unwashed left in front of England's last champion with :
“ Look here—l never yet met the man who could keep
that out of his face.” Larry’s blood was boiling, but old
Jem smiled. Larry wanted to have it out there and then
for love, but Mace restrained him, explaining afterwards
that if he (Jem) had tackled Jimmy, the half-caste might
have fluked him, “ and then where would all my great-
ness be ? ” queried the old-timer. “ Leave this nigger to;
me,” said he. Mace arranged that the Yellow-one would
have a go with Jack Thompson at the show that night.
Meanwhile Jimmy “loaded up” round town, and, of
course, proved a soft thing for the burlj and clever Jack.
Mace next.set-to with Thompson, and got all over the
big fellow, as per arrangement previously made. Then
wily old Jem posed as the man who beat the man who
made a monkey of the local Champion. “See how I
worked it, young ’un,” said Mace to Foley afterwards.
“ Take my word for it,” says Larry, “ what Jem lacked in
youth he made up in cunning.”
* * * .*
The once idol, Jonnel Sullivan, is hustling for a
living in the States with a series of pictures illustrating
incidents in his career. Jonnel now weighs 21st. of brawn
— and beer.


A young woman in a Northern town ap-
plied to have a young man bound over to keep the
peace. He was always worrying her and avowing
his love. Called so often at the house that she
lost her situation. Finally told her, “You have
only a short time to live, and not long to repent.”
But she was a practical girl ; hauled him up before
the Bench ; had him “bound over,” and then went
sweethearting with a big policeman.
Prevention of Seasickness.— The extra-
ordinary nature of the new remedy is the subject
of a letter from Mr. G. Cunningham, of Auckland,
N.Z. He writes : “ I was very pleased with the
bottie of ‘ Seajoi ’ purchased from you in August
last. I have clone a lot of travelling, but this trip
was the first I have enjoyed. On all former occa-
sions I was a martyr to seasickness.” “Seajoi”
is sol'd by Albert Sharp, 257 George-st., Sydney.
2s. 6d. and 4s. 6d. a bottle, postage sd. and Bd.
extra. *
At Quirindi (N.S.W.), the other day,
according to a statement published in the Sydney
papers, the railway officials put a lunatic into a
carriage occupied only by two ladies and two little
children, and cheerfully informed them that he
was “harmless mad.” They escaped into another
compartment, and at Muswellbrook he followed
them there. Then they appealed to have him
shifted, and were advised to call somebody’s
attention to the matter at the next station. They
were assaulted by the mild lunatic before they
reached the next station, and then the strong artn
of the law intervened and scruffed him off to
gaol on a serious charge. That, at least,
was the story told at Singleton police-court.
Now there is a regulation against lunatics riding
by train, and the ease of the railway officials who
are charged with having knowingly put a madman
into a carriage—especially along with women and
children —and the other officials who are charged
with having left him there after their attention
was called to him, wants a whole ocean of
explanation.

Is the title bestowed on “D.C.L.” Scotch (black
bottle) by many hotelkeepers. They remark that
the more brainy customers, veterans who think
twice before ordering, say—“ Mine is D.C.L.”
Mr. W. M. Rothery, of Cliefdon, near
Carcoar (N.S.W.), who died last week, aged 90
years, was a son-in-law of the old-time Major
Lockyer, and had resided at the homestead named
since Jan., 1831. He was the first N.S.W. squat-
ter to send wool direct to England, and had dealt
with the one firm of London woolbrokers for 6 r
years. Mr. Rothery was the oldest member c
the Sydney Australian Club.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “Ligmolliene,” >
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers. .
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salts," and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment >
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ’’ all the success it richly deserves,!
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAYS:—
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon, — August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Oapitas,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. 1 will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

The opening of the New Season has brought with it some important changes in last year's models, and especially so in the
11 Austral Swiftf a light machine which may be called a Road Racer, or Light Roadster, according to fancy. The history of
the “Austral Swift ”is shortly this -
Since the commencement of what we may call the “Swift Boom,” the Australian public have consistently shown some unwillingness to accept the
recognised English models without some modification, and the modifications called for have almost invariably tended in one direction. A careful study of
the question led us to the conclusion that it would be more satisfactory to instruct the makers in Coventry what alterations were necessary to meet Australian
views, and to ship direct the perfected machine which we called the “Austral Swift.”
Before going into detail, it quay be as .well to say that the chief feature aimed at in the Austral Swift ”is STYLE. ' This we insisted upon, as we have
found it waste of time to lay any model before the Australian public that did not at first glance claim to be STYLISH. The fact that the “Austral Swift ”
is the most stylish machine ever seen in Australia proves to us that the Swift Coventry Company have carried out the instructions we gave them both in
letter and spirit, and the first consignment we are now unpacking satisfies us that the whole of the order is to be up to sample. Among the leading
features of the new machine we may mention the following
A SHORT HEAD, assuring rigidity and strength.
LARGE HUBS for smoother running.
PERFECT SIMPLICITY in pedal attachments, the pedal pins being screwed into the cranks with left and right threads.
BIG GEAR WHEELS for easier propulsion.
BROOK’S SADDLES as per general desire.
PERFECT FINISH in every part of the machine.
v UV • , ,
Another feature—and an important one, too—is the price, and here again we have endeavored to meet the wishes of our Patrons. The Cash Price of
the “ AUS I RAL SWIFT ”is £2O. Terms : £2l for six months’, and £22 for twelve months’, extension.

FINNEGAN'S AUSTRAL.
’Twas an elegant day that we had, anyway,
When we met on the M.C.C. ground,
Long beers mixed with lime, at a tanner a time,
Which we drank as the riders went round.
There’s the first heat of this, and the second of that,
And the gals giving plenty of chin,
But the Austral, they say, is the race of the day,
So we wanted a tip who would win.
I goes up to Bob Lee, and he says then, says he,
“ It’s a dead bird you’re wanting, my man.
Well, Jackson is mine, but when formed into line
Keep your eye on that chap Finnegan.
“ For if Jackson don’t win, and I’ll think that a sin,
From the time (hat the pistol says go.
Finn's riding a bike which I ne’er saw the like,
And it won't give the others a show.
“ Mid's mounted on one, and the son of a gun
Will sail like the devil, so mind.
The front markers will go as they’re told, do ye know,
And the signals will come from behind.”
So we left our friend Bob and we got on the job,
With some money to back ’em to win ;
And when got on the mark, says I, “ Here’s a lark 1”
For bould Finnegan wore a wide grin.
Then the barrackers yell !• oh, for Parsons and Kellow,
Bobby Walne and for Jackson as well,
But I saw at a glattfce they hadn’t a chance
With the clang goes the bell.
You all know how 'twas done, how the two Masseys won,
Beat all-comei s from London and Paris.
Then came cheers long and loud from the throats of the
crowd
For the two Models Five Massey Harris.*

“ N.”: I have been burning to ring in this
roast chestnut. It turned up again to-day,
Dec. 31, in Melb. Advocate, slightly varied from
the old way, when the Protestant Bhi said to the
Catholic Gurrul, “ The bothom of Purgatory has
fallen out, and all the Catholics has tumbled
into Hell.” “ Agorra, the Prodestants will be
smothered! ”
Are there no French officers in that
nation’s army? One sees the names Henry
(English), Esterhazy (Austrian), Zurlinden (Ger-
man), Dreyfus (Yiddish), Panizzardi (Italian)
but where is Monsieur ?
The Union Co’s s.s. Mararoa now holds
the record for the fastest run between Sydney and
Hobart—4l hours—and is expected to still further
“cut” the time. Apropos, that fine figure of a
man, the popular “Long Bill” Featherstone, who
so long commanded the Mararoa’s predecessor, the
s.s. Oonah, is now in business for himself, having
by the favor of Tattersall Adams become owner
and master of the collier Governor Blackall, trad-
ing out of Bulli. Thousands of his old passengers
will wish him all good-luck.
“Cockaigne”-: Ikey Solomon was the
most renowned London fence. In Fraser’s
Magazine, 1841, is what purports to be a correct
record of his doings and how he became a Vande-
monian. He lived in hanging days. The authori-
ties craved to scrag him. Having got him for a
big thing, they raised such an accumulation of
charges that his friends thought it was all up.
An endeavor to escape was resolved upon, for
saving this original of Fagin. It necessitated a
bogus application for bail. The prisoner was
taken from Newgate to Westminster in a cab —the
driver bribed by Mrs. Solomon, who accom-
panied her husband on the trip, with two officers.
In an ante-room the officers were treated with
drugged brandy before leaving the court on re-
turn, the application having been contemptuously
refused. One officer succumbed in the cab, but
the other was only partially affected. Mrs. S.
was seized with a fit, and clasped him so tightly he
couldn’t move. The cab went to Solomon’s door,
where a mass of Jews crowded up. Sol bolted
out. He ran through his own house and other
premises. Ultimately he got away to America.
Mrs. Solomon, on a charge of receiving, said to
be trumped up, was transported to Van Diemen’s
Land. Solomon followed her there, was caught,
sent to London, tried, and also transported to
V.D.L.

This is the time of year when the far-see-
" lan ,' n his winter stock of Five Crowns
old Scotch whisky. In fact, the really far-seeing
man's laying in his stock of Five Crowns whisky
all the time, and he always gets this brand for
reasons too obvious to require mentioning. Five
Crowns whisky varies in price—if the man you
buy it off doesn’t happen to be looking when the
purchase is effected you may get it for nothing, or
the price may be six months ; or you may buy it
respectably for a very few shillings a bottle ;"but
either way it is always worth the price, and a
good deal over. It is one of the verv few old
institutions which have improved with age; it is
strong and rich and mellow, and the light of by-
gone centuries is reflected in the bottom of the
bottle. Also, the agents are Harrison and Att-
wood, of George-street, Sydney. Writer once
dreamt that he had discovered a horrid crime per-
petrated by Harrison, or else Attwood, when he
was young, and that he got all his Five Crowns
whisky gratis as the price of his silence -but it
was only a dream.

“C.G.T.”: Surely this is rare enough to be
worth recording. In Orange lately, one morning
noticed an elderly tom-cat coming across the
paddock with a 3-weeks-old kitten in his mouth.
Investigated, and found that Thomas had stolen
kitten at a farm, half-a mile away, during its
mother’s absence. The way he looked after that
kitten was a caution ; keeping it in tucker and not
allowing it to stray a yard from the house before
he would scruff it and take it back to his box
again. The kitten seemed quite contented with
the arrangement too.
An English scandal-item from The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent :
A repetition of the famous Osborne jewel-robbery has
reached the surface of publicity though 15 months since
the deed was done. Osborne case was about eight years
ago. Party at a country house. Lady’s jewels disap-
peared. No trace. Later, it was said, the hostess accused
Mrs. Osborne, wife of military captain.' Action for slan-
der, and, result, exposure of plaintiff, who got six months
for the theft in a criminal prosecution. As all the parties
were very “ swagger,” public excitement was great. In
to-day’s incident, £6OO worth of jewellery was lost by an
old lady at, a country house near Coventry, residence
of a local potentate and J.P. A certain lady saw
two men escaping. No trace of tracks in garden,
nor were they ever heard of again. The 55-year-old loser,
taken suddenly ill from shock, speeds home, never re-
covers and dies in October last. Husband takes up de-
layed enquiries. The evidence looks remarkably strong.
One of the servants, resenting suspicion thrown on her-
self, searches box of lady who had professed to see the
burglars, and finds part of the missing jewellery. Owner
of the box, however, asserts that the articles were
“ planted ” therein, and, altogether, it’s a very pretty
scandal.

A German hotel-keeper in an Out Back
town wailed thusly by word of mouth to a Bul-
letin correspondent:
Tt vas von of them dashed zhentlemen vith the bag—
von bagman. I saw him kiss mine frau. (Some of pub-
lican's language is here omitted as too shocking for
publication.) I say nottings to ze bag-hog, only I put in
his heel “To kissing my vife, von bound.” He pay ze
bound quite right and he laugh. (Some solid cursing left
out.) He drive past this vay again and he bring me a
message that mine brother vant to see me at vonce. Mine
brother he geep der bublic-house twenty miles back. He
offer to drive me there if I sdart at vonce as he got no
time to sdop. We sdart so much at vonce that I haf no
dime to put on mine boots and go in slippers. (Ten yards
of deep objurgation left out). Ten miles out he ask me
to get down and open ze gate and den he vallop ze horse
like some hells und drive away. I valk home ten mile in
mine slippers over hot rocks when ze little white thing
that stands straight oop in ze glass that hang on ze wall
is at 108. Next time he come I scharge zat bag-hog
another bound.”

NOXJE—Our ONLY Australian Address—QQ KING STREET, SYDNEY.
A little show which lately quitted Sydney for Maoriland
left one pleasing memory behind it. It was playing a
wailsome drama : scene laid in Sydney ; and the hero’s
wife and children were starving in an absolutely empty
room on a cold night. The galvanised iron wind blew
with a shrill note outside the house and shrieked through
the broken window. Then the hero rose up, and declared
that they should not per-r-risfi thus, and rushed forth.
He came back in two minutes with a liv-e sheep which he
had stolen as it grazed on the grassy wilds of Castlereagh-
street, Sydney, and cast it into the room saying, triumph-
antly : “They are saved ! -Here is food.” The im-
poverished family hadn’t even a knife to kill the animal
with, or a lire to cook it, or a dish of any kind, so pre-
sumably they killed it by skull-fracture against the wall
and core it up raw. But the curtain fell just then.
A trance artist put a man asleep once at an unknown
date and in a place which the present writer can’t remem-
ber. The man was to remain asleep nine days or a month,
or something like that, and did it to the satisfaction of
the general public. But the following performance was
incidental to his long doze : The police-sergeant on the
beat pushed the stage-door open one night to come in for
his usual drink and smoke and yarn, and, behold, the
sleeper was out of his case sitting smoking, and the
attendant who was retained to fan the flies off him during
the night-watch had gone for beer. The attendant swore
the sergeant to eternal secresy over the beer. He swore,
and waited to see the waker sleep again in anticipation
of a visit from one of the Vigilance Committee. The
beer renewed nightly kept the sergeant’s mouth shut till
the show left. Then for beer he opened it again and told
this yarn. And if any one wants the yarn retracted it
can be done for more beer.
There used to be on the M.L. West Coast a Milesian
publican, whose house was much frequented by travelling
showmen. “So yez are back agin,” he would say to
them. “ Well, I think yez ’ull do well this time —av yez
git good houses.”
“ H.” : A book's a book. I take up the queerest volume
ever issued from the Australian Press, “ Tragedies and
Comedies,” by Richard Capper. I saw his son the other
day, .pensioned civil servant, late respected caretaker of
Melbourne District Court. Capper, the elder, was stage
carpenter at Drury-lane in the days of Lord Byron and
Edmund Kean, both of whom he knew. He caine to
Melbourne when it was only a few years old, and became
actor and mechanist at the Queen’s under Coppin.
Then he retired to a sequestered farm, out Plenty
River way, about 20 miles from Melbourne. Here he
diligently wrote plays, to the number of about 29.‘ Most
were in five acts. A few condescended to three. The
volume published contains half-a-dozen, rigorously Shake-
spearian, in five acts, digging up extraordinary themes
from the histories of Egypt, Babylon, Nineveh, and such
places. For instance, there is “The Mummy-makers of
Egypt,” a comedy wherein Mr. and Mrs. Sanchoniatho
are most humorous. Dick lived to about ninety. When
long past eighty he acted a bit of “Shylock” in character
at Melbourne Royal for a benefit, also Dr. Flail in the
“Irish Tutor.’’
Harry Cardello, the spry “black” boy rider of Fitz-
geralds’ Circus, has been with the show since he was a
nipper. Years ago his father wrote from some outland-
ish spot in North Queensland to contradict a Bulletin
statement that the boy was a pure-bred aboriginal. Then
the boy looked almost as black as King Billy, but now he
is merely brown and curly, and a good-looking Harry at
that.
Walter Baker, Bland Holt’s spotless and indomitable
hero, although Australian bred, is a devout observer of
musty stage traditions, and his most marked mannerism
is his habit of religiously lifting his hat before kissing
the heroine, and before uttering the usual platitude that
always evokes the thunder of the gods. In the former
case the reverent lifting of the glossy topper has all the
suggestiveness of a fat saint saying grace before buns at
a methody tea-feast. Why ?—-oh ! why ?
An admirable old actor in Edwin Kelly died at Ade-
laide Hospital, on New Year’s Day, from the effects of
the hot weather. During his Australian career Kelly
played a wide range of parts, making his biggest success
as Gaspard the Miser, in “ Cloches de Corneville." This
was at Melbourne Opera House more than a dozen years
ago. A polished, gentlemanly “ heavy father,” he was
equally effective in his quiet, humorous way as a light
comedian until his limbs and memory grew infirm.
Kelly’s last notable performance was his Duke of Argyle
in a revival of “ Jessie Deans ” at Melbourne Royal. The
scene between Eloise Juno and the old man couldn’t have
been better acted on either side. Something of Kelly’s
incisive method has been acquired by his son, Edwin
Lester, who comes out strong on the rare occasions when
he gets a congenial character.
Sweethearts.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It’s gettin’ bits o’ posies
An’ feelin’ real good,
An’ thinkin’ how she loves you,
An’ wond’rin’ why she should.
An’ stoppin’ sorter sudden,
Because you’re full o’ thought,
An’ full o’ rec’less feelin’s—
That makes life feel too short.
It’s feelin’s if she’d loved you
Afore the world was made,
An’ that she’s goin’ to love you
When all our debts is paid—
As if there’s nothin’ mattered,
As if all life was good,
As if the Lord was lookin’
And sorter understood.
It’s feeling true an’ frien’ly
To every thing that’s weak,
An’ doin’ little actions
That someway seem to speak—
Such things as wimmen reckons
Is nat’ral to occur
When he’s in love with Someone,
An’ that Someone is her.
Paraguay.
M. J. C.
Dolly.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When an English King knighted the Sir Loin of
British Beef he was probably ignorant of the
august precedent of a Roman ruler, who intended
to raise a British oyster to consular rank, but, on
reflection, decided that the honor was an inade-
quate one. To enjoy a plate of oysters is a proof
of culture; to enjoy a barrel, a proof of complete
culture.
With the advent of the manufactured article,
an awful thing of slimy loathsomeness, surely
good taste would pass away, and there be left no
desire for the training of the palate for the mer-
maid embrace of this delicate softness in armor.
So I mused as, one by one, the Sydney Rocks
were shrined in an appreciative grave.
“ Good Lord ! Dolly ! ”
The words shaped themselves in the throat,
clattered up against the roof of the mouth, and
almost found utterance. But the sight of what
came after drove them back to the limbo of things
unubtered. It was certainly Dolly; Dolly looking
if anything more rounded and voluptuous than an
odd dozen or so months before. But he that fol-
lowed was as surely her husband, a husband still
under the glamor of a honeymoon preserved by
passion or art beyond its decent and proper limit
of 30 days. And—Great Heavens! would the
procession never end ?—there came filing in the
shop a third, a handmaiden of Venus Matrix,
bearing in her arms the baby of Dolly and the
obvious pride of Dolly’s husband. Confounded, I
watched the entrance of the nurse-girl, half ex-
pecting, half fearing the doorway to be darkened
by yet another.
“Will the line stretch out to the crack of
doom ? ”
A dreadful vision of a matronly Dolly, followed
by scores of maids, each burdened by an infant
that wagged a tiny finger of reproach, oppressed
me a moment. Then I saw that the procession
had ended. There was but one baby and one hus-
band. Still, the idea of Dolly with a husband and
a baby—and only 18 months since. It was sur-
prising, even alarming.
The husband seemed swelling with fatuous
pride, resembling fantastically the Sydney Rock
on my plate, which puffingly pushed out its ample
cream-colored waist from a flat shell, as though
the kindness of the Oyster Fates were favoring its
destiny. He was fair and beefy—would have
served as a model for Moore’s Dick Lennox—and
the proprietary air with which he viewed his wife
and his baby was as obvious as his necktie. It, a
royal-purple shot with brilliant green, wailed
frightfully or shrieked exultantly as it caught the
light obliquely or directly. She came to me with
outstretched hand and a smile, which showed'two
rows of fine, even teeth, specked in three places
with gold. (The history of those specks I could
have told with absolute devotion to detail. I can-
not recall whether I still possess the invoice.)
There was not a suggestion of diffidence in her
face. Her eyes did not plead : they boldly com-
manded.
“This, Dick (of course, his name was Dick.
Men who look like that always are Dick !), is Mr.
Brice, an old friend of mine. Mr. Brice, my hus-
band, Mr. Moxman.”
I felt confused, ashamed, afraid to take the
hand of the honest fellow in front of me. But
Dolly was imperturbable. I had to accept the
proffered hand and submit mine to its warm
grasp ; to refuse would have seemed strange, even
suspicious, to Mr. Moxman. Though, on reflection,
I could no more think of suspicion as possible in
his mind than a pearl under the vest of the fine
oyster, the last of its dozen, which still pushed out
its stomach as a suggestion to mine, losing, I
thought, a little of the first sleek look of content
as I continued to delay the devouring of it.
They stayed half-an-hour or so. Moxman told
me, admiringly, how. his wife doated on oysters—
“ not a common taste in women. ’’ It seemed to
him distinctly genteel. I was tempted to tell him
in return how the oyster was the touchstone of
character, by which the shop-girl and the theatre-
girl are judged ; a taste for the bivalve was signifi-
cant of much. But ivhy should I play the Serpent
in a Fool’s Paradise ? The baby, of course, had
to be admired, and its striking resemblance to
Dadda noted. When the maid brought the little
germ of a sinner up for admiration, Moxman sug-
gested that perhaps “the girl” would like some
prawns. Mrs. Moxman frowned the,idea down.
At last they went, he gloating over his -wife with
greedy eyes, the nurse-maid following with the
defiant humility of an Australian servant. '
Now at Deluigi’s you get the finest oysters in
all the city of Sydney ; the only Tasmanian trum-
peter that can make an Englishman forget the
turbot ; and schnapper of sentimental plumpness.
But I was forced to resolve upon an abandonment
of this home of salt delights. I could not risk
meeting Dolly again. To sacrifice Deluigi’s was
to lose something of life. But, after all, one must,
sooner or later, pay for one’s pleasures. After
eighteen months the price of that rosy fortnight
at Lake Macquarie had to be paid.
Saddened by the thought of this sacrifice on the
altar of a dead pleasure, I thought bitterly of
Dolly, her husband, and the baby, going home. I
could see her as she swam proudly by his side, and
hear her as she asked him if he had noticed how
confused and red I had been. “ Poor Mr. Brice.
He was an old admirer of mine. For twelve
months he teased me something dreadful to marry
him. He is not a bad-looking fellow, though, of
course, nothing like you, Dick.” (This with an
admiring look aloft.) “He has, too, no end of
money, hut, you know, Dick,” (with a squeeze of
the arm to which she is holding), “I always had
an ideal, and until I met you ” ——
When they reached home, I could imagine the
hot and flustered nursemaid asking to be allowed
to go out that night, and being refused by the in-
dignant little matron, who tells her how wrong it
is .for young women to go out unaccompanied, and
hints darkly as to what might happen to girls
guilty of such wrong.
A woman is never ashamed, but when neces-
sary she can appear to be.
RENAR.
ON THE POINT.
Hallo, here’s Chester. Watch him strut past me ;
Me that gave him his start in the biz !
Now while he heads the bill luck has outcast me.
■ I’m wantinghalf-a-pint ; he can call fizz 1
He was so stage-struck I gave him utilities.
How did he study his first half-a-side !
Now the Firm stars his barnstorming abilities—
Thanks to a woman and thanks to his hide.
I took him, an awkward burlesque of humanity,
Taught him to walk and talk, put in his brains;
Thinks they’re his own. Poor fool! in his vanity
How can he help it when his are the gains ?
He plays his love-scenes in a tailor-built attitude,
The girl in his arms while the gods have his eyes ;
And his tones are a parson’s propounding a platitude,
Contradicting their passion and damning them—lies.
Yet how they stand him and swallow it greedily,
Thinking it’s acting they’re crowding to see ;
Wouldn’t their fathers have cat-called him speedily,
Him—when they’d Sullivan, Creswick or—me 1
He copies my readings —the mere intonations—
My original points and the cut of my vest.
Mine are the talents that win his orations;
It’s me they’re applauding wherever he’s best I
What, Chester, my boy, well met; I was praising
Your reading of Melnotte to Rantaway here !
By Gad I enjoyed it ; you’ve come on amazing. ...
Don’t care if I do, though it’s early for beer.
So you’re fixed for two years at an in-and-out salary.
Cut away Londonwards, that’s my advice ;
They’re yours by the wool here, stalls, circle and gallery ;
Get the hall-mark and you’ll double your price.
Oh, I’m in suburbs ; you know the tread-mill it is;
_ You might see J.C. for me—oh, I ain’t proud,
I’ll take what is offering—small stuff—utilities—
Carry a banner—come on in the crowd 1
THE STROLLER.


Out of 100 13-15 year old boys, intended
forEuglish public schools the examining physician
found 34 below average height, 53 below average
weight, (53 below average chest-girth, 63 more or
less deformed, 14 with varicocele, and 24 with
albuminuria. The sinking generation !
Mayor M ‘Enchant was interviewed in
Colombo, and in the course of his valuable obser-
vations he condemned the sanitation of tliecapital
of Ceylon. “ Its nightsoil system,” he said, “ was
an abomination.” And this, mind you. from the
mayor of Smellbourne !
Item from a 'Ome paper: “Charles So-
and-So, said to be the son of a Q.C., who was
charged last week at West London with stealing
an umbrella, was discharged on Saturday, his
father undertaking to send him to Melbourne
immediately.” Charles will be the son of a Lord
Chancellor, probably, by the time he confers his
patronage on a Smellbourne hash-house.
“ Constance Clyde ” : Re alleged connec-
tion between genius and disease. Present scribe
thinks writing a remarkably healthy employment;
never takes an anti-bilious pill under any circum-
stances ; writes a love-poem instead—finds it does
just as well ; entertains a vague impression that
every branch of literature will some day be found
good for some special ailment, and that literature
was created to that end ; doesn’t find holidays
brain-destroying ; notices the more she writes the
more she wants to climb a mountain, the more she
climbs mountains the more she wants to write.
Grants that if she got ill, might possibly write
better, but doubts it. Was seasick once, and
didn’t find it so.
The Funafuti expedition from Sydney,
sent to test the truth of Darwin’s theory that
coral islets are formed by coral insects—which
cannot live far below the water-surface —upbuild-
ing to keep pace with the subsidence of a slowly-
sinking sea-bed, has done valuable work without
settling the question, though further information
may he expected when a microscopic examination
of the borings has been made. The truth at pre-
sent appears to lie midway between Darwin’s idea
and that of his chief opponent—Dr. Murray, of
the “Challenger” expedition—who thinks that
coral reefs are built on natural elevations in the
sea-bottom, and may be accounted for without
postulating subsidence. That is, as Dr. Mis-
sionary Brown points out in Sydney Telegraph,
there has probably been upheaval as well as sub-
sidence, and in some cases a stationary bottom.

Mr. Handley.
MR- WILLIAM HANDLEY, who resides at the lear of 94 H arris-street, Pyrmont, Sydney, N.S. W., suffered tortures from indigestion
for nearly 7 years. He has very kindly given a press reporter the particulars of his cure by Dr. Williams ’ Dink Pills.
“ I first noticed the pains about 7 years ago,” said Mr. Handley. “lam a green-grocer, and am out in all weathers, and used to
have very little time for my meals. The complaint came on gradually, and at first I didn’t let it worry me ; but I was very soon awakened.
I got worse and worse, in spite of various remedies which I tried. After eating I would feel a most terrible pain in the lower part
of my chest, as if some hundredweight of lead were there, and, though often hungry, I would refrain from eating, because I knew the result. I
became very thin, and felt as though life were hardly worth living. Meat, of course, I had almost to refrain from eating. Any excitement
brought on the pain, and I was at last unable to climb a hill, so great was my exhaustion and want of breath. Even a piece of bread would
disagree with me. I consulted three doctors, who told me I was suffering from indigestion and dyspepsia. I then tried the hospitals and
was six weeks in one institution, being laid up on that occasion for three months altogether. This bad attack occurred about two years a-o
but I’ve had several since. I had the most unpleasant taste in my mouth on rising, just as if I had been sucking some copper article. My
skin and my chest-bone became so tender that I could hardly bear the touch of my clothes. HYell, my wife, who has been ill for years is takin"
a course of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and she had even then derived the greatest benefit from them.” Here Mrs Handley nodded’ a smilirm
assent. “My wife thought the Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills might do me good, so I gave them a trial. The first few doses drove away the pains
in my head, and I also found, to my surprise and pleasure, that my indigestion was slowly disappearing. That was about six months ago :
and I have never gone back since. As time went on, my taste for food returned, and now I can eat with comfort. I can honestly say my
indigestion is entirely a thing of the past, and its disappearance is entirely owing to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. That terrible weight
on my chest and the unpleasant taste left me, and also the retching that accompanied my complaint. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are
undoubtedly the remedy of the age for dyspepsia, indigestion and like complaints.”
The real Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure anmmia, general debility, all headaches, diarrhoea, biliousness, liver and kidney troubles, indigestion, sleeplessness
vomiting, all ladies’ ailments, neuralgia, nervous prostration, fevers, gravel, St. Vitus’ dance, paralysis, locomotor ataxia, etc. All chemists and storekeepers sell them or
you can send direct to the Dr. Williams Medicine Co., Sydney, enclosing the price, 2s. 9d. per box, or 15s. 3d. for six boxes (post free), in postal note or stamps

"Which His Weakness is
Women”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first I met Dolores,
I swore—a ’prentice kid—
’Er Spanish eyes was glories j
Gord ’elp me ! So I did,
But that was Valparaiso,
Before I got to know—
Yes, that was Valparaiso,
An’ very long ago.
Which ’is weakness is women;
Oh, let us confess,
It might 'ave been greater,
’Twould 'ardly be less.
Two sins what ’e’ll boast of
In ’Ell, we opine,
Two sins what 'e’ll roast of —■
That’s women—an’ wine !
She sipped aguardiente,
An’ she was hot as flame ;
I loved ’er good an’ plenty—
She swore she did the same;
She vowed in West-Coast lingo,
“ For dios ! luf I you."
An’ left me for a gringo
With pesos for to blue.
Since which I’ve crossed the waters,
To spend my cash an’ leave
A courtin’ of the daughters
Of good ole Mother Eve ;
Since which I’ve fooled with women,
With women white an’ brown,
With Dagoes an’ she-devils
From ’Amburg to Cape-Town.
I might have saved my money—
Gord knows ’ow ’ard ’twas won—
But this is certain, sonny,
A chap will ’ave 'is fun ;
There’s not a rover rovin’
There’s not a sailorman,
Who doesn’t fall to lovin’
As frequent as ’e can.
If I could ’arp like David,
As always ’arped in tune,
My chanty it would echo
From Rio to Rangoon,
An’ places intervenin’
Some answer it might find,
For every port I’ve been in
I’ve left my love be’ind.
Which ’is weakness is women —
Oh, let us confess
It might ’ave been greater,
’Twould 'ardly be less;
For out of each ’undred
There's ninety an’ nine
Is damned an’ condemned for
Said women — an’ wine!
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
A Sin that Saved.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The revelation came suddenly, without warning;
the man, strong as he was, trembled to falling.
The Antinio Family gave the last turn in the
show; and in the little tent were taking off the
outer dress from the acrobat eostu ae they wore
beneath. “Antinio” was professional; in private
life the elder man was Jacobs, the girl his wife,
and the partner Gosper.
This Gosper had a dubious record : Jacobs had
taken him from the gaol-door that had opened to
him a world without a welcome. Jacobs meant to
make a man of him: taught him his profession,
and, with the simplicity of strong men, never
doubted. The elder acrobat had concentrated all
the energy of his life in his muscles—perhaps that
explained his mental sluggishness and ineptitude.
So far, his character had slumbered in his machine-
like body, dwarfed to its first childishness. A
minute passed, and he had left his comrades to go
to the balloon, but, missing the trapeze-strap,
turned back. His hand was on the tent-fly, his
head bent to push through the hanging canvas,
when the sound of words spoken in his wife’s
voice struck him like a bolt.
“ Kiss me, darling ; the old Bear’s gone ! ”
As one overcome with much wine Jacobs reeled
backwards; then, witli unsteady steps, stumbled
across the show-ground. Life is measured by inci-
dent. That walk, but a few hundred paces,
seemed miles and miles to the man, so fast did his
mind travel; he saw his past as in some unrolled
picture; his toil and struggle; how he had pitied
this abandoned girl and made an honest wife of
her; how, later, he had pitied the man and given
him a chance, and this was his reward. His
dumb animal affeotion for the woman was out-
raged, and all his preconceived ideas of cause and
effect overthrown. “To sow kindness and reap—
and reap—” instinctively his hand went to his
brow to brush away what his eyes pictured. As
he neared the balloon his gait steadied, his step
acquiring a new alertness as his eyes sparkled with
fresh-kindled light. He pushed through the
crowd, vaulted the enclosure, and saw to the fas-
tenings and guide-ropes with more than usual
care. All was ready when his companions ap-
peared.
* * * •
They stood in the car, bowing to the multitude
underneath ; a shout from a thousand throats fol-
lowed the ascending balloon. To the aeronauts,
the earth sank beneath them, the noises grew
suddenly fainter—the ‘ ping, ’ ‘ ping ’of a shooting-
fallery, and the yelping cry of a dog became the
ominant sounds. Quickly they descended from
the car.
According to the programme the elder Antinio
was to hold a trapeze suspended from his mouth
while hanging head-down wards by his feet from
the balloon, his partners performing the usual
feats upon the bar underneath. In reality the
weight of the trapeze was borne by a concealed
strap ; the whole performance, to those accustomed
to aeronautics, was far more dangerous in appear-
ance than in reality.
A fresh shout from below, rising soft as an echo,
as the three midgets were seen to hang suspended.
It was to be a brief performance, swiftly executed.
• * • *
With a laugh on her lips the woman climbed the
body of her companion, and paused for a moment
to sit with him on the bar: again she was mount-
ing to the car when her husband spoke : ‘ 1 Wait,
Lena ! I want to talk to you. ”
It was on her tongue to respond sharply. But,
seeing his face, the words choked back, and with
sudden fear she gripped tightly at the rope.
Jacobs was smiling a blighting, bitter smile, and
his companions perceived with terror that the
trapeze, no longer held by the strap, was sustained
by his hands alone.
“The old Bear holds you now, Lena. Kiss
your darling.”
The man and woman crouched away from each
other on the bar as though the words seared, she
moaning dismally without a tear or a word.
4 Gosper tried to put courage in his white face:
“Don’t be hard on me, Jacobs; it was all her
doing. I’d never have thought of such a thing,
so help me God !
“You beast! ” hissed the woman, interrupting.
“My doing, you cur! How dare you!” Then,
turning to her husband, coaxingly: “I always
loved you, Pete: you know I do. I was mad,
that thing tempted me, fooled me; but, truly, I
loathe him now. See how I loathe him, ” and the
woman motioned to spit with her dry mouth on
the man by her side. ‘ ‘ Spare me ; you will spare
me, won’t you, Pete ? ”
Jacobs, his face suffused with purple blood, his
eyes staring, smiled down below, a smile palpable,
withering, triumphant.
The balloon had entered a cloud, and hung in
the mist hidden from earth and sky; only the
round red sun looked up at them with lurid gleam.
Quite plainly they could hear a child’s voice call-
ing coup ! coup! coup! to lingering cows, the
tinkle of a school-bell and the shriek of an engine-
whistle as a train crossed aviaduct. And then in
that solitude, as the two sat there shivering for
the meroy that did not come, a strange sight came
to sicken their souls. In the mist, close to them,
a second balloon took gradual shape, growing into
substance as they gazed, with a car like theirs and
three shades swinging below it like themselves.
As they moved, so these creatures moved, yet
with something added that was diabolic and
frightful. The lovers, that had been, saw it not
as a mirage, but as a wraith of their dead selves
doomed to float thus to eternity, ever to fall and
never falling.
“Oh, let me up; let me up!” the woman
moaned,, while Gosper drew himself together with
evident intention of making a spring at his partner
above.
“No, you don’t,” said Jacobs, shaking the
trapeze menacingly. “ Kiss, my darlings—and be
quick ! ”
They approached each other like contaminated
things, the lips meeting without sound.
“ Closer, closer; put your loving arms about
each other! ”
Each passed a nerveless aim to the other’s body,
their dry lips meeting again in mockery.
“ Now, kiss and be damned ! ”
Like a dropped stone the trapeze parted from
the giant’s hands ; a wail as of the wind, and the
balloon, relieved of weight, shot high into space.
* • • *
At the inquest the jury saw fit to add as rider
to its verdict:—“ The authorities should take
measures to prohibit such dangerous performances
in future.”
Jacobs is how the owner of a mammoth circus ;
he is counted the smartest, shrewdest, most suc-
cessful man on the road ; hard as nails, yet jolly
as a sandboy. The stolid acrobat, Antinio, is
dead.

The Message of the Spear.
Awav bank in the year 54 li.o. —1948 years ago—
a little detatelnuent of Roman soldiers was
besoiged by. a host of Gauls at Cbaleroi, in the
north of what is now called France. Believing
that no help could possibly reach them, the
Romans expected to light until they were all
striken down. One day a short spear came
whizzing over the ramparts and stuck in one of
the wooden towers. Nobody paid any attention
to it at first. They just went on slaughtering
Gauls and getting slaughtered themselves. At
length an officer saw something fluttering from
that spear. It proved to be a note from Julius
Cißsar—written in Greek. He said he was on his
way with an army to relieve the garrison. That
note had been there unheeded for several days.
In due time Caesar came and made short work of
the Gauls.
That people should suffer and struggle hope-
lessly when help is near at hand is sad to think of,
but they must knovj of the coming help before they
can draw courage from it. Take the torments and
dangers of disease for example. We fight them
with all the weapons we have. Sometimes we hit
on the right thing, and more often we don’t, even
when it is as close as the spear in the tower was to
the exhausted parrison.
If Mr. Robert Lavis, postmaster at-High Ham,
Langport, Somerset, could have found a remedy
for indigestion, dyspepsia and liver complaint, he
certainly would not have suffered from it, as he
did, for twepty years. Why, that is more than
half an average lifetime. A single day of illness
is always long enough, goodness knows. But
fancy such an experience stretching itself into
weeks, months, and years ! Common enough ?
Yes, dreadfully common. So is poverty ; but
does that fact reconcile anybody to either ? Mr.
Lavis is not a man to sit tamely down and brood
oyer a misfortune. Besides being postmaster he
is a grocer, with work enough to keep him busy.
Under what difficulties this work was done he
tells us in a letter dated Nov. 8, 1893.
“ I had a bad taste in the mouth,” he says,
“ particularly in the morning, and my mouth was
dry, and I spat up thick, tenacious phlegm. After
eating even the simplest food I had great pain in
the chest and around the heart. I suffered greatly
from sick headache and giddiness, and if I exer-
cised much I got out of breath.”
[This was asthma, caused by the stupefying
action of foul blood upon the nerves that move the
lungs; the impurities in the blood having com©
from the festering mass of undigested food in the
stomach. The heart trouble, the sick headache,
and the giddiness were symptoms of the same
thing.]
“As time went on,” continues Mr. Lavis, “I
became very low, weak, and mentally discouraged
and depressed.”
[A doctor, writing about this case for a learned
medical journal, would use many tough Latin
words, but he would not make the facts as plain
as Mr. Lavis himself has made them. Naturally
a man who can eat but little—which little mostly
rots in his stomach—will lose flesh and strength
and come to be of small use to himself or to any-
one else We can all see that even by candle-
light.]
n l* tried many medicines,” he says finally,
“ but they did me no real good. At last I heard
of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and used it. I
am happy to tell you that it soon relieved and
cured me. Whenever I feel a sign of my old
ment I resort to the Syrup at once, and the disease
gets no further hold upon me. Publish my state-
ment if you like, and believe me yours, &e.,
(Signed) Robert La vis.”
The Roman soldiers left Caesar’s message unre-
garded for days. It was written on parchment and
fluttered from a spear, as I have said. But, see!
We live in the age of print. Are you ill and in
pain as Mr. Lavis was? Every newspaper, every
magazine, almost every publication your eye falls
on, contains accounts of what Mother Seigel’s
Syrup has done, and daily does, fox- those who
suffer. Read the messages. This is one; and,
having read it, you know where to look for help.
